The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Definition of the Proof-Syllogism (Qiyas al-Dalil)
Author defines proof-syllogism and describes its premises and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key components of a proof-syllogism and distinguish it from a sign-syllogism.

Core Idea
The proof-syllogism relies on acknowledged premises, not physical signs.

In this passage, the author defines a specific type of categorical syllogism called the proof-syllogism (قياس الدليل). Unlike a sign that points to something else (like smoke to fire), the proof-syllogism works by combining two premises that are publicly accepted, leading to a conclusion through acknowledgment. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic dialectics and rhetoric.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic passage. The author starts with 'أما الدليل فهو...' indicating that 'الدليل' (al-dalil, proof) is the term under definition.
2
Extract the genus
The author places 'الدليل' in the category of a syllogism (قياس). Specifically, it is 'قياس مؤلف من مقدمتين' (a syllogism composed of two premises). This is the genus.
The genus is 'syllogism' with two premises, which is a categorical syllogism.
3
Identify the differentia
The differentia is that its major premise is 'مقدمة محمودة' (a praised premise) accepted by the public (الجمهور). Additionally, it is explicitly distinguished from sign-syllogism: 'لا على سبيل أن جزءا منه يدل على جزء آخر' (not in the way that a part indicates another part).
The differentia includes both the praised nature of the major premise and the absence of a sign relation.
4
Contrast with sign-syllogism
The author contrasts proof-syllogism with the sign-syllogism (قياس العلامة). In a sign, part indicates another part (e.g., smoke indicates fire). In proof, the combination of both premises is itself acknowledged as valid evidence.
5
Examine the example
The example given is: 'The envious are hated, and the benefactors are loved.' These are two premises that serve as proofs, either separately or combined, forming a major premise potentially or actually. The minor premises would be individual cases like 'So-and-so is envious' or 'So-and-so is a benefactor.'
The example illustrates how two universal statements act as major premises for individual cases, based on prevailing opinion (ظن غالب).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في [قياس] الدليل [وقياس] العلامة
أما الدليل فهو قياس مؤلف من مقدمتين كبراه مقدمة محمودة؛ يراها الجمهور ويقول بها؛ وتؤخذ حجة ودليلا لا على سبيل أن جزءا منه يدل على جزء آخر مثل الدخان على النار بل على أن نفس القول الحاصل من الجزأين معترف به. وربما كان على أمر مستقبل، وربما كان على أمر حاضر، وربما كان عاما، وربما كان على الأكثر مثل قولهم إن الحساد ممقوتون، والمنعمون مودودون، فإنها بين المقدمتين دليلان أو يتحد منهما الدليل على أنها تجعل كبرى بالقوة أو بالفعل؛ وليس الغرض أن نفس المقت أو الود علامة ودليل على أن هذا بنفسه دليل أي متبع مقبول مرجوع البتة: فإن الدليل في هذا الموضع يراد به هذا وهذه المقدمات تؤخذ في القياس من الكبريات وصغرياتها شخصيات كقولهم إن فلانا حسود وإن فلانا منعم، وحمل هذه المقدمات بحسب الظن الغالب. والكبريات إذا كانت بالقوة كان القياس نوعا من قياس الضمير؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
Chapter: On the syllogism of proof and the syllogism of sign.
As for the proof, it is a syllogism composed of two premises, its major premise being a praised premise; the public sees it and affirms it; and it is taken as evidence and proof, not in the way that a part of it indicates another part like smoke indicates fire, but rather because the very statement resulting from the two parts is acknowledged. It may be about a future matter, it may be about a present matter, it may be general, and it may be for the most part, like their saying: 'The envious are hated, and the benefactors are loved.' For between the two premises are two proofs, or from them the proof unifies, that they make a major premise potentially or actually. The purpose is not that hatred or love itself is a sign and proof that this itself is a proof, i.e., followed, accepted, absolutely resorted to. For proof in this place is intended to mean this, and these premises are taken in the syllogism from major premises and their minor premises as individuals, like their saying: 'So-and-so is envious' and 'So-and-so is a benefactor,' and the application of these premises is according to prevailing opinion. If the major premises are potential, then the syllogism is a type of syllogism of the conscience; that will be known later.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage defines the proof-syllogism (قياس الدليل) and explains its structure. The author begins by stating that a proof-syllogism consists of two premises, with the major premise being a 'praised' (محمودة) premise—one that is accepted by the public and commonly affirmed. This premise is used as evidence, but not in the way that a physical sign like smoke indicates fire; rather, the combined statement of the two premises is itself acknowledged as true. The proof can relate to future, present, general, or most common matters. An example is given: 'The envious are hated, and the benefactors are loved'—these are two proofs that together form a major premise either potentially or actually. The author clarifies that the emotion itself (hatred or love) is not the sign; rather, the proof is the combined inference. The premises are taken as universals (major premises) and particular instances (minor premises), like saying 'So-and-so is envious' and 'So-and-so is a benefactor.' These are applied according to prevailing opinion (ظن غالب). If the major premises are only potential, the syllogism becomes a type of 'syllogism of the conscience' (قياس الضمير), which will be explained later.

Key Terms
الدليل
المقدمة المحمودة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What characterizes the major premise in a proof-syllogism (قياس الدليل)?
A) It is a necessary and certain premise derived from first principles.
B) It is a praised premise accepted by the public (جمهور).
C) It is a particular factual statement about an individual.
D) It is a premise that indicates a physical sign like smoke.

Key Takeaway
A proof-syllogism is a categorical syllogism whose major premise is a publicly accepted statement, and it proves by acknowledged combination, not by physical signs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'All scholars are respected. Zayd is a scholar. Therefore Zayd is respected.' Is this a proof-syllogism or a sign-syllogism? Explain why the major premise is considered 'praised'.
?
Construct a proof-syllogism on a topic from Fiqh, such as the obligation of prayer, using a major premise that is commonly accepted by the public.

The Syllogism
intermediate

The Three Types of Sign-Syllogism
Definition of sign-syllogism and three types based on middle term position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct each of the three types of sign-syllogism based on the position of the middle term.

Core Idea
The sign-syllogism's validity depends on where the middle term sits relative to the major and minor.

In this passage, the author defines the sign-syllogism and then breaks it down into three distinct types according to the logical role of the middle term. Understanding this classification is key to evaluating whether a given sign-argument is strong or weak.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the definition of sign-syllogism
Locate the opening sentence: 'وقياس العلامة أيضا يرجع إلى الأشكال، وإنه ضمير يثبت فيه الأكبر للأصغر بعلامة'. It tells you it is a syllogism of the conscience where the major is affirmed for the minor by a sign.
2
Identify the three positions of the middle term
The author lists three cases: (1) middle is predicated of the minor but not the major, (2) middle is subject of both, (3) middle is predicated of both. Write these down.
3
Examine the examples for each case
For case 1: milk as a sign of childbirth. For case 2: 'so-and-so is virtuous and wise' as evidence that the wise have virtues. For case 3: yellowness as a sign of childbirth, with the note that the conversion is from 'every mother is yellow' not 'every yellow is a mother'.
4
Note the figure assignment
The author explicitly says case 3 uses the second figure. For case 2, the middle being subject of both suggests the third figure. Case 1 is incomplete without a universal premise and is not assigned a figure.
Recognizing the figure helps assess the sign's strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقياس العلامة أيضا يرجع إلى الأشكال، وإنه ضمير يثبت فيه الأكبر للأصغر بعلامة. وتلك العلامة إما ضرورية وإما محمودة مظنونة; والحد الأوسط في هذا القياس يقع على ثلاث جهات وذلك لأن الأوسط إما أن يكون محمولا على الأصغر دون الأكبر مثل اللبن إذا جعل علامة للولادة فيقال هذه المرأة لها لبن فقد ولدت، وإما أن يكون موضوعا لهما جميعا كما يقال الحكماء ذو فضائل لأن فلانا ذو فضيلة وهو حكيم، وإما أن يكون محمولا عليهما جميعا؛ ولو بالإيجاب في الشكل الثاني لأن مثل هذا في الخطابيات مقبول. فيقال مثلا هذه المرأة صفراء فقد ولدت، ولا يقال كل صفراء والدة بل كل والدة صفراء؛ فهذا يقبل في الظن، وإنما يكون علامة ودليلا إذا ضم الكبرى وإلا لكان قياسا. والذي في الشكل الأول يستمر ولا ينتقص وهو صحيح. وأما الذي في الشكل الثالث فينتقص فإنه ليس إذا كان حكيم ما فاضلا كان كل حكيم فاضلا. وكذلك ما يكون في القياس الثاني لأن القياس المركب من موجبتين ولا تنعكس كبراه سهل المناقضة بأن يقال ليس يجب أن تكون صغراء والدة فيكون طعنا في التأليف، وتبيينها على أنه غير واجب بما هو أيضا محمود ومقبول. وأما المؤاخذة بما هو بينة برهاني وليس مشهورا مستعملا في العرف فليس عدلا في الخطابة. فلذلك صار الذي في الشكل الأول أفضل العلامات. ولا يظن أن المقدمات في قياس العلامة لا بد وأن تكون موجبة بل يمكن أن تكون موجبة وسالبة كما في الدليل فإنه قد يكون للعدم علامة وهو عدم علامة الوجود مثلا. ولا يظن أيضا أن الدليل والعلامة بمعنى واحد؛ إذ الدليل هو الذي جعل علامة من الشكل الأول، والعلامة هي ما في الغير من الأشكال فكان الدليل أقوى من العلامة؛ والعلامة دليل ضعيف. والعلامة قد تسمى علة لما أنها علة للعلم بالشيء. وفرق بين قولهم العلة كذا وبين قولهم كذا «العلة». وكذلك بين قولهم العلة هي كذا وبين قولهم «العلة هي الكذا»، وعلى هذا في الدليل والعلامة وإنه من جملة ما قد مر الكلام فيه. ثم من المستدلين بالشاهد على الغائب قوم يطلبون القياسات كلها من العلامات، ويحصلون مقدمة كلية من العلامة والحد الأكبر بطريق الاستقراء.
And the syllogism of the sign also returns to the figures, and it is a syllogism of the conscience in which the major term is affirmed of the minor by means of a sign. That sign is either necessary or praised and conjectured. The middle term in this syllogism occurs in three ways: either it is predicated of the minor but not of the major, like milk when it is made a sign of childbirth, so it is said 'This woman has milk, therefore she has given birth'; or it is subject of both, as is said 'The wise possess virtues because so-and-so possesses virtue and he is wise'; or it is predicated of both, even by affirmation in the second figure, because such is accepted in rhetorical discourses. For example, it is said 'This woman is yellow, therefore she has given birth,' and it is not said 'Every yellow woman is a mother' but rather 'Every mother is yellow'; this is accepted in conjecture, and it is only a sign and proof if the major premise is added, otherwise it would be a syllogism. That which is in the first figure proceeds without deficiency and is sound. As for that in the third figure, it is deficient, for if some wise person is virtuous, it does not follow that every wise person is virtuous. Similarly, what is in the second syllogism because the syllogism composed of two affirmatives whose major premise does not convert is easily refuted by saying 'It is not necessary that a small woman is a mother,' so it is an attack on the composition, and its demonstration that it is not necessary is also praised and accepted. As for objecting with what is a demonstrative proof and not well-known and used in custom, it is not fair in rhetoric. Therefore, that which is in the first figure is the best of signs. And one should not think that the premises in the sign-syllogism must necessarily be affirmative; rather, they can be affirmative and negative as in the proof, for non-existence may have a sign which is the absence of a sign of existence, for example. Nor should one think that proof and sign are the same; rather, the proof is that which is made a sign from the first figure, and the sign is what is in other forms, so the proof is stronger than the sign, and the sign is a weak proof. The sign may be called a cause because it is a cause of knowledge of the thing. There is a difference between saying 'the cause is such' and saying 'such is the cause,' and similarly between saying 'the cause is such' and 'the cause is the such.' And so on regarding proof and sign, and it is among what has been discussed. Then among those who reason from the seen to the unseen, there are people who seek all syllogisms from signs, and they obtain a universal premise from the sign and the major term by way of induction.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage defines the sign-syllogism (قياس العلامة) and classifies its types. The author states that it is a syllogism of the conscience (ضمير) where the major term is established for the minor by a sign. The sign can be necessary (ضرورية) or praised and conjectured (محمودة مظنونة). The middle term in this syllogism falls into three categories: (1) it is predicated of the minor but not the major, e.g., milk as a sign of childbirth: 'This woman has milk, therefore she gave birth'; (2) it is subject of both terms, e.g., 'The wise have virtues, for so-and-so is virtuous and wise'; (3) it is predicated of both, even by affirmation in the second figure, which is acceptable in rhetoric, e.g., 'This woman is yellow, therefore she gave birth,' noting that the conversion is from 'every mother is yellow' not 'every yellow is a mother'. The strength of the sign varies by figure: the first figure is sound and uninterrupted; the third is deficient (not every wise person is virtuous); the second is easily refuted because an affirmative syllogism whose major premise does not convert is open to objection. The author then compares sign to proof: proof is a sign taken from the first figure, while sign is from other figures; hence proof is stronger. The sign may be called a cause (علة) because it causes knowledge. A distinction is drawn between stating 'the cause is such' and 'such is the cause' in terms of logical order. Finally, some reasoners from the seen to the unseen derive all syllogisms from signs, obtaining universal premises via induction.

Key Terms
العلامة
الحد الأوسط
الشكل الثاني
الشكل الثالث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the sign-syllogism type where the middle term is predicated of both the minor and the major, which figure is used according to the author?
A) The first figure
B) The second figure
C) The third figure
D) No specific figure

Key Takeaway
The sign-syllogism is a syllogism where the major is affirmed by a sign; its middle term can appear in three positions, each corresponding to a different figure and level of strength.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a type 1 sign-syllogism (middle predicated of minor only) on a fiqh issue: 'This substance is intoxicating, therefore it is forbidden.' What is the missing universal premise that would turn it into a full syllogism?
?
Identify the type of sign-syllogism in the following: 'Zaid is learned, therefore Zaid is knowledgeable, because every learned person is knowledgeable.' Match it to one of the three types based on middle term position.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Evaluating Sign-Syllogisms: Strength by Figure and the Distinction from Proof
Evaluation of sign-syllogisms by figure and comparison to proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the strength of a sign-syllogism based on its figure and distinguish between a sign (al-'alama) and a proof (al-dalil).

Core Idea
The first figure yields the strongest sign; signs differ in strength from proofs, which are first-figure signs.

Having defined the three types, the author now ranks them: the first-figure sign is best, the third is deficient, the second is easily refuted. He also clarifies that a proof (dalil) is a sign taken from the first figure, making it stronger than signs from other figures. This lesson will help you evaluate the reliability of sign-arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the evaluation of each figure
Find where the author discusses the first, second, and third figure signs. He says: 'والذي في الشكل الأول يستمر ولا ينتقص وهو صحيح' (first figure is sound), 'الذي في الشكل الثالث فينتقص' (third is deficient), and the second is open to objection because of conversion issues.
2
Understand why the first figure is best
The first figure sign is sound because its universal premise (if added) is in the correct direction. For example, with the yellowness sign, the correct universal is 'every mother is yellow', not the reverse, so the first figure yields a valid syllogism.
3
Grasp the weakness of the third figure
The author gives the example: if some wise person is virtuous, it does not follow that every wise person is virtuous. This is a fallacy of incomplete induction, making the third-figure sign weak.
4
Distinguish between sign and proof
The proof (dalil) is specifically a sign taken from the first figure; signs from other figures are weaker and called simply 'alama. The proof is stronger because it rests on a sound figure.
The sign may also be called a cause (علة) because it causes knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقياس العلامة أيضا يرجع إلى الأشكال، وإنه ضمير يثبت فيه الأكبر للأصغر بعلامة. وتلك العلامة إما ضرورية وإما محمودة مظنونة; والحد الأوسط في هذا القياس يقع على ثلاث جهات وذلك لأن الأوسط إما أن يكون محمولا على الأصغر دون الأكبر مثل اللبن إذا جعل علامة للولادة فيقال هذه المرأة لها لبن فقد ولدت، وإما أن يكون موضوعا لهما جميعا كما يقال الحكماء ذو فضائل لأن فلانا ذو فضيلة وهو حكيم، وإما أن يكون محمولا عليهما جميعا؛ ولو بالإيجاب في الشكل الثاني لأن مثل هذا في الخطابيات مقبول. فيقال مثلا هذه المرأة صفراء فقد ولدت، ولا يقال كل صفراء والدة بل كل والدة صفراء؛ فهذا يقبل في الظن، وإنما يكون علامة ودليلا إذا ضم الكبرى وإلا لكان قياسا. والذي في الشكل الأول يستمر ولا ينتقص وهو صحيح. وأما الذي في الشكل الثالث فينتقص فإنه ليس إذا كان حكيم ما فاضلا كان كل حكيم فاضلا. وكذلك ما يكون في القياس الثاني لأن القياس المركب من موجبتين ولا تنعكس كبراه سهل المناقضة بأن يقال ليس يجب أن تكون صغراء والدة فيكون طعنا في التأليف، وتبيينها على أنه غير واجب بما هو أيضا محمود ومقبول. وأما المؤاخذة بما هو بينة برهاني وليس مشهورا مستعملا في العرف فليس عدلا في الخطابة. فلذلك صار الذي في الشكل الأول أفضل العلامات. ولا يظن أن المقدمات في قياس العلامة لا بد وأن تكون موجبة بل يمكن أن تكون موجبة وسالبة كما في الدليل فإنه قد يكون للعدم علامة وهو عدم علامة الوجود مثلا. ولا يظن أيضا أن الدليل والعلامة بمعنى واحد؛ إذ الدليل هو الذي جعل علامة من الشكل الأول، والعلامة هي ما في الغير من الأشكال فكان الدليل أقوى من العلامة؛ والعلامة دليل ضعيف. والعلامة قد تسمى علة لما أنها علة للعلم بالشيء. وفرق بين قولهم العلة كذا وبين قولهم كذا «العلة». وكذلك بين قولهم العلة هي كذا وبين قولهم «العلة هي الكذا»، وعلى هذا في الدليل والعلامة وإنه من جملة ما قد مر الكلام فيه. ثم من المستدلين بالشاهد على الغائب قوم يطلبون القياسات كلها من العلامات، ويحصلون مقدمة كلية من العلامة والحد الأكبر بطريق الاستقراء.
And the syllogism of the sign also returns to the figures, and it is a syllogism of the conscience in which the major term is affirmed of the minor by means of a sign. That sign is either necessary or praised and conjectured. The middle term in this syllogism occurs in three ways: either it is predicated of the minor but not of the major, like milk when it is made a sign of childbirth, so it is said 'This woman has milk, therefore she has given birth'; or it is subject of both, as is said 'The wise possess virtues because so-and-so possesses virtue and he is wise'; or it is predicated of both, even by affirmation in the second figure, because such is accepted in rhetorical discourses. For example, it is said 'This woman is yellow, therefore she has given birth,' and it is not said 'Every yellow woman is a mother' but rather 'Every mother is yellow'; this is accepted in conjecture, and it is only a sign and proof if the major premise is added, otherwise it would be a syllogism. That which is in the first figure proceeds without deficiency and is sound. As for that in the third figure, it is deficient, for if some wise person is virtuous, it does not follow that every wise person is virtuous. Similarly, what is in the second syllogism because the syllogism composed of two affirmatives whose major premise does not convert is easily refuted by saying 'It is not necessary that a small woman is a mother,' so it is an attack on the composition, and its demonstration that it is not necessary is also praised and accepted. As for objecting with what is a demonstrative proof and not well-known and used in custom, it is not fair in rhetoric. Therefore, that which is in the first figure is the best of signs. And one should not think that the premises in the sign-syllogism must necessarily be affirmative; rather, they can be affirmative and negative as in the proof, for non-existence may have a sign which is the absence of a sign of existence, for example. Nor should one think that proof and sign are the same; rather, the proof is that which is made a sign from the first figure, and the sign is what is in other forms, so the proof is stronger than the sign, and the sign is a weak proof. The sign may be called a cause because it is a cause of knowledge of the thing. There is a difference between saying 'the cause is such' and saying 'such is the cause,' and similarly between saying 'the cause is such' and 'the cause is the such.' And so on regarding proof and sign, and it is among what has been discussed. Then among those who reason from the seen to the unseen, there are people who seek all syllogisms from signs, and they obtain a universal premise from the sign and the major term by way of induction.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage defines the sign-syllogism (قياس العلامة) and classifies its types. The author states that it is a syllogism of the conscience (ضمير) where the major term is established for the minor by a sign. The sign can be necessary (ضرورية) or praised and conjectured (محمودة مظنونة). The middle term in this syllogism falls into three categories: (1) it is predicated of the minor but not the major, e.g., milk as a sign of childbirth: 'This woman has milk, therefore she gave birth'; (2) it is subject of both terms, e.g., 'The wise have virtues, for so-and-so is virtuous and wise'; (3) it is predicated of both, even by affirmation in the second figure, which is acceptable in rhetoric, e.g., 'This woman is yellow, therefore she gave birth,' noting that the conversion is from 'every mother is yellow' not 'every yellow is a mother'. The strength of the sign varies by figure: the first figure is sound and uninterrupted; the third is deficient (not every wise person is virtuous); the second is easily refuted because an affirmative syllogism whose major premise does not convert is open to objection. The author then compares sign to proof: proof is a sign taken from the first figure, while sign is from other figures; hence proof is stronger. The sign may be called a cause (علة) because it causes knowledge. A distinction is drawn between stating 'the cause is such' and 'such is the cause' in terms of logical order. Finally, some reasoners from the seen to the unseen derive all syllogisms from signs, obtaining universal premises via induction.

Key Terms

The Syllogism

Types of Signs in Rhetorical Syllogisms
Author presents two types of signs: subject common to both terms and predicate common to both terms in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of signs in rhetorical syllogisms and distinguish them from full demonstrative proofs.

Core Idea
Rhetorical signs produce opinion, not certainty, and become proofs only when the missing premise is added.

In rhetorical reasoning, we often use signs that are not fully deductive but still persuasive. This lesson examines two types of signs based on the position of the middle term: subject of both premises or predicate of both premises. The author explains how these signs work in the second figure and when they become proper proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term position
In the first type, the middle term is the subject common to both premises (mawḍūʿ lahumā). In the second type, it is the predicate common to both premises (maḥmūl ʿalayhimā). Examine the examples given: 'The wise possess virtues because so-and-so is virtuous and wise' has 'wise' as subject of both. 'This woman is yellow; therefore she has given birth' has 'yellow' as predicate of both (minor premise: 'this woman is yellow', major premise: 'every mother is yellow').
2
Recognize the second figure
When the middle term is predicate of both premises, the syllogism is in the second figure. In the example, the middle term 'yellow' is predicate in both: 'this woman is yellow' (minor) and 'every mother is yellow' (major). The second figure typically requires a negative conclusion, but here it is used with an affirmative conclusion in rhetoric.
Rhetorical syllogisms often violate strict logical constraints to achieve persuasion.
3
Distinguish sign from proof
The author says the sign (ʿalāma) only produces opinion (ẓann). It becomes a proof (dalīl) when the major premise is explicitly stated. Without the explicit major premise, it is a mere sign. For example, 'This woman is yellow, therefore she has given birth' is accepted in rhetoric because the audience believes the implied major premise 'every mother is yellow', even though it is not convertible.
4
Test the logical form
Try to reconstruct the implicit syllogism. For the sign to be a full syllogism, you need: Major premise: 'Every mother is yellow'; Minor premise: 'This woman is yellow'; Conclusion: 'Therefore, she is a mother.' But note that this is invalid because the middle term is not distributed (fallacy of undistributed middle). However, in rhetoric, it is accepted because it is plausible and produces opinion.
This shows that rhetorical arguments are not always logically valid but are persuasive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] وإما أن يكون موضوعًا لهما جميعا كما يقال الحكماء ذو فضائل لأن فلانًا ذو فضيلة وهو حكيم، [ت] وإما أن يكون محمولا عليهما جميعا؛ ولو بالإيجاب في الشكل الثاني لأن مثل هذا في الخطابيات مقبول. فيقال مثلا هذه المرأة صفراء فقد ولدت، ولا يقال كل صفراء والدة بل كل والدة صفراء؛ فهذا يقبل في الظن، وإنما يكون علامة ودليلا إذا ضم الكبرى وإلا لكان قياسًا.
[B] Or it may be a subject common to the two of them, as when it is said: 'The wise possess virtues because so-and-so possesses virtue and he is wise.' [T] Or it may be a predicate common to the two of them, and that by affirmation in the second figure because such [arguments] are acceptable in rhetorical discourses. So it is said, for example: 'This woman is yellow; therefore she has given birth.' But it is not said: 'Every yellow woman is a mother,' rather: 'Every mother is yellow.' So this is accepted in opinion, and it only becomes a sign and a proof if the major premise is added; otherwise it would be a syllogism.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage describes two types of signs used in rhetorical reasoning. The first type [ب] occurs when the middle term is the subject common to both premises, as in the example: 'The wise possess virtues because so-and-so is virtuous and wise.' The second type [ت] occurs when the middle term is the predicate common to both premises, typically using the second figure with an affirmative conclusion, as in the example: 'This woman is yellow, therefore she has given birth.' The implicit major premise is 'Every mother is yellow'—not the converse—which is accepted in rhetoric because it produces belief (ẓann), not certainty. The sign becomes a full proof (dalīl) when the major premise is explicitly stated; otherwise it remains merely a sign (ʿalāma). The author distinguishes this from a full syllogism (qiyās) which would require explicit premises. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that rhetorical signs are deductively valid proofs; they are only plausible indicators.

Key Terms
موضوع لهما
محمول عليهما
الشكل الثاني
علامة
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second example of a sign, why is 'this woman is yellow, therefore she has given birth' accepted in rhetoric?
A) Because it is a valid first-figure syllogism
B) Because the major premise 'every mother is yellow' is true
C) Because it is based on a plausible premise that is not convertible
D) Because it is a demonstrative proof

Key Takeaway
Rhetorical signs rely on plausible but not necessarily convertible premises, producing opinion rather than certainty, and must be distinguished from complete syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why the First Figure Produces the Best Signs
Author evaluates the three figures for sign-syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of a sign-syllogism in each of the three figures.

Core Idea
Only the first figure gives a sound and continuous sign.

In this passage, the author compares the three figures of categorical syllogism when used as signs. He shows that the first figure is perfect, the third is deficient, and the second is easily opposed. Understanding this helps you know which sign-arguments are reliable in Islamic argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about the first figure
What does the author say about signs in the first figure? They are continuous and sound.
2
Examine the third figure example
The author gives: 'If a wise man is virtuous, it does not follow that every wise man is virtuous.' Why is this deficient? The conclusion is not universal.
The third figure often yields particular conclusions.
3
Analyze the second figure problem
The author says the syllogism with two affirmatives in the second figure is easily opposed because the major premise is not convertible. What does 'convertible' mean? The major premise must be convertible to yield a valid conclusion; here it is not.
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises.
4
Understand why the first figure is best
The author concludes that the first figure produces the best signs because it is sound and continuous. What does this imply for using signs in kalam?
The first figure is the only one that yields certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي في الشكل الأول يستمر ولا ينتقص وهو صحيح. وأما الذي في الشكل الثالث فينتقص فإنه ليس إذا كان حكيم ما فاضلا كان كل حكيم فاضلا. وكذلك ما يكون في القياس الثاني لأن القياس المركب من موجبتين ولا تنعكس كبراه سهل المناقضة بأن يقال ليس يجب أن تكون صغراء والدة فيكون طعناً في التأليف، وتبيينها على أنه غير واجب بما هو أيضًا محمود ومقبول.
وأما المؤاخذة بما هو بينة برهاني وليس مشهورًا مستعملا في العرف فليس عدلا في الخطابة. فلذلك صار الذي في الشكل الأول أفضل العلامات. ولا يظن أن المقدمات في قياس العلامة لا بد وأن تكون موجبة بل يمكن أن تكون موجبة وسالبة كما في الدليل فإنه قد يكون للعدم علامة وهو عدم علامة [۲۷۱] الوجود مثلا . ولا يظن أيضًا أن الدليل والعلامة بمعنى واحد / ؛ إذ الدليل هو الذي جعل علامة من الشكل الأول، والعلامة هي ما في الغير من الأشكال فكان الدليل أقوى من العلامة ؛ والعلامة دليل ضعيف. والعلامة قد تسمى علة لما أنها علة للعلم بالشيء. وفرق بين قولهم العلة كذا وبين قولهم كذا «العلة . وكذلك بين قولهم العلة هي كذا وبين قولهم «العلة هي الكذا»، وعلى هذا في الدليل والعلامة وإنه من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه.
ثم من المستدلين بالشاهد على الغائب قوم يطلبون القياسات كلها من العلامات، ويحصلون مقدمة كلية من العلامة والحد الأكبر بطريق الاستقراء
إما مستويًا وإما منعكسًا وهو الذي يكون على عكس النقيض ويسمون العلامة علة. وبيان كونها علة بالطريق الذي مرَّ ذكره أولا في التمثيل وهو المسمى بتنقيح المناط؛ والكلام فيه قد مرّ فلا يعاد مرة أخرى.
And that which is in the first figure is continuous, not deficient, and it is sound. As for that which is in the third figure, it is deficient: for if a wise man is virtuous, it does not follow that every wise man is virtuous. Similarly, what is in the second figure, because the syllogism composed of two affirmatives whose major premise is not convertible is easily opposed by saying: 'It is not necessary that the minor term is a mother,' so it becomes an attack on the composition, and its clarification that it is not necessary is also praiseworthy and acceptable.
And as for the objection using what is a demonstrative proof and not a commonly used custom, it is not just in rhetoric. Therefore that which is in the first figure becomes the best of signs. And do not think that the premises in a sign-syllogism must be affirmative; rather they can be affirmative and negative, as in the proof: for non-existence may have a sign, which is the absence of the sign of existence, for example. And do not think also that the proof and the sign are synonymous; rather, the proof is what is made a sign from the first figure, and the sign is what is in other figures. Thus the proof is stronger than the sign, and the sign is a weak proof. And the sign may be called a cause (ʿilla) because it is a cause of knowledge of the thing. And there is a difference between saying 'the cause is such-and-such' and 'such-and-such is the cause.' And similarly between saying 'the cause is such-and-such' and 'such-and-such is the cause.' And regarding this, the discussion of proof and sign has already passed.
Then among those who infer from the witnessed to the unseen, some seek all syllogisms from signs, and they obtain a universal premise from the sign and the major term by induction, either straightforwardly or by contraposition (inversion of the contradictory), and they call the sign a cause. And the demonstration of its being a cause is by the method mentioned first in the example, which is called tanqīḥ al-manāṭ; and the discussion of it has passed, so it will not be repeated again.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of signs in rhetoric. The author evaluates the three figures: the first figure is perfect and continuous; the third figure is deficient because it does not yield a universal conclusion (from 'a wise man is virtuous' one cannot infer 'every wise man is virtuous'). The second figure syllogism composed of two affirmatives is also weak because the major premise is not convertible, and it is easy to object that the minor term might not be a mother. However, such objection is itself acceptable in rhetoric. The author then states that using a demonstrative premise as an objection is not fair in rhetoric. Therefore, the first figure produces the best signs. He clarifies that the premises of a sign-syllogism can be negative as well; for example, non-existence can be inferred from the absence of a sign of existence. Then he distinguishes between proof (dalīl) and sign (ʿalāma): the proof is a sign taken from the first figure, while the sign is from other figures, so the proof is stronger. The sign can be called a cause (ʿilla) because it causes knowledge. There is a subtle difference in phrasing: 'the cause is such-and-such' vs 'such-and-such is the cause.' Finally, some logicians derive all syllogisms from signs and produce a universal premise by induction (istiqrāʾ), either straightforward or by contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ), and they call the sign a cause. The method of demonstrating that it is a cause is tanqīḥ al-manāṭ, which has been discussed earlier. This passage guards against the confusion between proof and sign, and clarifies that the first figure yields the most reliable signs.

الشكل الأول
الشكل الثالث
القياس الثاني

Distinguishing Proof (Dalīl) from Sign (ʿAlāma)
Author refutes misconceptions and defines proof vs sign.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a proof (dalīl) and a sign (ʿalāma) based on figure and strength, and recognize that sign-syllogisms can have negative premises.

Core Idea
A proof is a strong sign from the first figure; a sign is a weak proof from other figures.

The author clears up two common misconceptions: that sign-syllogisms must have affirmative premises, and that 'proof' and 'sign' are synonyms. He defines proof as a sign from the first figure, and sign as a sign from other figures. This distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two misconceptions
What two things does the author warn against thinking? (1. Premises must be affirmative; 2. Proof and sign are synonymous.)
2
Understand the flexibility of premises
The author gives an example: non-existence can be a sign, i.e., absence of the sign of existence. How does this show that premises can be negative?
The sign of non-existence is the absence of the sign of existence, which involves a negative premise.
3
Define proof and sign
According to the author, what is the difference? (Proof is a sign from the first figure; sign is from other figures.)
This makes proof stronger.
4
Analyze the cause terminology
Why is the sign called a cause? (Because it causes knowledge.) What subtle distinction does the author make about 'cause'? (Difference in phrasing: 'the cause is such-and-such' vs 'such-and-such is the cause.')
The order of words changes the logical import.

Deriving Universal Premises from Signs via Induction and Contraposition
Author describes methods for deriving universal premises from signs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how logicians derive a universal premise from signs using induction (istiqrāʾ) or contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ), and explain the role of tanqīḥ al-manāṭ in identifying the cause.

Core Idea
Universal premises from signs are obtained by induction or contraposition, and the cause is identified by tanqīḥ al-manāṭ.

This passage explains a method used by some logicians to derive universal premises from particular signs. They use either straightforward induction (collecting instances) or contraposition (inversion of contradictory) to reach a universal statement. They then call the sign a cause, and the method to verify the cause is tanqīḥ al-manāṭ, a technique familiar from usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the goal
What are these logicians trying to obtain? (A universal premise from the sign and the major term.)
2
Two methods mentioned
What are the two ways? (Straight induction and contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ).)
3
Understand straightforward induction
How does it work? Collect multiple instances of the sign and generalize.
Induction can be complete or incomplete; here likely incomplete.
4
Understand contraposition
The author says 'inversion of the contradictory'. How does this produce a universal premise? It transforms 'if sign then thing' into 'if not thing then not sign', which is universal if the original is.
Contraposition preserves truth for conditionals.
5
Link to cause and tanqīḥ al-manāṭ
Why are signs called causes? Because they cause knowledge. What is tanqīḥ al-manāṭ? A method of eliminating irrelevant attributes to identify the effective cause.
Tanqīḥ al-manāṭ is discussed earlier in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي في الشكل الأول يستمر ولا ينتقص وهو صحيح. وأما الذي في الشكل الثالث فينتقص فإنه ليس إذا كان حكيم ما فاضلا كان كل حكيم فاضلا. وكذلك ما يكون في القياس الثاني لأن القياس المركب من موجبتين ولا تنعكس كبراه سهل المناقضة بأن يقال ليس يجب أن تكون صغراء والدة فيكون طعناً في التأليف، وتبيينها على أنه غير واجب بما هو أيضًا محمود ومقبول.
وأما المؤاخذة بما هو بينة برهاني وليس مشهورًا مستعملا في العرف فليس عدلا في الخطابة. فلذلك صار الذي في الشكل الأول أفضل العلامات. ولا يظن أن المقدمات في قياس العلامة لا بد وأن تكون موجبة بل يمكن أن تكون موجبة وسالبة كما في الدليل فإنه قد يكون للعدم علامة وهو عدم علامة [۲۷۱] الوجود مثلا . ولا يظن أيضًا أن الدليل والعلامة بمعنى واحد / ؛ إذ الدليل هو الذي جعل علامة من الشكل الأول، والعلامة هي ما في الغير من الأشكال فكان الدليل أقوى من العلامة ؛ والعلامة دليل ضعيف. والعلامة قد تسمى علة لما أنها علة للعلم بالشيء. وفرق بين قولهم العلة كذا وبين قولهم كذا «العلة . وكذلك بين قولهم العلة هي كذا وبين قولهم «العلة هي الكذا»، وعلى هذا في الدليل والعلامة وإنه من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه.
ثم من المستدلين بالشاهد على الغائب قوم يطلبون القياسات كلها من العلامات، ويحصلون مقدمة كلية من العلامة والحد الأكبر بطريق الاستقراء
إما مستويًا وإما منعكسًا وهو الذي يكون على عكس النقيض ويسمون العلامة علة. وبيان كونها علة بالطريق الذي مرَّ ذكره أولا في التمثيل وهو المسمى بتنقيح المناط؛ والكلام فيه قد مرّ فلا يعاد مرة أخرى.
And that which is in the first figure is continuous, not deficient, and it is sound. As for that which is in the third figure, it is deficient: for if a wise man is virtuous, it does not follow that every wise man is virtuous. Similarly, what is in the second figure, because the syllogism composed of two affirmatives whose major premise is not convertible is easily opposed by saying: 'It is not necessary that the minor term is a mother,' so it becomes an attack on the composition, and its clarification that it is not necessary is also praiseworthy and acceptable.
And as for the objection using what is a demonstrative proof and not a commonly used custom, it is not just in rhetoric. Therefore that which is in the first figure becomes the best of signs. And do not think that the premises in a sign-syllogism must be affirmative; rather they can be affirmative and negative, as in the proof: for non-existence may have a sign, which is the absence of the sign of existence, for example. And do not think also that the proof and the sign are synonymous; rather, the proof is what is made a sign from the first figure, and the sign is what is in other figures. Thus the proof is stronger than the sign, and the sign is a weak proof. And the sign may be called a cause (ʿilla) because it is a cause of knowledge of the thing. And there is a difference between saying 'the cause is such-and-such' and 'such-and-such is the cause.' And similarly between saying 'the cause is such-and-such' and 'such-and-such is the cause.' And regarding this, the discussion of proof and sign has already passed.
Then among those who infer from the witnessed to the unseen, some seek all syllogisms from signs, and they obtain a universal premise from the sign and the major term by induction, either straightforwardly or by contraposition (inversion of the contradictory), and they call the sign a cause. And the demonstration of its being a cause is by the method mentioned first in the example, which is called tanqīḥ al-manāṭ; and the discussion of it has passed, so it will not be repeated again.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of signs in rhetoric. The author evaluates the three figures: the first figure is perfect and continuous; the third figure is deficient because it does not yield a universal conclusion (from 'a wise man is virtuous' one cannot infer 'every wise man is virtuous'). The second figure syllogism composed of two affirmatives is also weak because the major premise is not convertible, and it is easy to object that the minor term might not be a mother. However, such objection is itself acceptable in rhetoric. The author then states that using a demonstrative premise as an objection is not fair in rhetoric. Therefore, the first figure produces the best signs. He clarifies that the premises of a sign-syllogism can be negative as well; for example, non-existence can be inferred from the absence of a sign of existence. Then he distinguishes between proof (dalīl) and sign (ʿalāma): the proof is a sign taken from the first figure, while the sign is from other figures, so the proof is stronger. The sign can be called a cause (ʿilla) because it causes knowledge. There is a subtle difference in phrasing: 'the cause is such-and-such' vs 'such-and-such is the cause.' Finally, some logicians derive all syllogisms from signs and produce a universal premise by induction (istiqrāʾ), either straightforward or by contraposition (ʿaks al-naqīḍ), and they call the sign a cause. The method of demonstrating that it is a cause is tanqīḥ al-manāṭ, which has been discussed earlier. This passage guards against the confusion between proof and sign, and clarifies that the first figure yields the most reliable signs.

The Syllogism of Physiognomy: Inferring Character from Bodily Form
Author defines the structure and verification of the physiognomy syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the two-step analogical syllogism of physiognomy (form → temperament → moral quality) and apply the inductive check to verify the link between a bodily form and a moral trait.

Core Idea
Physiognomy infers character via a two-step syllogism: form → temperament → moral quality, verified by induction.

This lesson unpacks a special type of analogical syllogism called qiyās al-firāsa (the syllogism of physiognomy). The author explains how a bodily form (hayʾa badaniyya) serves as the middle term, leading first to a temperament (mizāj) and then to a moral quality (khuluq nafsānī). By working through the example of Zayd's broad chest and bravery, you will see how this two-step inference is strengthened by observing animals that share the same bodily form. The goal is to equip you with a method for evaluating such inferences critically, using the inductive check described in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
The author states that the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) in the physiognomy syllogism is the bodily form (hayʾa badaniyya), e.g., a broad chest. Do not confuse it with the temperament or the moral quality.
2
Trace the two-step inference
The inference moves from the bodily form to an underlying temperament (mizāj), and from that temperament to a moral quality (khuluq nafsānī). Write this as: bodily form → temperament → moral quality. For example, broad chest → specific temperament → bravery.
3
Analyze the example
The author gives the example: 'Zayd is brave, for he is broad-chested like a lion.' Identify each term: broad chest is the bodily form, bravery is the moral quality, and the temperament is implied (the specific mixture that produces both the broad chest and bravery).
4
Apply the inductive verification
The author explains that to confirm the connection, one must check animals that share the same bodily form and see if they also share the moral quality. If those with the form have the quality and those without it do not, then a strong conjecture (ḥads qawī) is obtained. This step guards against naive direct inference.
The passage says: 'this is verified by looking at animals that share that bodily form: if that moral quality is found in those having that bodily form, and is not found in those not having such a form, then one makes a strong conjecture.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في قياس الفراسة ] واعلم بأن قياس العلامة وقياس الفراسة من جملة القياسات التمثيلية؛ وقياس الفراسة عبارة عما يكون الحد الأوسط فيه هيئة بدنية توجد في الحيوان وهو بيان تلك الهيئة أن تتَّبع مزاجا يتبع ذلك المزاج خُلق من أخلاق النفس فتلك الهيئة البدنية؛ وذلك الخُلق النفساني من توابع المزاج وهو كيفية تحدث في ابتداء الخلقة من امتزاج الأركان بعضها البعض. فإذا وجدت تلك الهيئة البدنية في الإنسان مثلا حدس بوجود ذلك الخُلق الإنساني له استدلالا بوجود تلك الهيئة على المزاج، وبوجود المزاج على ذلك الخلق، كما يقال إن زيدًا شجاع فإنه عريض الصدر كالأسد؛ فإنه عريض الصدر وله شجاعة، فاستدل بعريض الصدر على الشجاعة لما أنه من توابع مزاج خاص تتبعه الشجاعة لكنه لا يتم إلا ويسلم أن المزاجات الواقعة في ابتداء الخلقة والطبيعة تتبعها أخلاق نفسانية كما تتبعها هيئات بدنية. ويسلم أيضًا أن الأخلاق النفسانية التي لذلك الإنسان بواسطة حصول تلك الهيئة البدنية والنفس تتبع في تحقيق ذلك بأن تنظر في الحيوانات المشاركة لذلك الحيوان في تلك الهيئة البدنية: فإن وجد ذلك الخلق فيما له تلك الهيئة البدنية، ولا يوجد فيما ليس له مثل تلك الهيئة يحدس حينئذ حدسا قويًا وفراسة محكمة بأن ذلك الخلق من لوازم تلك الهيئة. وإن لم يكن الأمر كذلك يحدس بأن اللازم لتلك الهيئة
Chapter: On the Syllogism of Physiognomy. Know that the syllogism of the sign and the syllogism of physiognomy are among the analogical syllogisms. The syllogism of physiognomy is that whose middle term is a bodily form found in the animal, and its clarification is that this form follows a temperament, and that temperament is followed by a moral quality from the moral qualities of the soul. So that bodily form and that moral quality of the soul are among the concomitants of the temperament, and it is a quality that arises at the beginning of creation from the mixing of the elements with one another. So if that bodily form is found in a human, for example, it is conjectured (ḥudisa) that he possesses that human moral quality, by inference from the existence of that form to the temperament, and from the existence of the temperament to that moral quality, as it is said: 'Zayd is brave, for he is broad-chested like a lion.' For he is broad-chested and possesses bravery, so one infers from the broad chest to bravery because it is a consequence of a specific temperament that is accompanied by bravery. But it is not completed unless it is accepted that the temperaments occurring at the beginning of creation and nature are followed by moral qualities of the soul just as they are followed by bodily forms. And it is also accepted that the moral qualities of the soul that belong to that human by means of the acquisition of that bodily form and the soul... [truncated] This is verified by looking at animals that share that bodily form: if that moral quality is found in those having that bodily form, and is not found in those not having such a form, then one makes a strong conjecture (ḥads qawī) and a firm physiognomy that that moral quality is a necessary concomitant of that form. And if it is not so, one conjectures that the necessary concomitant of that form...
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage introduces a special type of analogical syllogism called the syllogism of physiognomy (qiyās al-firāsa). It belongs to the broader category of analogical syllogisms (al-qiyāsāt al-tamthīliyya). The author defines it as a syllogism whose middle term is a bodily form (hayʾa badaniyya) observed in animals. The reasoning proceeds in two steps: first, from the bodily form to the underlying temperament (mizāj), and second, from the temperament to a moral quality (khuluq nafsānī). The example given is that Zayd is brave because he has a broad chest like a lion. The broad chest indicates a specific temperament, which in turn is associated with bravery. The inference is strengthened by induction: by observing animals that share the same bodily form and seeing whether they also share the moral quality. If the quality is consistently present in animals with that form and absent in animals without it, then a strong conjecture (ḥads qawī) and firm physiognomy (firāsa muḥkama) is obtained. The passage is truncated, but it conveys the method of verifying the link between bodily form and character. This guards against the naive assumption that bodily form alone directly indicates character; rather, it requires an intermediate step through temperament and empirical induction.

Key Takeaway
A physiognomy syllogism is valid only when it proceeds from bodily form to temperament to moral quality, and the connection is empirically verified by induction on similar cases.

The Structure of Qiyas al-Firasa
Author defines qiyas al-firasa and lists its four limits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four limits in a qiyas al-firasa and explain how it functions as an analogical syllogism.

Core Idea
Qiyas al-firasa infers character from bodily form using a shared animal as the fourth limit.

The author defines qiyas al-firasa as a type of analogical syllogism where the middle term is a bodily form. In this lesson, we will dissect its structure: the four limits (minor, major, middle, and the shared animal) and see how it bridges a proof (dalil) and analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four limits
Find the four terms: minor (e.g., Zayd), major (bravery), middle (broad chest), and fourth (lion) in the example.
2
Understand the role of the fourth limit
The fourth limit is an animal that shares the bodily form with the human. It serves as a comparator to confirm the inference.
Without this, the inference would be merely speculative.
3
Trace the inferential chain
Notice the chain: bodily form indicates temperament, temperament indicates character. This is the logical path from middle to major.
4
Compare to categorical syllogism
Note that here the middle term is a particular trait, not a universal, and the fourth term is unique to analogical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن قياس العلامة وقياس الفراسة من جملة القياسات التمثيلية؛ وقياس الفراسة عبارة عما يكون الحد الأوسط فيه هيئة بدنية توجد في الحيوان وهو بيان تلك الهيئة أن تتَّبع مزاجا يتبع ذلك المزاج خُلق من أخلاق النفس فتلك الهيئة البدنية؛ وذلك الخُلق النفساني من توابع المزاج وهو كيفية تحدث في ابتداء الخلقة من امتزاج الأركان بعضها البعض. فإذا وجدت تلك الهيئة البدنية في الإنسان مثلا حدس بوجود ذلك الخُلق الإنساني له استدلالا بوجود تلك الهيئة على المزاج، وبوجود المزاج على ذلك الخلق، كما يقال إن زيدًا شجاع فإنه عريض الصدر كالأسد؛ فإنه عريض الصدر وله شجاعة، فاستدل بعريض الصدر على الشجاعة لما أنه من توابع مزاج خاص تتبعه الشجاعة لكنه لا يتم إلا ويسلم أن المزاجات الواقعة في ابتداء الخلقة والطبيعة تتبعها أخلاق نفسانية كما تتبعها هيئات بدنية. ويسلم أيضًا أن الأخلاق النفسانية التي لذلك الإنسان بواسطة حصول تلك الهيئة البدنية والنفس تتبع في تحقيق ذلك بأن تنظر في الحيوانات المشاركة لذلك الحيوان في تلك الهيئة البدنية: فإن وجد ذلك الخلق فيما له تلك الهيئة البدنية، ولا يوجد فيما ليس له مثل تلك الهيئة يحدس حينئذ حدسا قويًا وفراسة محكمة بأن ذلك الخلق من لوازم تلك الهيئة. وإن لم يكن الأمر كذلك يحدس بأن اللازم لتلك الهيئة ليس هذا الخلق بل خلق آخر. فإن كان هناك خلق آخر مع هذا الخلق مثل ما للأسد فإنه مع أنه شجاع ينسب إليه جود وكرم فيجب أن تتأمل أولا أيهما يتبع تلك الهيئة. فإنه إذا وجد الخلق الأول في كل فرد توجد تلك الهيئة فيه، ولا يوجد الخلق الثاني في الكل يعلم أن ذلك لا يكون بتلك الهيئة الأخرى أن النمر لا يشارك الأسد في الكرم ويشاركه في عظم الأطراف العالية، ومع ذلك فيشاركه في الشجاعة فيكون عظم الأطراف العالية دليلا على الشجاعة دون الكرم، فإذن يجب أن يكون الحد الأوسط في الفراسة أعم من الأصغر ومساويًا للأكبر. والحدود في هذا القياس أربعة كما في التمثيل : [أ] أحدهما الإنسان كزيد في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو الحد الأصغر. [ب] والثاني الخُلق الإنساني بالشجاعة فيه وهو الحد الأكبر. [ت] والثالث الهيئة البدنية وهو الحد الأوسط. [ث] والرابع الحيوان الآخر كالأسد وهو المشارك للأصغر في الحد الأوسط. وهذا القياس يشبه الدليل من حيث إن الأوسط فيه وهو عريض الصدر إذا حصل الحد الأصغر يتبعه وجود شيء آخر له كالشجاعة، ونسبة التمثيل أيضًا من حيث إن الحكم المدعى في جزئي كزيد لوجوده في جزئي آخر كالأسد.
Know that the syllogism of the sign and the syllogism of physiognomy are among the analogical syllogisms. The syllogism of physiognomy is that whose middle term is a bodily form found in the animal, and it is an explanation of that form that it follows a temperament that is followed by a character from among the characters of the soul, so that is the bodily form; and that soul-character is among the consequences of the temperament, which is a quality that occurs at the beginning of creation from the mixing of the elements with each other. When that bodily form is found in a human, for example, one surmises the existence of that human character for him, inferring by the existence of that form to the temperament, and by the existence of the temperament to that character, as it is said: Zayd is brave because he has a broad chest like a lion; for he has a broad chest and has bravery, so one infers from the broad chest to bravery because it is a consequence of a specific temperament that bravery follows. But it is not completed unless one concedes that the temperaments occurring at the beginning of creation and nature are followed by soul-characters just as they are followed by bodily forms. And one also concedes that the soul-characters that belong to that human by means of obtaining that bodily form and the soul are followed in confirming that by examining the animals that share with that animal in that bodily form: if that character is found in what has that bodily form, and is not found in what does not have such a form, then one surmises a strong surmise and a firm physiognomy that that character is a necessary concomitant of that form. And if it is not the case, he surmises that the necessary concomitant of that form is not this character but another character. If there is another character along with this character, such as for the lion—for while it is brave, generosity and nobility are also attributed to it—then one must first consider which of them follows that form. For if the first character is found in every individual in which that form exists, and the second character is not found in all, one knows that it is not due to that other form—that the tiger does not share with the lion in generosity but shares in the largeness of the high limbs, and yet shares in bravery, so the largeness of the high limbs is evidence for bravery without generosity. Therefore the middle term in physiognomy must be more general than the minor term and equal to the major term. The limits in this syllogism are four, as in analogical reasoning: [A] one is the human, like Zayd in the example that was mentioned—this is the minor limit. [B] The second is the human character of bravery in him—this is the major limit. [C] The third is the bodily form—this is the middle limit. [D] The fourth is the other animal, like the lion—this is the one that shares with the minor in the middle limit. This syllogism resembles a proof (dalil) in that the middle term—which is the broad chest—when it occurs in the minor term, the existence of something else like bravery follows; and it also has the aspect of analogy in that the judgment claimed for a particular like Zayd is because of its existence in another particular like the lion.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism of physiognomy (qiyas al-firasa) as a type of analogical reasoning (tamthili). The author explains its structure: the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) is a bodily form (hay'a badaniyya) that indicates a temperament (mizaj), which in turn indicates a soul-character (khuluq nafsani). For example, a broad chest indicates a certain temperament that is followed by bravery, as seen in a lion. The inference is not certain but a strong surmise (hads qawiyy). The author details the method: one must observe animals that share the bodily form and check if the character is consistently present or absent. If multiple characters are possible, one must determine which character actually follows the form by comparative observation. The passage then enumerates the four limits in this syllogism: the minor (e.g., Zayd), the major (bravery), the middle (broad chest), and the fourth term (the shared animal, e.g., lion). The author notes that this syllogism resembles a proof (dalil) because the middle term's presence leads to the major, and it is analogical because the judgment is transferred from one particular (lion) to another (Zayd).

Key Terms

الحد الأوسط
التمثيل
الخلق النفساني

The Method of Verification in Qiyas al-Firasa
Author describes the verification method and condition for the middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the comparative method used to confirm a physiognomic inference and handle cases with multiple possible characters.

Core Idea
To verify a qiyas al-firasa, one must compare animals that share the bodily form and check if the character consistently appears.

The author not only defines the structure but also explains how to ensure the inference is valid. This lesson focuses on the empirical method: examining animals with the same bodily form, handling multiple characters, and determining which character truly follows the form.

Guided Path
1
Understand the verification process
The author says one must examine animals that share the bodily form: if the character is found in all such animals and absent in those without the form, then the inference is strong.
2
Handle multiple characters
If a bodily form is associated with multiple characters (like bravery and generosity in lions), one must check which character is present in every animal with that form. The character found in all is the one that truly follows.
Use the example of lion vs. tiger: tiger shares bravery but not generosity, so bravery is the true concomitant.
3
Apply the condition for the middle term
The middle term (bodily form) must be more general than the minor (human) and equal to the major (character) in scope.
This ensures the inference is valid: the form covers all humans and corresponds exactly to the character.

واعلم بأن قياس العلامة وقياس الفراسة من جملة القياسات التمثيلية؛ وقياس الفراسة عبارة عما يكون الحد الأوسط فيه هيئة بدنية توجد في الحيوان وهو بيان تلك الهيئة أن تتَّبع مزاجا يتبع ذلك المزاج خُلق من أخلاق النفس فتلك الهيئة البدنية؛ وذلك الخُلق النفساني من توابع المزاج وهو كيفية تحدث في ابتداء الخلقة من امتزاج الأركان بعضها البعض. فإذا وجدت تلك الهيئة البدنية في الإنسان مثلا حدس بوجود ذلك الخُلق الإنساني له استدلالا بوجود تلك الهيئة على المزاج، وبوجود المزاج على ذلك الخلق، كما يقال إن زيدًا شجاع فإنه عريض الصدر كالأسد؛ فإنه عريض الصدر وله شجاعة، فاستدل بعريض الصدر على الشجاعة لما أنه من توابع مزاج خاص تتبعه الشجاعة لكنه لا يتم إلا ويسلم أن المزاجات الواقعة في ابتداء الخلقة والطبيعة تتبعها أخلاق نفسانية كما تتبعها هيئات بدنية. ويسلم أيضًا أن الأخلاق النفسانية التي لذلك الإنسان بواسطة حصول تلك الهيئة البدنية والنفس تتبع في تحقيق ذلك بأن تنظر في الحيوانات المشاركة لذلك الحيوان في تلك الهيئة البدنية: فإن وجد ذلك الخلق فيما له تلك الهيئة البدنية، ولا يوجد فيما ليس له مثل تلك الهيئة يحدس حينئذ حدسا قويًا وفراسة محكمة بأن ذلك الخلق من لوازم تلك الهيئة. وإن لم يكن الأمر كذلك يحدس بأن اللازم لتلك الهيئة ليس هذا الخلق بل خلق آخر. فإن كان هناك خلق آخر مع هذا الخلق مثل ما للأسد فإنه مع أنه شجاع ينسب إليه جود وكرم فيجب أن تتأمل أولا أيهما يتبع تلك الهيئة. فإنه إذا وجد الخلق الأول في كل فرد توجد تلك الهيئة فيه، ولا يوجد الخلق الثاني في الكل يعلم أن ذلك لا يكون بتلك الهيئة الأخرى أن النمر لا يشارك الأسد في الكرم ويشاركه في عظم الأطراف العالية، ومع ذلك فيشاركه في الشجاعة فيكون عظم الأطراف العالية دليلا على الشجاعة دون الكرم، فإذن يجب أن يكون الحد الأوسط في الفراسة أعم من الأصغر ومساويًا للأكبر. والحدود في هذا القياس أربعة كما في التمثيل : [أ] أحدهما الإنسان كزيد في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو الحد الأصغر. [ب] والثاني الخُلق الإنساني بالشجاعة فيه وهو الحد الأكبر. [ت] والثالث الهيئة البدنية وهو الحد الأوسط. [ث] والرابع الحيوان الآخر كالأسد وهو المشارك للأصغر في الحد الأوسط. وهذا القياس يشبه الدليل من حيث إن الأوسط فيه وهو عريض الصدر إذا حصل الحد الأصغر يتبعه وجود شيء آخر له كالشجاعة، ونسبة التمثيل أيضًا من حيث إن الحكم المدعى في جزئي كزيد لوجوده في جزئي آخر كالأسد.
Know that the syllogism of the sign and the syllogism of physiognomy are among the analogical syllogisms. The syllogism of physiognomy is that whose middle term is a bodily form found in the animal, and it is an explanation of that form that it follows a temperament that is followed by a character from among the characters of the soul, so that is the bodily form; and that soul-character is among the consequences of the temperament, which is a quality that occurs at the beginning of creation from the mixing of the elements with each other. When that bodily form is found in a human, for example, one surmises the existence of that human character for him, inferring by the existence of that form to the temperament, and by the existence of the temperament to that character, as it is said: Zayd is brave because he has a broad chest like a lion; for he has a broad chest and has bravery, so one infers from the broad chest to bravery because it is a consequence of a specific temperament that bravery follows. But it is not completed unless one concedes that the temperaments occurring at the beginning of creation and nature are followed by soul-characters just as they are followed by bodily forms. And one also concedes that the soul-characters that belong to that human by means of obtaining that bodily form and the soul are followed in confirming that by examining the animals that share with that animal in that bodily form: if that character is found in what has that bodily form, and is not found in what does not have such a form, then one surmises a strong surmise and a firm physiognomy that that character is a necessary concomitant of that form. And if it is not the case, he surmises that the necessary concomitant of that form is not this character but another character. If there is another character along with this character, such as for the lion—for while it is brave, generosity and nobility are also attributed to it—then one must first consider which of them follows that form. For if the first character is found in every individual in which that form exists, and the second character is not found in all, one knows that it is not due to that other form—that the tiger does not share with the lion in generosity but shares in the largeness of the high limbs, and yet shares in bravery, so the largeness of the high limbs is evidence for bravery without generosity. Therefore the middle term in physiognomy must be more general than the minor term and equal to the major term. The limits in this syllogism are four, as in analogical reasoning: [A] one is the human, like Zayd in the example that was mentioned—this is the minor limit. [B] The second is the human character of bravery in him—this is the major limit. [C] The third is the bodily form—this is the middle limit. [D] The fourth is the other animal, like the lion—this is the one that shares with the minor in the middle limit. This syllogism resembles a proof (dalil) in that the middle term—which is the broad chest—when it occurs in the minor term, the existence of something else like bravery follows; and it also has the aspect of analogy in that the judgment claimed for a particular like Zayd is because of its existence in another particular like the lion.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage defines the syllogism of physiognomy (qiyas al-firasa) as a type of analogical reasoning (tamthili). The author explains its structure: the middle term (al-hadd al-awsat) is a bodily form (hay'a badaniyya) that indicates a temperament (mizaj), which in turn indicates a soul-character (khuluq nafsani). For example, a broad chest indicates a certain temperament that is followed by bravery, as seen in a lion. The inference is not certain but a strong surmise (hads qawiyy). The author details the method: one must observe animals that share the bodily form and check if the character is consistently present or absent. If multiple characters are possible, one must determine which character actually follows the form by comparative observation. The passage then enumerates the four limits in this syllogism: the minor (e.g., Zayd), the major (bravery), the middle (broad chest), and the fourth term (the shared animal, e.g., lion). The author notes that this syllogism resembles a proof (dalil) because the middle term's presence leads to the major, and it is analogical because the judgment is transferred from one particular (lion) to another (Zayd).

الحد الأوسط
التمثيل
الخلق النفساني

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Four Terms in an Analogical Syllogism
Enumeration of the four terms in an analogical syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and label the four terms of an analogical syllogism and explain how the middle term enables the transfer of the judgment.

Core Idea
An analogical syllogism needs four terms: minor, major, middle, and the analogous case.

In Islamic logic, analogical reasoning (tamthīl) is structured as a syllogism with four distinct terms. While a categorical syllogism has three terms, the analogical form adds a fourth term representing the known case that serves as the basis for the analogy. This passage from the Mantiq Kabir enumerates these four terms using the example of inferring courage from a broad chest, like that of a lion. Understanding this structure is essential for correctly applying qiyas in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor term (al-ḥadd al-aṣghar)
The minor term is the subject of the conclusion. In the example, it is the human like Zayd. It is the entity about which we want to infer a property.
Also called the subject term.
2
Identify the major term (al-ḥadd al-akbar)
The major term is the attribute being inferred. Here it is 'courage' (al-shajā‘ah). It is the predicate of the conclusion.
Also called the predicate term.
3
Identify the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ)
The middle term is the shared characteristic that connects the minor and the fourth term. In the example, it is the bodily form of a broad chest.
The middle term is the basis for the analogy.
4
Identify the fourth term (al-ḥadd al-rābi‘)
The fourth term is the other animal (like a lion) that shares the middle term with the minor term. It is the known case from which the judgment is transferred.
This term is unique to analogical syllogisms; it does not appear in categorical syllogisms.
5
Trace the logical relationship
The presence of the middle term in the minor implies the major, because the same middle term in the fourth term already indicates the major. Thus, the judgment (courage) is transferred from the lion to Zayd due to their shared broad chest.
This shows how the syllogism resembles both a proof (dalīl) and an analogy (tamthīl).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحدود في هذا القياس أربعة كما في التمثيل : أ أحدهما الإنسان كزيد في المثال الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو الحد الأصغر. ب والثاني الخُلق الإنساني بالشجاعة فيه وهو الحد الأكبر. ت والثالث الهيئة البدنية وهو الحد الأوسط. ث والرابع الحيوان الآخر كالأسد وهو المشارك للأصغر في الحد الأوسط. وهذا القياس يشبه الدليل من حيث إن الأوسط فيه وهو عريض الصدر إذا حصل الحد الأصغر يتبعه وجود شيء آخر له كالشجاعة، ونسبة التمثيل أيضًا من حيث إن الحكم المدعى في جزئي كزيد لوجوده في جزئي آخر كالأسد.
The limits in this syllogism are four, as in the analogical [syllogism]: A) one of them is the human, like Zayd in the example that was mentioned, and it is the minor term. B) The second is the human disposition of courage in him, and it is the major term. C) The third is the bodily form, and it is the middle term. D) The fourth is the other animal, like the lion, which shares with the minor term in the middle term. This syllogism resembles the proof (dalil) in that the middle term in it, which is the broad chest, when the minor term is realized, the existence of something else like courage follows it. And it is also an analogical relation in that the judgment claimed concerning a particular like Zayd [is transferred] because of its existence in another particular like the lion.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage defines the four terms (hudud) that constitute an analogical syllogism (qiyas tamthil). The author first lists them using enumeration: the minor term (al-asghar) such as Zayd representing a human; the major term (al-akbar) which is the courage disposition; the middle term (al-awsat) which is the physical form like broad chest; and a fourth term (al-rabi') which is another animal like the lion that shares the middle term with the minor. The author then explains that this syllogism is like a proof (dalil) because the presence of the middle term in the minor implies the major, as broad chest indicates courage. It is also analogical because the judgment from the known case (the lion) is transferred to the unknown case (Zayd) due to their shared middle term.

Key Terms

الحد الأكبر
الحد الأوسط

The analogical syllogism in Islamic logic uniquely requires a fourth term that serves as the analogical source, distinguishing it from other syllogisms.

The Syllogism
