Predicates and Middle Terms in Demonstrative Syllogisms
Author classifies predicates in demonstrative syllogisms and conditions for seeking genus/differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify admissible predicates in demonstrative syllogisms and distinguish between existential (inniyya) and causal (limiyya) inquiries.

Core Idea
Demonstrative premises require predicates that are essential: genus, differentia, or essential accident.

This passage provides a detailed taxonomy of predicates permissible in demonstrative syllogisms. Understanding which predicates can serve as the major term is crucial for constructing valid demonstrations. We will explore the author's classification and its application to Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify admissible predicates
Locate where the author lists the types of predicates that can appear in demonstrative premises. He explicitly says only genera, differentiae, and essential accidents are allowed.
See the lines: 'لا يصلح أن يكون محمولاً في المقدمات البرهانية ... إلا الأجناس والفصول وما يشبهها.'
2
Understand exclusion of accidental accidents
The author emphasizes that predicates must be essential (dhati), not alien (ghariba). Accidental accidents are not suitable because they do not convey necessary properties.
He states: 'بعد أن تكون الأعراض ذاتية لا غريبة.'
3
Distinguish limiyya and inniyya
When the inquiry is for the cause ('why', limiyya), the middle term must be a cause for the major term. When only existence ('that', inniyya) is sought, the middle term can be an essential accident.
The passage: 'فإذا كان المطلوب هو اللمية دون الإنية فيصلح أن يجعل مقوم ما حدا أوسط.'
4
Apply to the soul example
The author gives the example of proving the soul is a substance. We know the soul only by its accidents (relation to body), so we use substance (a genus) as a middle term. This shows how predicates can be sought even when the subject is known only by accidents.
See: 'يطلب أن النفس جوهر أو ليس بجوهر؛ والجوهر جنس للنفس.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد الأوسط أو رسمه أوسط بين الأصغر وبين الأوسط لكن شيء منهما لا يكون قياسًا لأن القياس إنما يكون على خفي البيان والم. وأما مطلب أن هذا المحمول حد أو جنس أو فصل فهذا ما يجوز أن يكون مطلوبًا لأن كون الشيء طبيعة وكونه جنسًا أو فصلا مختلفان فإن الحساس من جهة ما هو حساس طبيعة ما، وبالقياس إلى الإنسان فصل جنسه فنسبة أن يكون إنما يشكل في مثل هذا أنه جنس للإنسان أو ليس بجنس، ولا يشكل أنه موجود للإنسان من جهة ما هو معنى ما من شأنه أن يكون جنسا أو فصل جنس إذا اعتبرناه من جهة أنه أعم؛ وقد بينه على دخول أمثال هذه المحمولات المقومة ببيان ما من ليس سليم الفطرة كما بينه على المبادئ الأولية. وأيضًا قد يبرهن على وجودها لشيء ما عرف بعوارضه دون حقيقته فعرف مثلا من جهة ما هو منسوب إلى شيء أو له فعل أو انفعال مثل ما يطلب أن النفس جوهر أو ليس بجوهر؛ والجوهر جنس للنفس. وإنما يجوز ذلك الأنا ما عرفنا النفس بذاتها وحقيقتها ولكن عرفناها من جهة ما هي مضافة إلى البدن وكمال له وتصدر عنها الأفاعيل الحيوانية. وكثيرًا ما يتفق هذا الطلب إذا لم يكن معنى الموضوع؛ والمطلوب ثبوته له محصلا لنا بل منهما عندنا اسم فقط كما يطلب هل الصورة جوهر أم غير جوهر، فإنا إذا عرفنا بالحقيقة ما الجوهر وعرفنا بالحقيقة ما الصورة عرفنا أن الصورة جوهر؛ ولم نحتج إلى وسط ولكن إذا كان عندنا من الصورة خيال أخذنا نحتج ونقيس فنثبت أن المقدمات قد توجد محمولات في المسائل على أحد هذه الوجوه التي مر ذكرها بل المسائل إذا كانت موضوعاتها من موضوع الصناعة كانت محمولاتها أعراضا ذاتية لها وأجناس أعراضها وفصول أعراضها وأعراض أعراضها وإن كانت موضوعاتها من أعراضها الذاتية جاز أن تكون محمولاتها من جنس الموضوع ومن أنواعه وفصوله وأعراضه وأعراض أعراضه وأجناس أعراضه وفصولها وما يجري مجراها بعد أن تكون الأعراض ذاتية لا غريبة. وقد تكون محمولات الصنفين اللذين ذكرناهما من الموضوعات عوارض ذاتية للجنس مثل المساواة في علم الهندسة والعدد، وعوارض ذاتية لما هو شبيه جنس كالمضادة في العلم الطبيعي فإن المضادة من عوارض الموجود الذي هو شبيه جنس. وإنما لا تكون المضادة محمولة في مسائل العلوم الرياضية لأن موضوعات العلم الرياضي إما غير متحركة وإما مشابهة للحركة لا مضادة فيها وإن لم تبق حركاتها من كل جهة. وأما موضوعات العلم الطبيعي فمنتهاه للتغيير بين الأضداد وهي الأجسام الطبيعية من حيث إنها متحركة وساكنة. فأما إذا كان المطلوب هو اللمية دون الإنية فيصلح أن يجعل مقوم ما حدا أوسط يبين به مقوم آخر إذا كان الأوسط علة لوجود الأكبر له أو يكون الأكبر أولا للأوسط؛ ولسببه يكون للأصغر كالمدرك فإنه يكون أولا للناطق والحساس ثم للإنسان. فأقول كل ما لم يصلح محمولًا في المسائل البرهانية فلا يصلح أن يكون محمولاً في المقدمات البرهانية البتة خاصة من المبادئ أو عامة إلا الأجناس والفصول وما يشبهها. فإنه يجوز أن تكون محمولة على أنواعها في المقدمات فإنه يمكن أن يكون الأكبر جنسًا للأوسط أو فصلا، والأوسط عرضًا ذاتيا للأصغر. وكما أن العرض يمكن أن يبتدأ فيطلب فكذلك يمكن أن يبتدأ فيطلب جنسه أو فصله، ويمكن أن يكون الأوسط جنسًا للأصغر أو فصلا، والأكبر عرضًا ذاتياً للأوسط: فمن هذه الجهة تدخل الأجناس والفصول في جملة المحمول. وأما أن يكون الأكبر مقوما للأصغر فليس يقع إلا على الوجه المحدود. ولا يقال لما كان من حق الجنس أن لا يحمل على النوع فكيف يعرف وجود النوع في الأصغر ولا يعرف وجود
The definition of the middle term or its description: it is middle between the minor term and the major term, but neither of them alone constitutes a syllogism because the syllogism is only for what is hidden in evidence and obscure. As for the inquiry whether this predicate is a definition, genus, or differentia, this is permissible to seek because a thing being a nature and being a genus or differentia are different: for 'sentient' insofar as it is sentient is a nature, and in relation to man it is a differentia of his genus. Its being such that it is a genus for man or not is questionable, but it is not questionable that it exists in man as a meaning that is liable to be a genus or differentia of a genus if we consider it from the aspect of being more general. And he has explained the inclusion of such constitutive predicates by a demonstration that is not for the sound of intellect just as he explained on the primary principles. Also, one can prove their existence for something known by its accidents without its reality: for example, something is known from the aspect of its relation to something or its action or passion, such as when one asks whether the soul is a substance or not; and substance is a genus for the soul. This is permitted because we did not know the soul by its essence and reality, but we knew it from the aspect that it is attached to the body and a perfection for it, and from it issue animal actions. This inquiry often occurs when the meaning of the subject and the predicate whose existence for it is sought is not obtained for us, but we have only a name for them, as when one asks whether form is a substance or not; then if we know in reality what substance is and know in reality what form is, we would know that form is substance, and we would not need a middle term. But if we have only an imagination of form, we resort to demonstation and syllogize and prove that premises can have predicates in problems in one of these ways that were mentioned. Indeed, the problems: if their subjects are from the subject of the science, their predicates are essential accidents of them, and genera of their accidents, and differentiae of their accidents, and accidents of their accidents. But if their subjects are from their essential accidents, it is permissible that their predicates be from the genus of the subject and its species and differentiae and accidents and accidents of its accidents and genera of its accidents and their differentiae and what runs their course, provided the accidents are essential, not alien. And the predicates of the two kinds that we mentioned from the subjects can be essential accidents of the genus, like equality in geometry and arithmetic, and essential accidents of what is like a genus, like opposition in natural science: for opposition is among the accidents of existence that is like a genus. Opposition is not a predicate in the problems of mathematical sciences because the subject of mathematical science is either unmoving or similar to motion, with no opposition in it even if its motions do not persist in every respect. As for the subjects of natural science, their end is change between opposites, and they are natural bodies insofar as they are moving and resting. But if what is sought is the 'why' without the 'that', then it is suitable to make a constitutive thing a middle term by which another constitutive thing is explained, if the middle term is a cause for the existence of the major term for it, or if the major term is primary for the middle term; and because of it it exists for the minor term, like the sense which is primary for the rational and sentient, and then for man. So I say: everything that is not suitable as a predicate in demonstrative problems is not at all suitable as a predicate in demonstrative premises, whether particular or universal, except genera and differentiae and what resembles them. For they can be predicated of their species in premises; it is possible that the major term be a genus or differentia of the middle term, and the middle term be an essential accident of the minor term. And just as an accident can be initially sought, so too can its genus or differentia be initially sought; and it is possible that the middle term be a genus or differentia of the minor term, and the major term be an essential accident of the middle term. From this aspect, genera and differentiae enter into the class of predicates. As for the major term being constitutive of the minor term, this only occurs in the limited way. And it is not said: since it is the right of the genus not to be predicated of the species, how then is the existence of the species in the minor known while the existence of the genus is not known?
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of predicates in demonstrative syllogisms, focusing on what types can serve as the major, middle, or minor term. The author begins by defining the middle term (al-awsat) as that which is between the minor and major terms, emphasizing that a syllogism requires such a middle term to explicate what is hidden. He then addresses the possibility of inquiring whether a predicate (mahmul) is a definition (hadd), genus (jins), or differentia (fasl). The author clarifies that being a nature (tabi'a) is different from being a genus or differentia; for example, 'sentient' (hassas) is a nature in itself but a differentia for man. The question of whether it is a genus is problematic, but its existence in man as a meaning that can be a genus is not questionable. The author notes that demonstration of such constitutive predicates is possible for those not naturally sound, just as primary principles are demonstrated. He then discusses cases where we prove existence for something known only by its accidents, like asking whether the soul is a substance. We know the soul only by its relation to the body and its actions, so we need a middle term (substance) to prove it. Similarly, if we know only the names of form and substance, we can syllogize to prove that form is substance. The author then classifies predicates in demonstrative problems: if the subject is from the subject of the science, predicates are essential accidents, genera of accidents, differentiae of accidents, and accidents of accidents. If the subject is an essential accident, predicates can be the genus, species, differentia, etc. Essential accidents must be non-alien. He gives examples: equality in geometry is an essential accident of the genus (magnitude), and opposition in natural science is an accident of existence (which is like a genus). Opposition is absent in mathematical sciences because their subjects are unmoving or only similar to motion. Natural science deals with change between opposites. Then the author distinguishes causal (limiyya) from existential (inniyya) inquiry. For 'why' questions, one can use a constitutive thing as a middle term if it is a cause, e.g., sense is primary for rational and sentient, then for man. Finally, he asserts that only genera, differentiae, and their likes are suitable as predicates in demonstrative premises. He allows the major term to be a genus or differentia of the middle term, and the middle term to be an essential accident of the minor, and vice versa. Genera and differentiae enter as predicates only in this limited way. The passage concludes with a rhetorical objection about how the genus can be known in the minor without being predicated, implying that the genus's existence is known through the middle term.

المحمول

Key Takeaway

Two Types of Transferring Demonstrations Across Sciences
Author explains two types of transferring demonstrations across sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of transfer of demonstration and identify which type is meant by 'naql al-burhan' in the scholarly tradition.

Core Idea
Transferring demonstration is not just borrowing a premise—it is using a middle term from another science.

This passage from a post-Avicennan logic text defines a key concept: how logical proofs can be moved from one science to another. You will encounter two distinct ways this happens: one where a premise is already accepted, and another where the entire proof's middle term originates in a different field. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating interdisciplinary arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types mentioned
Read the Arabic and translation to find the two kinds of transfer. The author numbers them: one and two.
The first is simpler; the second is more substantive.
2
Analyze the first type
In the first type, a proposition is taken from one science but its demonstration belongs to another. The first science accepts it on authority of the second.
This is like a lemma used without proof.
3
Analyze the second type
In the second type, a sought proposition in one science is demonstrated using a middle term from another science. The terms of the syllogism span both sciences.
This is the author's intended meaning.
4
Examine the examples
The author gives examples from optics and music. In optics, angles of the visual cone are proved with geometric estimations. In music, demonstrations rely on arithmetic.
These illustrate how sciences can share a subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في نقل البرهان من علم إلى علم وتناول الجزئيات تحت الكليات وكذلك تناول الحد إياها
نقول نقل البرهان على وجهين : [1] أحدهما أن يكون الشيء مأخوذا من مقدمة في علم ويكون برهانه في علم آخر فيتسلم في هذا العلم ويُنقل برهانه إلى ذلك العلم أي يحال به عليه. [ب] والثاني أن يكون الشيء مأخوذا في علم أنه مطلوب ثم يبرهن عليه ببرهان حده الأوسط من علم آخر فيكون أجزاء القياس وهي الحدود صالحة للوقوع في العلمين كما يبرهن على زوايا مخروط الخارج من البصر في علم المناظر بتقديرات هندسية على جهة لو جعلت معها تلك الزاوية هندسية محضة لكان البرهان عليها ذلك. وكذلك البراهين التي تقوم على الأعداد في علم التأليف والمراد من نقل البرهان هنا هو هذا القسم الثاني؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون أحد العلمين تحت الآخر أو يشتركان في الموضوع حتى يشتركا في إفادة: إما على الإطلاق وإما بوجه ما. وهذا الوجه يكون أحدهما تحت الآخر فيمكن أن ينقل البرهان من العام إلى الخاص فيكون العام وهو الذي يكون على الوجه الأول يعطى العلة الخاص على ما سنوضحه بعد. وأما إذا اشتركا في الموضوع على الوجه الثاني فيمكن أن يتفقا في القياس فإنه إذا كان الحد الأوسط جنســا للأصغر أو فصلا مقوما؛
Section: On the transfer of demonstration from one science to another and the application of particulars under universals, and likewise the application of definition to them.
We say: The transfer of demonstration is in two ways: [1] One is that a thing is taken from a premise in a science and its demonstration is in another science, so it is received in this science and its demonstration is transferred to that science, i.e., it is referred to it. [B] The second is that a thing is taken in a science as a sought proposition, then it is demonstrated by a demonstration whose middle term is from another science, so that the parts of the syllogism, which are the terms, are suitable to occur in both sciences, as is demonstrated concerning the angles of the cone outside the eye in the science of optics with geometric estimations, in such a way that if that angle were made purely geometric, the demonstration of it would be that. Likewise, the demonstrations that are based on numbers in the science of music. The intended meaning of 'transfer of demonstration' here is this second type. And that is not possible unless one of the two sciences is subordinate to the other or they share in the subject, so that they share in providing [the demonstration] either absolutely or in some respect. This type occurs when one is subordinate to the other, so it is possible to transfer the demonstration from the general to the specific, and the general, which is the first type, gives the cause of the specific, as we will clarify later. As for when they share in the subject in the second way, it is possible that they agree in the syllogism, for if the middle term is a genus for the minor term or a constitutive differentia...
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of transferring demonstrations between sciences. The author defines two types of transfer. The first type occurs when a premise is taken from one science, but its demonstration comes from another science; the premise is simply accepted in the first science and its proof is referred to the other. The second type is when a proposition is sought in one science but is proven using a middle term from another science, so that the terms of the syllogism belong to both sciences. The author gives an example from optics (ilm al-manazir) where the angles of a visual cone are demonstrated using geometric premises—if the angle were treated purely geometrically, the demonstration would be similar. Similarly, demonstrations in music (ilm al-ta'lif) rely on arithmetical premises. The intended sense of 'transfer of demonstration' is the second type. This transfer is possible only if one science is subordinate to the other or they share a subject, either absolutely or in some respect. When one is subordinate (the specific under the general), the demonstration can be transferred from the general to the specific, with the general science providing the cause of the specific. When they share a subject in the second way (same subject, different aspects), they can agree in the syllogism if the middle term is a genus or constitutive differentia of the minor term. The passage ends abruptly, suggesting further elaboration on the conditions.

Key Terms
نقل البرهان (naql al-burhan): Transfer of demonstration: using a proof from one science in another science. [Note: A technical concept in post-Avicennan logic; less common in earlier texts.]
حد أوسط (hadd awsat): Middle term of a syllogism, the cause of the conclusion. [Note: Standard usage.]
مطلوب (matlub): A sought proposition or conclusion to be proved. [Note: No significant shift.]
جزئي (juz'i): Particular; in this context, a specific case or lower science. [Note: Common.]
كلي (kulli): Universal; higher science or general principle. [Note: Common.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition is required for the second type of transfer of demonstration (where the middle term comes from another science)?
A) The two sciences must be completely unrelated
B) One science must be subordinate to the other, or they must share a subject
C) The sciences must be about the same object from the same aspect
D) The middle term must be from the same science as the major term

Key Takeaway
The intended meaning of 'transfer of demonstration' is the second type, where the middle term originates in another science, requiring subordination or shared subject.

Conditions for Transfer: Subordination and Shared Subject
Author explains two types of transferring demonstrations across sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a transfer of demonstration of the second type is possible by checking the relationship between the two sciences.

Core Idea
For a demonstration to transfer, the sciences must be ordered or share a subject.

Not every attempt to use a proof from another science is valid. The author lays down a strict condition: the two sciences must either be in a subordinate relation (one under the other) or share a common subject. This lesson teaches you how to evaluate whether a given interdisciplinary argument meets this condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition clause
Find the sentence beginning with 'وذلك لا يمكن إلا أن...' This states that the transfer is only possible under two conditions.
This is a necessary condition.
2
Understand the first condition: subordination
One science can be under another (kulli and juz'i). The general science provides the cause for the specific. For example, geometry is more general than optics.
The general science gives the 'why'.
3
Understand the second condition: shared subject
The two sciences may share a subject but consider different aspects. In that case, they can agree in the syllogism if the middle term is a genus or differentia of the minor term.
This is more complex; the middle term must be common.
4
Apply the conditions to examples
The optics-geometry example: optics and geometry share the subject of angles (geometry considers them abstractly, optics as visual). This is a shared subject case.
Check if the conditions hold in the music-arithmetic example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

نقول نقل البرهان على وجهين : [1] أحدهما أن يكون الشيء مأخوذا من مقدمة في علم ويكون برهانه في علم آخر فيتسلم في هذا العلم ويُنقل برهانه إلى ذلك العلم أي يحال به عليه. [ب] والثاني أن يكون الشيء مأخوذا في علم أنه مطلوب ثم يبرهن عليه ببرهان حده الأوسط من علم آخر فيكون أجزاء القياس وهي الحدود صالحة للوقوع في العلمين كما يبرهن على زوايا مخروط الخارج من البصر في علم المناظر بتقديرات هندسية على جهة لو جعلت معها تلك الزاوية هندسية محضة لكان البرهان عليها ذلك. وكذلك البراهين التي تقوم على الأعداد في علم التأليف والمراد من نقل البرهان هنا هو هذا القسم الثاني؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا وأن يكون أحد العلمين تحت الآخر أو يشتركان في الموضوع حتى يشتركا في إفادة: إما على الإطلاق وإما بوجه ما. وهذا الوجه يكون أحدهما تحت الآخر فيمكن أن ينقل البرهان من العام إلى الخاص فيكون العام وهو الذي يكون على الوجه الأول يعطى العلة الخاص على ما سنوضحه بعد. وأما إذا اشتركا في الموضوع على الوجه الثاني فيمكن أن يتفقا في القياس فإنه إذا كان الحد الأوسط جنســا للأصغر أو فصلا مقوما؛

The Four Causes as Middle Terms in Demonstration
Author enumerates the four causes and their role as middle terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes and explain how each can serve as a middle term in a demonstrative syllogism.

Core Idea
Causes are not just classifications; they are the middle terms that make demonstration yield certain knowledge.

In this passage, the author explains how the four causes—efficient, material, formal, final—function as middle terms in demonstrative syllogisms. Understanding this helps us see how Kalam arguments about creation and divine attributes are structured. We will parse the terms, analyze their applicability, and connect them to abstract sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the four causes
Find the Arabic terms for the four causes in the passage: al-ʿilla al-fāʿila (efficient), al-ʿilla al-mādiyya (material), al-ʿilla al-ṣūriyya (formal), al-ʿilla al-ghāʾiyya (final). Note the enumeration markers [أ], [ب], [ت], [ث].
The author uses 'principle of motion' for efficient, 'subject' for material, 'form' for formal, 'perfection' for final.
2
Match each cause to its definition
The author gives a brief characterization: efficient is the principle of motion; material is the subject underlying change; formal is the form or essence; final is the end or perfection for the sake of which something exists.
These definitions align with the Aristotelian framework but are adapted for logical demonstration.
3
Analyze the condition for all four causes
The author notes that it may happen that all four causes come together for one thing essentially. This applies to material entities where each cause plays a distinct role.
For example, a living being: the efficient cause is the parent, material cause is the body, formal cause is the soul, final cause is perfection.
4
Examine the case of separate intellects
For immaterial beings like the separate intellects, only the efficient and final causes apply, but the efficient cause is described differently—its relation is internal to the form. The material and motion are absent.
This shows that causes are not always the same in kind; their mode of being depends on the subject.
5
Connect to abstractive sciences
The author introduces ʿulūm intizāʿiyya (abstractive sciences) that investigate forms abstracted from matter. He distinguishes those that are essentially abstractive, studying pure forms.
Examples include metaphysics and logic, which deal with concepts independent of material instantiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم العلل كيف تكون حدودًا وسطى؛ ولو كانت حدودًا وسطى فكيف يكون يعطيه البرهان التام؟ فنقول العلل أربعة : [أ] مبدأ حركة أي الفاعل وما في جملته [ب] والموضوع وما في جملته، [ت] والصورة وما يجري مجراها، ث] والغاية وهي التمام الذي لأجله يكون ما يكون وإليه يسوق مبدأ الحركة وما يجري مجراها. وقد يتفق أن تجتمع هذه العلل كلها لشيء واحد بالذات، وربَّما كان للشيء ليس منها إلا الفاعلية والغاية كالعقول المفارقة. وإذا لم تكن للشيء مادة وحركة فإن الفاعل الذي يقال له إنه فاعل: فبنحو آخر يقال؛ وتكون نسبته إليه نسبة داخلة في صورته. وكذلك غايته فكل ما هو مجرد عن المادة فإنما يمكن أن يعطي من العلل ما هو صورته فقط وتسمى العلوم المختصّة بمثله علوما انتزاعية ؛ ومنها ما تكون انتزاعية بالذات كالعلوم الناظرة في
Then, how do causes become middle terms? And if they are middle terms, how does the complete demonstration give it? We say: The causes are four: [A] the principle of motion, i.e., the efficient cause and what is included with it; [B] the subject and what is included with it; [T] the form and what runs in its course; [TH] the end, which is the perfection for the sake of which that which comes to be comes to be, and toward which the principle of motion and what runs in its course drives. It may happen that all these causes come together for one thing essentially, and sometimes a thing has only the efficient and the end, like the separate intellects. If a thing has no matter and motion, then the agent that is said to be an agent is said in another way, and its relation to it is a relation entering into its form. Similarly its end, for everything that is abstracted from matter can only give from the causes what is its form alone, and the sciences specialized in such things are called sciences of abstraction; among them are those that are abstractive essentially, like the sciences that investigate
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the four causes (causes) and their role as middle terms (ḥudūd wusṭā) in demonstration. The author begins by asking how causes serve as middle terms and then enumerates them: (1) the efficient cause (principle of motion), (2) the material cause (the subject), (3) the formal cause (the form), and (4) the final cause (the end or perfection). He notes that sometimes all four causes apply to a single thing essentially, but for incorporeal beings like the separate intellects, only the efficient and final causes are present. For immaterial things, the agent and end are described differently—the agent's relation is internal to the form. Finally, he states that sciences dealing with abstract entities are called 'abstractive sciences' (ʿulūm intizāʿiyya), and some are essentially abstractive, like the sciences that investigate forms without matter.

First Principles as Implicit Logical Force
Author explains how first principles like excluded middle are implicitly used in syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how a first principle (e.g., excluded middle) is implicitly embedded in a categorical syllogism and explain its role in the logical force of the premises.

Core Idea
First principles are not premises but the hidden engine driving every syllogism.

This passage from the logic textbook tackles a subtle but crucial point: the most fundamental logical principles, like the law of excluded middle, are not stated as explicit premises in scientific demonstrations. Instead, they operate silently in the background, providing the logical force that connects the terms. Understanding this will sharpen your ability to analyze how arguments actually work in Islamic sciences like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first principle mentioned
Find the statement of the law of excluded middle in the Arabic: 'كل شيء إما أن تصدق عليه الموجبة وإما أن تصدق عليه السالبة'. Note that it is about every thing being either affirmatively or negatively true.
This principle is a first principle (mabda' awwal).
2
See how the author says it is not explicit
The author says it is not 'موضوع في العلوم وضعا بالفعل' (not actually posited in the sciences). It is placed 'بوجوه' (in ways). Understand that it is not a written premise but a presupposition.
This contrasts with explicit premises like 'Every human is mortal'.
3
Understand the 'force' in the relation
The author mentions 'قوة النسبة' (force of the relation) in the major premise. This force makes you believe that if the major is affirmative, the negative cannot be true, and vice versa. This belief is always held without explicit statement.
This implicit belief ensures the conclusion follows.
4
Apply to the given example
Analyze the example: 'Every human is an animal'. The author says this implicitly carries 'and not an animal' (i.e., not non-animal). Realize that the excluded middle principle ensures that the predicate either holds or does not, and the affirmative statement implicitly excludes the negative.
The principle applies to the subject side of the judgment, not the predicate side (the author clarifies this boundary).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في المبادئ والمسائل المناسبة وغير المناسبة المبادئ الواجب قبولها وخصوصا المبادئ الأولى مثل قولنا كل شيء إما أن تصدق عليه الموجبة وإما أن تصدق عليه السالبة. فإنه ليس يوضع في العلوم وضعا بالفعل بل إنما يوضع فيها على ما قيل في التعليم الأول بوجوه (۱): منها يجب أن يعتبر في تكميل التصديق بالمقدّمة الكبرى ليعتبر بمثله في النتيجة. وذلك بأن يعتقد أن الكبرى إن كانت موجبة فلا يمكن أن تصدق سالبة، وإن كانت سالبة فلا يمكن أن تصدق موجبة لتكون النتيجة بهذه الصفة؛ فهذا الاعتقاد يعتقد دائمًا لأنه إذا كان موجباً فليس بسالب، وإذا كان سالبًا فليس بموجب البتة والسلب والإيجاب لا يجتمعان البتة فكل شيء يصدق فيه أحدهما فلا يحتاج إلى التصريح به؛ وإنما تكون هذه القوة في نسبة الأوسط إلى الأكبر في الكبرى أو الأصغر إلى الأكبر في النتيجة من غير عكس. فإنك إذا قلت في الأكبر مثلا فكل إنسان حيوان فقد أضمرت وليس بحيوان. وكذلك في النتيجة إذا قلت إن الكاتب حيوان أضمرت وليس بحيوان. وبالجملة ما جعل موضوعًا لحكم فليس موضوعًا لما يقابله. وأما من جهة المحمول فلا يستمر هذا حتى يكون للحيوان في القياس محمولا على الإنسان؛ وليس محمولاً
Chapter: On principles and appropriate and inappropriate propositions. The principles that must be accepted, especially the first principles, such as our statement: 'Everything is either affirmed by an affirmative proposition or affirmed by a negative proposition.' For it is not actually posited in the sciences as a position, but rather it is placed in them, as was said in the Prior Analytics, in ways (1): Among them, it must be considered in completing the assent with the major premise so that it is considered similarly in the conclusion. That is by believing that if the major is affirmative, then a negative cannot be true, and if it is negative, then an affirmative cannot be true, so that the conclusion has this character. This belief is always held because if it is affirmative, it is not negative; if it is negative, it is absolutely not affirmative. Negation and affirmation never coexist, so everything in which one of them is true does not need explicit statement. This force is only in the relation of the middle to the major in the major premise, or the minor to the major in the conclusion, without reversal. For if you say in the major, for example, 'Every human is an animal,' you have implicitly implied 'and not an animal.' Similarly in the conclusion if you say 'The writer is an animal,' you have implied 'and not an animal.' In sum, whatever is made a subject for a judgment is not a subject for its opposite. As for the predicate side, this does not hold such that 'animal' in the syllogism is predicated of 'human' and is not predicated.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage is a syllogistic analysis of how first principles, specifically the law of excluded middle, function in scientific demonstrations. The author begins a new chapter on principles and appropriate/inappropriate propositions. He identifies the law of excluded middle as a fundamental principle: everything is either affirmed or denied by a proposition. This principle is not explicitly stated in the sciences as a premise; rather, it is implicitly embedded in the logical structure. The author explains, referring to Aristotle's Prior Analytics, that this principle is considered in the major premise to ensure the conclusion follows. One must believe that if the major premise is affirmative, the corresponding negative cannot be true, and vice versa. This belief is constant: an affirmative is not negative, and a negative is not affirmative; they never coexist. Therefore, when we assert a proposition, we need not also assert its opposite. This implicit force is present in the relation of the middle term to the major term in the major premise, or the minor term to the major term in the conclusion, without the need for conversion. For example, saying 'Every human is an animal' implicitly carries the implication 'and not an animal' (i.e., not non-animal). Similarly, 'The writer is an animal' implies 'not an animal'. In summary, the subject of a judgment is not also subject to its contradictory opposite. However, this principle does not apply to the predicate side, as when 'animal' is predicated of 'human', it is not also not-predicated.

Key Terms
مبادئ أولى
التعليم الأول
قوة النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the role of the first principle (e.g., excluded middle) in syllogisms?
A) It is explicitly stated as a major premise
B) It is implicitly believed and embedded in the relation of terms
C) It is only used in the conclusion
D) It applies to both subject and predicate parts equally

Key Takeaway
First principles like excluded middle are not explicit premises but the necessary logical force that makes syllogistic reasoning valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Why Mathematical Sciences Avoid Error
Author explains why mathematical sciences avoid error while dialectic is prone to it due to term ambiguity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two causes of jahl mudadd and why mathematical sciences are immune from the first cause while dialectical sciences are prone to it.

Core Idea
Mathematical certainty comes from precise definitions and imaginal support, not from superior intellect.

In classical Islamic logic, the problem of error in reasoning is traced to two main sources: ambiguous terms and faulty syllogistic forms. This passage contrasts the mathematical sciences, where terms are well-defined and imagination aids understanding, with dialectical sciences, where ambiguity reigns. Understanding this contrast helps you see why some disciplines yield certainty while others remain disputable.

Guided Path
1
Define jahl mudadd (ignorance opposed to knowledge)
Recognize that it is a false belief contradicting the truth, not mere absence of knowledge. The author states it occurs when you believe and see its form opposed to the form of knowledge.
2
List the two causes of this ignorance
Identify the two causes: (1) disparity in the concept of syllogism limits due to homonymy (especially the middle term), and (2) faulty composition (e.g., two affirmatives in the second figure).
3
Examine why mathematics avoids the first cause
Note that mathematical terms are known by acquisition, have clear meanings, and are close to imagination—so the middle term is clear and produces necessary conclusions. The middle term is 'doubled' (taken twice with known causes).
4
Contrast with dialectic
See that dialectical terms are often ambiguous, the intellectual meaning is fleeting, and the imagination may lead astray. Thus, dialectic suffers from the first cause of ignorance.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٥.٢٠٥. ] فصل: في اختلاف العلوم الرياضية وغيرها مع الجدل وفي أن الرياضية بعيدة عن الغلط بخلاف غيرها وفي بيان اختلاف ما مر في التحليل والتركيب. إن الجهل المضاد للعلم وهو الذي ليس إنما بعدم مع العلم بل أن تعتقد وترى صورته مضادة لصورة العلم كما يقع في الوجه الثاني من وجهي اللا علمي واللا هندسي فلما يقع في التعاليم؛ وذلك لأن هذا الجهل إنما يقع لأمور وأظهرها :أمران أحدهما التباين في مفهوم حدود القياس لاشتراك الاسم وخصوصًا الأوسط. والثاني حال التأليف فإنه قد يكون منتجا، وقد لا يكون كما إذا كان من الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني مثلا. [1] وأما القسم الأول فإنه مما لا يقع في التعليميات فإن الألفاظ المستعملة في الهندسيات معلومة بالتحصيل فلكل لفظ منها معنى مفهوم بحسب الغرض أو بحسب ما سبق من التحديد. ثمَّ المعاني لتلك الألفاظ قريبة من الخيال فكما يفهم في العقل معنّى كذلك يقوم له في الوهم خيال؛ فيُثبت خياله حقيقة ذلك المعنى ويحفظه. فحينئذ يكون الحد الأوسط مضاعفًا أي واحد يوخذ مرتين بعلتين معلومتين فينتج ضرورة. وأما في العلوم الأخرى وخصوصا في الجدل فلا يكون هذا المعاون بل تكون ألفاظها في أكثر الأمر مشتركة. والمعنى العقلي عابر في النفس غير ملائم ثمَّ لذلك المعنى بل ربما كان الخيال اللائح منه في (۲۹۹) الذهن مناسبا لمعنى. والغرض بمعنى آخر فيزيغ الذهن / غير الغرض إلى الخيال، والخيال فيما سوى التعليمات في أكثر الأمر فضل، وفي التعليمات مزيد. ولذلك ما صارت المسائل الرياضية يصعب تعليمها إلا بأشكال محسوسة معلمة بالحروف ليكون ذلك معونة للخيال. وأما في العلوم الأخرى وخصوصا في الجدل فليس كذلك فإن معنى اللفظ المستعمل فيه قد يكون واحدًا بحسب الشهرة لا بحسب الحقيقة. فربَّما كان بحسب الحقيقة مشتركًا
Chapter: On the difference between mathematical sciences and others with dialectic, and that the mathematical sciences are far from error unlike others, and on explaining the difference of what has passed in analysis and synthesis. Indeed, ignorance opposed to knowledge is not merely absence with knowledge, but rather that you believe and see its form opposed to the form of knowledge, as occurs in the second of the two aspects of the non-scientific and the non-geometric—so it occurs in the mathematical sciences. That is because this ignorance occurs due to matters, the clearest of which are two: one is disparity in the concept of the limits of the syllogism due to homonymy, especially the middle [term]; the second is the state of composition, for it may be productive or not, as when it is from two affirmatives in the second figure, for example. As for the first kind, it does not occur in the mathematical sciences, because the terms used in geometry are known by acquisition, so each term has a meaning understood according to the purpose or according to what preceded by definition. Then the meanings of those terms are close to imagination, so just as a meaning is understood in the intellect, likewise an image stands for it in the estimation; so its image establishes the reality of that meaning and preserves it. Then the middle term becomes doubled—that is, one taken twice with two known causes—so it produces necessarily. But in other sciences, especially dialectic, this assistance does not occur; rather, their terms are for the most part ambiguous. And the intellectual meaning passes through the soul without settling, then for that meaning perhaps the image appearing from it in the mind is suitable for a meaning, and the purpose with another meaning, so the mind deviates from the purpose to the image. And the image in things other than the mathematical sciences is for the most part excess, while in the mathematical sciences it is an increase. Therefore, mathematical problems become difficult to teach except through sensible figures marked with letters, so that this may be an aid to the imagination. But in other sciences, especially dialectic, it is not so, for the meaning of the term used therein may be one according to notoriety, not according to reality, so perhaps it is in reality homonymous.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage explains why mathematical sciences are immune from error, unlike dialectical sciences. The author begins by defining ignorance opposed to knowledge as a false belief that contradicts the truth, and he states that such ignorance occurs in mathematics due to two main causes: (1) ambiguity in the concept of the terms of the syllogism, especially the middle term, due to homonymy, and (2) faulty composition, e.g., two affirmatives in the second figure which does not yield a valid conclusion. However, the first cause does not affect mathematics because its terms are precisely defined (by 'acquisition' or prior definition) and their meanings are closely tied to mental images, so the middle term is clear and produces necessary conclusions. In contrast, dialectical sciences suffer from ambiguous terms that confuse the intellect and lead to error. The author further notes that mathematical education relies on visible diagrams with letters to aid the imagination, whereas dialectic lacks such aids, leaving terms ambiguous and often merely conventional.

الحد الأوسط

D) Because mathematical conclusions are empirical.

Why Two Affirmatives in the Second Figure Fail
Author explains error from invalid syllogistic form in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a categorical syllogism is invalid due to figure and mood constraints, specifically two affirmatives in the second figure.

Core Idea
Not all premise combinations yield a conclusion; the second figure with two affirmatives is a classic invalid form.

In this passage, the author contrasts the reliable mathematical sciences with dialectic, where invalid syllogisms often slip in. The key example is using two affirmative premises in the second figure, which appears convincing but is logically invalid. Understanding this principle helps you spot flawed arguments in Islamic disciplines like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction between mathematical and dialectical sciences
The author states that mathematical sciences mostly use the first figure and its first mood, which are always valid. Dialectic, however, often uses invalid syllogisms inadvertently.
This sets the context for why error occurs.
2
Recognize the structure of the second figure
In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. For a valid conclusion, one premise must be negative. Two affirmatives yield no necessary conclusion.
The condition is that one premise must be negative.
3
Examine the example given
The author provides: 'Fire is quick in generation; whatever multiplies greatly is quick in generation; therefore fire multiplies greatly.' This is two affirmatives in the second figure.
The middle term is 'quick in generation'.
4
Determine why it fails
Because the middle term is not distributed, the premises do not force the conclusion. The argument is invalid despite seeming plausible.
In dialectic, this deceptive appearance leads to error.
5
Generalize the rule
For any syllogism, check the figure and mood. In the second figure, if both premises are affirmative, the argument is invalid.
This applies to all categorical syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنرجع إلى بيان القسم الثاني من وجوه الغلط الواقع في العلوم دون التعليمات فنقول إن العلوم الرياضية إنما يستعمل منها في أكثر الأمر الشكل الأول ومن ضروبه الضرب الأول. وربما يستعمل الضرب الثاني فلا يقع فيه المغالطة بجهة التأليف إلا نادرا. وأما الجدل فكثيرا ما تستعمل فيه قياسات غير منتجة سهوا وانخداعا، ويستعمل فيه من الأمور الحقيقية والظنية. وكثيرا ما يستعمل فيه من الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني كما يقال إن النار كثيرة الأضعاف في النسبة؛ وذلك لأن النار سريعة التولد والتزايد، وكثير الأضعاف في النسبة سريع التولد والتزيد فينتج أن النار كثيرة الأضعاف في النسبة: فإنه لا يكون منتجا في الحقيقة وإن كان يعد أنه منتج في الظاهر . ثم الجدل والتعاليم يتخالفان غاية التخالف في التحليل بالعكس؛ وذلك لأن التعاليم توخذ محمولات مسائلها من الحدود وما يلزم من العوارض بسبب الحدود وهي العوارض التي تعرض للأشياء بذاتها، ومن جهة ما هي هي من حيث لها
Let us return to the explanation of the second division of the aspects of error occurring in sciences other than the mathematical sciences. We say: The mathematical sciences mostly use the first figure and from its moods the first mood. And perhaps the second mood is used, so fallacy does not occur in it by way of composition except rarely. As for dialectic, often invalid syllogisms are used in it inadvertently and deceptively, and it uses real and probable matters. And often two affirmatives in the second figure are used in it, as it is said: 'Fire is that which multiplies greatly in proportion; because fire is quick in generation and increase, and that which multiplies greatly in proportion is quick in generation and increase; therefore fire multiplies greatly in proportion.' For it is not productive in reality, although it is considered productive in appearance. Then dialectic and mathematical sciences are extremely different in analysis in opposite ways; because the mathematical sciences take the predicates of their propositions from definitions and from the necessary concomitants due to the definitions, which are the accidents that belong to things essentially, and from the perspective that they are what they are.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage addresses the second cause of error: invalid syllogistic composition. The author contrasts mathematical sciences, which predominantly use the valid first figure (especially the first mood), with dialectic, which often employs invalid syllogisms, such as two affirmative premises in the second figure. He provides an example: 'Fire is that which multiplies greatly in proportion' — the reasoning uses two affirmatives in the second figure, which is invalid, though it appears productive. The author then notes that mathematical and dialectical sciences differ fundamentally in analysis: mathematical analysis derives predicates from definitions and essential accidents, while dialectical analysis does not. This guards against the misunderstanding that any combination of premises is valid; specific figures and moods are required.

Mathematical vs Dialectical Analysis: Source of Error
Author explains difference in analysis between mathematical and dialectical sciences as a source of error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the methods of analysis in mathematical sciences and dialectic, and explain how this difference makes dialectic prone to invalid syllogistic composition.

Core Idea
Error in dialectic stems from using probable premises without the rigorous analysis of definitions characteristic of mathematical sciences.

The passage not only identifies invalid syllogistic forms but also traces the source of error to the difference in analytical method. Mathematical sciences analyze from definitions and essential accidents, ensuring valid predicates. Dialectic, using probable premises and less rigorous analysis, is prone to invalid composition. Understanding this helps evaluate the strength of arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the author's statement about the difference in analysis
The author says 'dialectic and mathematical sciences are extremely different in analysis in opposite ways' (التحليل بالعكس). This is the key point.
The phrase 'بالعكس' indicates a fundamental opposition.
2
Understand mathematical analysis
Mathematical analysis takes predicates from definitions (حدود) and necessary concomitants (عوارض ذاتية). This gives certainty and ensures the premises are true and essential.
This makes the syllogisms in mathematics valid both in form and matter.
3
Understand dialectical analysis
Dialectic uses 'real and probable matters' (الأمور الحقيقية والظنية). It does not rely on strict definitions but on widely accepted or plausible premises.
This leads to looser reasoning and susceptibility to invalid forms.
4
Connect analysis to the invalid syllogism example
The example of two affirmatives in the second figure is more likely to occur in dialectic because dialecticians are not as rigorous in checking the logical form or the derivation of predicates.
The error is both in form (invalid figure) and in matter (probable premises).
5
Reflect on implications for Islamic sciences
In Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh, arguments should ideally imitate mathematical analysis by relying on clear definitions and essential attributes to avoid error.
This doesn't mean all arguments must be demonstrative, but awareness of the weakness of dialectical arguments is crucial.

The Second-Figure Fallacy of Two Affirmatives
Author demonstrates a common second-figure fallacy in dialectic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain why a second-figure categorical syllogism with two affirmative premises is invalid.

Core Idea
Two affirmatives in the second figure never yield a valid conclusion.

In this passage, the author contrasts the reliability of syllogisms in mathematical sciences with the deceptive ease of fallacious reasoning in dialectic. He provides a concrete example of a second-figure syllogism that seems convincing but is logically unsound. Understanding this fallacy sharpens your ability to evaluate arguments in Islamic scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
Examine the example: the premises are 'Fire is quick to generate and increase' (S is M) and 'That which is many times greater is quick to generate and increase' (P is M). The conclusion is 'Fire is many times greater' (S is P). The middle term (M) is 'quick to generate/increase', which is predicate in both premises — that is the second figure.
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises.
2
Check distribution of the middle term
In affirmative propositions, the predicate is not distributed. Since both premises are affirmative, the middle term (predicate of both) is not distributed in either premise. For a valid categorical syllogism, the middle term must be distributed at least once.
Distribution rule: every valid syllogism requires the middle term to be distributed in at least one premise.
3
Determine validity
Because the middle term is undistributed, no necessary connection between the major and minor terms can be established. The syllogism is invalid — the conclusion does not follow necessarily, even if the premises are true.
The author says it is 'not actually productive' (لا يكون منتجا في الحقيقة).
4
Recognize the dialectical context
The author notes that such fallacies are rare in mathematical sciences but common in dialectic (جدل). In debate, participants may inadvertently or deceptively use such invalid forms, relying on the apparent plausibility.
This highlights the importance of logical training to avoid being misled in theological and legal arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنرجع إلى بيان القسم الثاني من وجوه الغلط الواقع في العلوم دون التعليمات فنقول إن العلوم الرياضية إنما يستعمل منها في أكثر الأمر الشكل الأول ومن ضروبه الضرب الأول. وربَّما يستعمل الضرب الثاني فلا يقع فيه المغالطة بجهة التأليف إلا نادرًا. وأما الجدل فكثيرًا ما تستعمل فيه قياسات غير منتجة سهوا وانخداعا، ويستعمل فيه من الأمور الحقيقية والظنية. وكثيرًا ما يستعمل فيه من الموجبتين في الشكل الثاني كما يقال إن النار كثيرة الأضعاف في النسبة؛ وذلك لأن النار سريعة التولد والتزايد، وكثير الأضعاف في النسبة سريع التولد و التزيد فينتج أن النار كثيرة الأضعاف في النسبة: فإنه لا يكون منتجا في الحقيقة وإن كان يعد أنه منتج في الظاهر.
Let us return to explaining the second kind of error that occurs in the sciences as opposed to the instructive [sciences]. We say that the mathematical sciences mostly use the first figure and among its moods the first mood. Occasionally the second mood is used, and fallacy in it due to composition rarely occurs. As for dialectic, unproductive syllogisms are often used inadvertently and deceptively, and both certain and probable matters are used in it. Frequently, two affirmative [premises] in the second figure are used, as when it is said: 'Fire is many times greater in proportion; because fire is quick to generate and increase, and that which is many times greater in proportion is quick to generate and increase'; so it is concluded that fire is many times greater in proportion. But it is not actually productive, even though it is considered to be apparently productive.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between errors in demonstrative sciences (تعاليم) and those in dialectic (جدل). In mathematical sciences, the first figure first mood is mostly used, and compositional fallacies are rare. In dialectic, however, unsound syllogisms are common. The author gives an example of a second-figure syllogism with two affirmative premises: 'Fire is quick to generate/increase. That which is many times greater is quick to generate/increase. Therefore, fire is many times greater.' This syllogism is in the second figure (both premises affirmative) and is invalid. The author says it is not actually productive (لا يكون منتجا في الحقيقة) even though it seems so. The fallacy is that the middle term ('quick to generate/increase') is not distributed in either premise, so no necessary conclusion follows.

C) The major premise is missing
D) The conclusion contradicts common sense

Key Takeaway
In the second figure, two affirmative premises always fail to produce a valid conclusion because the middle term is never distributed.

Second Aspect of Burhan Inni: Effects and Signs as Middle Terms
Author explains the second face of demonstration where the middle term is an effect or sign.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the second aspect of burhan inni from the first, and identify whether the middle term is a cause, effect, or sign.

Core Idea
Burhan inni proves that a thing is by using an effect or sign, not the cause, as the middle term.

In demonstration, we often seek to prove the cause from its effect. This lesson explores the second face of demonstration where the middle term is not a cause but an effect or a convertible sign. The author explains that such terms can serve as the middle when they are better known than the cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major term (cause) and the middle term
In a burhan inni syllogism, the major term is the cause, and the middle term is the effect or sign. Find these two in the syllogism.
For example, in 'planets are non-twinkling; non-twinkling things are near; therefore planets are near', the major is 'near', the middle is 'non-twinkling'.
2
Determine if the middle term is an effect or a sign
Check whether the middle term is caused by the major (effect) or merely indicates it (sign). The passage gives examples: twinkling is an effect of distance; increase of moon's light is an effect of sphericity; halo is a sign of rain.
Effects are causally linked; signs may be merely correlated.
3
Check convertibility (in‘ikās)
An effect or sign that is convertible (mun‘akis) can be used as a middle term because it allows inference from middle to major and vice versa. The author notes that effects are often convertible, but not always.
Convertibility means the two terms are co-extensive in existence.
4
Verify which term is better known (a‘raf)
The middle term must be better known than the major term relative to the minor subject. If the effect is more familiar, it should be the middle; if the cause is more familiar, it should be the middle (as in burhan limmi).
This ensures the demonstration proceeds from what is more evident to what is less evident.

وأما الوجه الثاني فإن لا يكون قد أعطى في كل قياس منها علة لا قريبة ولا بعيدة ولكن أعطى في أحدهما ما ليس بعلة أصلا. فإنه قد يمكن أن يكون ما ليس بعلة متعكّسًا على الحد الآخر من المقدمة سواء كان ما ليس بعلة معلولا للآخر كلمع الكوكب الذي هو معلول لبعدها؛ وإنه مما ينعكس على العلة وهي بعدها. ومثل هيئة تزيد ضوء القمر الذي هو معلول كريته ; وإنه مما ينعكس على العلة أيضًا : وهي كريته. أو كان ما ليس معلولاً ولا علة مثل دلالة ثبات الهالة على سموم المطر عن السحاب الذي فيه الهالة فإنه إذا كان يمكن أن يكون معلول منعكس وعلامة منعكسة. وإن لم يجب ذلك فربَّما لم يكن المعلول منعكسا بل كان أعم مثل إضاءة البيت بالاصطباح، أو كان أخص مثل التدخن عن النار. وكذلك العلامة على ما علمت فظهر أنه يمكن أن يبين بالمعلول العلة وبالعلامة ذو العلامة ; ويمكن أن يبين بالعكس. وإنما يتوقف الأمر على الأعرف وإن كان الأعرف شبه المعلول أو العلامة إلى الحد الأصغر كان هو الأولى أن يجعل حدا أوسط، والعلة حدا أكبر. فكان ذلك وجها من وجهي هذا البرهان مثل قولك إن الكواكب المتحيرة مضيئة غير لامعة، وكل مضيء غير لامع فهو قريب فالكواكب المتحيرة قريبة. وأيضًا الكواكب الثابتة مضيئة لامعة، وكل مضيء لامع فهو بعيد فالكواكب الثابتة بعيدة. ثم كل واحد من اللمع وسلبه علة ومعلول ذلك للبعد، وهذا للقرب. وكذلك قولك القمر يتزيد ضوؤه كذا وكذا وكل ما يتزيد ضوؤه كذا وكذا فهو كري فالقمر كري: فهذا أيضًا الحد الأوسط فيه معلول للأكبر. فهذا أمثلة الضرب الثاني من برهان إن.
As for the second aspect, it is that he has not given in every syllogism thereof a cause, neither near nor far, but gave in one of them what is not a cause at all. For it may be that what is not a cause is convertible with the other term of the premise, whether what is not a cause is an effect of the other, like the twinkling of a star which is an effect of its distance; and it is that which is converted upon the cause, which is its distance. And like the state of the increase of the moon's light, which is an effect of its sphericity; and it is that which is converted upon the cause as well: its sphericity. Or it may be neither an effect nor a cause, like the indication of the stability of the halo concerning the ... of the rain from the cloud in which the halo is, for if it is possible that it be a convertible effect and a convertible sign. And if that is not necessary, perhaps the effect is not convertible but is more general, like the lighting of the house by the lamp, or more specific, like smoke from fire. And likewise the sign, as you know. So it appears that it is possible to demonstrate the cause by the effect, and the thing of the sign by the sign; and it is possible to demonstrate by the converse. And the matter depends only on what is better known. And if the better known, whether it is an effect or a sign, is with respect to the minor term, then it is more appropriate to make it a middle term, and the cause a major term. So that is one of the two faces of this demonstration, like your saying: The wandering stars (planets) are luminous, non-twinkling; and everything luminous and non-twinkling is near; so the wandering stars are near. And also: The fixed stars are luminous, twinkling; and everything luminous and twinkling is far; so the fixed stars are far. Then each of twinkling and its negation is a cause and an effect of that, for distance and for nearness. And likewise your saying: The moon increases its light in such and such a manner; and everything that increases its light in such and such a manner is spherical; so the moon is spherical: this also has the middle term as an effect of the major. So these are examples of the second type of the demonstration of 'that'.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the second aspect (wajh) of demonstration—specifically the 'demonstration of that' (burhan inni). Unlike the first aspect where a cause (illa) is given as the middle term, here the middle term is not a cause at all. It may be an effect (ma'lul) of the major term, or a sign (alama) that is neither cause nor effect but is convertible. The author explains that an effect can be convertible with its cause: e.g., the twinkling of a star is an effect of its distance, and the increase of the moon's light is an effect of its sphericity. These effects can be used as middle terms to prove the cause. Alternatively, a sign that is not an effect (like a halo indicating rain) may also be convertible. The key principle is that the middle term must be better known (a'raf) than the major term relative to the minor. If the effect or sign is better known, it should be taken as the middle term, and the cause as the major. The author provides concrete syllogistic examples: planets are non-twinkling, and everything non-twinkling is near, so planets are near; fixed stars are twinkling, so they are far; the moon's light increases, so it is spherical. In each case, the middle term (non-twinkling, twinkling, light increase) is the effect, and the major term (nearness, farness, sphericity) is the cause. These are examples of the second type of burhan inni (demonstration of that).

علة
معلول

Checkpoint
In the author's explanation, what is the condition that determines whether an effect or sign can serve as the middle term in a burhan inni syllogism?
A) The effect must be the cause of the major term.
B) The middle term must be convertible and better known than the major term.
C) The sign must be more general than the cause.
D) The effect must be more specific than the cause.

Key Takeaway
The second aspect of burhan inni uses effects or signs as middle terms, provided they are convertible and better known than the cause.

?
Identify the middle term in the following demonstration: 'The world is composed of parts; everything composed of parts requires a maker; therefore the world requires a maker.' Is it a cause, effect, or sign?

The Syllogism
beginner

Analyzing the Examples of Burhan Inni Syllogisms
Author provides concrete syllogistic examples of the second type of burhan inni.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formalize a burhan inni syllogism from a given example and verify its validity.

Core Idea
In burhan inni syllogisms, the effect is the middle term, the cause is the major, and the subject is the minor.

The author provides three concrete syllogistic examples to illustrate the second type of burhan inni. By analyzing these examples, we can see how effects serve as middle terms and how the syllogism is structured.

Guided Path
1
List the three terms for each example
For the moon example: minor term (subject) = 'the moon', middle term = 'increase of light in a certain manner', major term = 'spherical' (the cause). For the planets example: minor = 'planets', middle = 'luminous non-twinkling', major = 'near'. For fixed stars: minor = 'fixed stars', middle = 'luminous twinkling', major = 'far'.
The middle term is the effect; the major is the cause.
2
Write the premises in standard form
For the moon: Minor premise: 'The moon increases its light in such a manner.' Major premise: 'Everything that increases its light in such a manner is spherical.' The middle term appears in both premises.
Ensure the middle term is the subject of the major premise and the predicate of the minor premise (first figure).
3
Check that the middle term is the effect
In the moon example, the increase of light is an effect (ma'lul) of sphericity. The author explicitly states this. Confirm that the relationship is causal and that the effect is better known.
If the middle term were not an effect, it might still be a sign; but in these examples, they are effects.
4
Verify the syllogism's validity
All examples are in the first figure (Barbara). Check that the premises are true and that the conclusion follows necessarily. The conclusion must be a statement about the minor term having the major term.
The validity does not depend on the truth of premises, but here the premises are taken as evident.

Using Cause as Middle Term and Avoiding Circularity
Author explains using cause as middle term and avoiding circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish burhan limmi from burhan inni and explain why sequential inn-then-lim demonstrations do not constitute circular reasoning or petitio principii.

Core Idea
Sequential inn-then-lim demonstrations are not circular because each has a distinct epistemic goal: 'that' and 'why'.

In this passage, the author extends the discussion of demonstration by considering cases where the cause is better known than the effect. He shows how to construct a burhan limmi using the cause as middle term, and then addresses a potential objection: if you first know the 'that' via the effect and later know the 'why' via the cause, is that not circular? The author argues it is not, because the two demonstrations have different aims. This lesson will help you understand the interplay between burhan inni and burhan limmi and the conditions under which they are epistemically sound.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two demonstrations in the astronomical example
The passage gives two demonstrations: (1) Observational science establishes that the moon is spherical (the 'that', burhan inni). (2) Natural science demonstrates that all celestial bodies are spherical (the 'why', burhan limmi). Note that the first uses effects (light) as middle term, the second uses the cause (nature of celestial bodies).
This example illustrates that the same fact (moon's sphericity) can be known both ways without contradiction.
2
Understand why the sequence is not circular
The author argues: the first demonstration does not seek the 'why' at all; it only establishes 'that' it happens. The second does not seek the 'that' at all; it only explains 'why'. Since each addresses a different question, there is no logical circle. The term 'petitio principii' (musadara 'ala al-matlub) would apply if the conclusion of one was assumed in the other, but here they are independent.
Circularity would require the second demonstration to rely on the first's conclusion as a premise for the same question.
3
Distinguish between middle terms: cause vs. concomitant
The passage later explains that if the middle term is a cause, the demonstration gives both 'why' and 'that' (burhan limmi inni simultaneously). If it is an effect or concomitant (lazim), the demonstration gives only 'that' (burhan inni). This distinction is crucial for choosing the right type of demonstration.
For example, using 'sphericity' as middle term (cause) gives both; using 'receiving light' (effect) gives only 'that'.
4
Apply the principle to a new case
Consider a fiqh example: you know that nabidh is forbidden (that) because it intoxicates, but you only later learn the wisdom (hukm) behind the prohibition. This is not circular: the first knowledge is from transmitted evidence, the second from causal reasoning.
The two demonstrations use different sources and answer different questions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كانت هذه الحدود الكبرى أعرف من هذه الحدود الوسطى فكان القرب والبعد للمتحيرة والثابتة أعرف من اللمع واللامع، والكرية أعرف للقمر من هيئة قبول الضوء لكان يمكن أن يجعل هذه العلل حدودًا وسطى. فيقال إن الكواكب المتحيرة قريبة الضوء، وكل ما هو قريب الضوء فإنه لا يلمع؛ أو يقال إن القمر كري، وكل كري فإنه يقبل الضوء هكذا، فكان هذا برهان لم على أنه يجوز أن يعلم أولا الان بالمعلول ثم يقلب فيعلم اللم بالعلة. ولا يكون دورًا لأن البيان الأول لم يطلب فيه [لِمَ] البتة، والبيان الثاني لم يطلب فيه إن البتة فيكون هذا قريبا من المصادرة على المطلوب؛ وليس بمصادرة على المطلوب. ففي أمثال هذه المواد المنعكسة يمكن في علم واحد أن يعلم أن صرف أولا ثم يعلم ثمَّ صرف ثانيًا من مواد بأعيانها مع ما فيها من تقديم وتأخير وزيادة ونقصان: مثاله أن يعلم بالعلم الرصدي أن القمر كري الشكل لأنه يستضيء به كذا كذا فيكون هذا محفوظا؛ ثم يتعرف من علم الطبيعي أن الأجرام السماوية يجب أن تكون مخصوصة بالأشكال الكرية من جهة برهان طبيعي يعطي اللمَ والإن جميعًا. فلذلك ما صار يتشكل على هذا الشكل الذي أنتَ لا تشك في إنيته؛ وإنما يجهل لميته. وقد يمكن مثل ذلك من وجه آخر ; وذلك لأنه قد يمكن أن يكون لشيء واحد معلولات ولوازم مقارنة لا هي علل ولا معلولات مثل أن يكونا معلولين لشيء واحد، وتكون منعكسة عليه يكون له أيضًا علل ذاتية منعكسة عليه. ويكون وجود تلك المعلولات واللوازم لموضوع ما أعرف من وجود تلك العلة أيضًا لذلك الموضوع أعرف من وجود الشيء له: فإن جعل الحد الأوسط من العلل كان البرهان برهان لم وإن معا. وإن جعل من اللوازم والمعلولات كان برهان إن فقط، فإذن هذا الوجه الواحد من وجهي ما نحن فيه. فربَّما قد انشعب إليه وجهين: أحدهما
And if these major terms were better known than these middle terms, such that nearness and farness for the planets and fixed stars are better known than twinkling and non-twinkling, and sphericity for the moon is better known than the state of receiving light, then it would be possible to make these causes middle terms. So it would be said: The wandering stars are near in light, and everything near in light does not twinkle; or it is said: The moon is spherical, and everything spherical receives light in this way. So this would be a demonstration of 'why', on the basis that it is permissible to know first the 'that' by the effect, then to invert and know the 'why' by the cause. And it is not circular, because the first demonstration did not seek the 'why' at all, and the second did not seek the 'that' at all, so this is close to petitio principii; yet it is not petitio principii. For in such convertible matters, it is possible in one science to know the 'that' first, then to know the 'why' secondly from the very same materials, with some precedence and delay, increase and decrease. For example, one knows by observational science that the moon is spherical in shape because it is illuminated in such and such a way; this is retained. Then one learns from natural science that celestial bodies must be characterized by spherical shapes through a natural demonstration that gives both the 'why' and the 'that'. Hence it comes to be formed in this way, such that you do not doubt its 'that', but you are ignorant of its 'why'. And it is possible from another aspect as well: because it may be that a single thing has multiple effects and concomitant properties that are neither causes nor effects, such as both being effects of a single thing, and they are convertible with it, and it also has essential causes convertible with it. And the existence of those effects and concomitants for a subject is better known than the existence of that cause for that subject, or better known than the existence of the thing for it. For if the middle term is taken from the causes, the demonstration is a 'why' and a 'that' together. And if it is taken from the concomitants and effects, it is only a demonstration of 'that'. So this is one of the two faces of what we are discussing. Perhaps it has branched into two aspects: one of them...
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage extends the discussion of demonstration by considering the scenario where the cause (illa) is better known than the effect. In such cases, the cause can serve as the middle term, yielding a 'why' demonstration (burhan limmi). The author illustrates with the same astronomical examples but reversed: if nearness/farness are better known than twinkling, we can say 'planets are near in light, and what is near in light does not twinkle, so planets do not twinkle'—this gives the why (the cause nearness) of the effect (non-twinkling). Importantly, the author addresses a potential objection of circularity: one might first know the 'that' (inn) via the effect, then later know the 'why' (lim) via the cause. This is not circular because the first demonstration does not seek the 'why', and the second does not seek the 'that'. They are separate demonstrations even if using the same terms. The author gives a concrete example from two sciences: observational astronomy (ilm rasci) establishes that the moon is spherical (the 'that'), while natural philosophy (ilm tabi'i) provides a causal demonstration that celestial bodies must be spherical (the 'why'). Thus, the same fact is known both ways without circularity. The passage then introduces a further nuance: a thing may have multiple effects and concomitants (lawazim) that are not causes but are better known. If we take a cause as middle term, we get a demonstration that gives both 'why' and 'that' (burhan limmi inni simultaneously). If we take an effect or concomitant, we get only a 'that' demonstration. This shows that the single face of demonstration under discussion branches into two sub-types depending on whether the middle term is a cause or a non-cause.

Key Terms
برهان لم
دور
مصادرة على المطلوب
لازم

Checkpoint
In the example from observational and natural sciences, what is known first (the 'that') and what is known second (the 'why')?
A) First: moon is spherical; second: celestial bodies must be spherical
B) First: moon receives light; second: moon is spherical
C) First: moon is spherical; second: moon receives light
D) First: celestial bodies are spherical; second: moon is spherical

Key Takeaway
Sequential inn-then-lim demonstrations are legitimate and non-circular because each answers a different epistemic question: 'that' and 'why'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a burhan inni and a burhan limmi for the same Islamic ruling (hukm). For example, the prohibition of riba: first demonstrate 'that' it is forbidden (using textual evidence), then demonstrate 'why' it is forbidden (using the causal principle of preventing exploitation). Explain why the two demonstrations are not circular.
?
Identify in the following argument whether the middle term is a cause or an effect: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Is this burhan limmi or burhan inni? Why?

Why the First Figure is the Most Perfect
Author explains why the first figure is best for certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state three reasons why the first figure is the most perfect syllogistic figure and identify which figure can produce a demonstration of 'why' (burhan lim) actually.

Core Idea
The first figure alone yields actual demonstration of cause, while others only promise it.

In this passage, the author reiterates that the first figure of the categorical syllogism is superior to the others. He gives several compelling reasons: it alone can provide a demonstration of 'why' (burhan lim) actually, it is the only figure to which all syllogisms can be reduced to primary premises, and its form is that of an actual syllogism. We will break down these reasons to understand why the first figure is the foundation of logical certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three reasons for superiority
Read the passage and list the three main reasons the author gives for the superiority of the first figure: (1) it is the only figure that can provide a demonstration of 'why' (burhan lim) actually, (2) it is the only figure that allows analysis of all syllogisms into primary premises, (3) its form is that of an actual syllogism rather than a potential one.
2
Understand 'burhan lim' vs. 'burhan inn'
The author distinguishes between a demonstration that gives the cause (burhan lim) and one that only gives the fact (burhan inn). The first figure, because it directly links effect to cause by primary intention, gives the 'why' actually. Other figures give the cause only potentially.
This is a key epistemological distinction in Islamic logic.
3
See why analysis requires the first figure
The author argues that every syllogism needs at least one universal affirmative premise. Affirmative premises cannot be resolved in the second figure, and universal premises cannot be resolved in the third figure. Thus, only the first figure can serve as the ultimate reductive target.
This is based on the nature of the premises in each figure.
4
Apply to a real syllogism
Take a kalam example: 'Every temporally originated thing has a cause; the world is temporally originated; therefore the world has a cause.' This is a first-figure syllogism (Barbara). Verify that it meets the conditions: the middle term 'temporally originated' is subject in the major premise and predicate in the minor, universal affirmative premises, etc.

And the affirmative cannot be resolved into its premises in the second figure, and the universal cannot be resolved into its premises in the third figure, as previously mentioned. And it has also passed before that the second is better than the third, and the third is better than the fourth, so we shall not repeat it again. Then an objector might say to the First Teacher: When he showed that the first figure is the soundest of figures and the most productive of certainty in several ways, and during that he mentioned that the analysis of syllogisms from the other two figures into premises without a middle term in the first figure, then how can the negative be analyzed into premises without a middle term?
