Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the superiority of the first figure of the syllogism over the other figures, followed by an objection regarding the analysis of negative syllogisms. The author begins by restating that the first figure is the best and most complete in providing certainty. The reasons given are: (1) Demonstrative proof (burhan) by universal affirmative is only possible in the first figure because in this figure the effect is directly linked to the cause by primary intention—this figure actually provides a demonstration of 'why' (burhan lim), whereas the other figures do so only potentially. (2) All syllogisms can be analyzed (reduced) to primary premises only in the first figure, because every syllogism requires a universal affirmative premise, and affirmative premises cannot be resolved in the second figure, nor universal premises in the third figure. (3) The very form of the first figure is that of an actual syllogism, while the others have the form of a potential syllogism; thus the first is superior. The author also notes that among the remaining figures, the second is better than the third, and the third better than the fourth, but he does not repeat the reasons. 

The objection is then raised: The First Teacher (Aristotle) had claimed that all syllogisms from the other figures can be analyzed (reduced) to premises without a middle term in the first figure. The objector questions how this can be done for negative syllogisms. For if the premises of a syllogism include a negative, then the reductive analysis must end with a negative premise that has no middle term (i.e., an immediate negative proposition). But how can there be a negative immediate premise (a negative axiom)? Affirmative immediate premises are possible—they are self-evident truths. But negative immediate premises seem problematic because they involve absence of a middle term in a negative way. The answer begins, but is cut off: 'Just as the affirmative may be without...' The implication is that just as some immediate affirmatives are known directly, some immediate negatives may also be known directly (e.g., 'No black is white'). The text ends before the answer is fully explained.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. Considered the best for demonstration. [Note: Stable across Islamic logic; the first figure is always the most perfect.]
برهان لم (burhan lim): A demonstration that provides the cause (why); the first figure alone yields this actually. [Note: Standard term in Aristotelian demonstration; stable.]

Key Takeaway
The first figure is the most perfect syllogistic figure because alone it yields actual demonstration of the cause, enables reduction to primary premises, and has the form of an actual syllogism.

The Problem of Reducing Negative Syllogisms
Author presents an objection on reducing negative syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difficulty raised by objectors about reducing negative syllogisms to immediate premises in the first figure.

Core Idea
If all syllogisms reduce to immediate premises, negative ones require immediate negatives — can such negatives be known?

After establishing the superiority of the first figure, the author reports an objection: if all syllogisms from other figures are to be analyzed (reduced) to premises without a middle term in the first figure, how is this possible for negative syllogisms? Negative premises seem to require a negative immediate proposition, but is such a thing conceivable? Let us examine the problem.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reduction claim
The First Teacher (Aristotle) claimed that syllogisms in the second and third figures can be reduced to the first figure, and ultimately to immediate premises (propositions without a middle term). The objector accepts this for affirmative syllogisms but questions how it works for negative ones.
2
Identify the core difficulty
The objector argues that if a syllogism contains a negative premise, then the reductive analysis must end with a negative premise that is immediate (without a middle term). But how can there be a negative immediate premise? An immediate affirmative is self-evident (e.g., 'The whole is greater than the part'), but can there be a self-evident negative?
The objector uses the phrase 'ليت شعري' (I wish I knew) to express puzzlement.
3
Consider an example
Think of a negative syllogism like 'No human is a stone; all humans are animals; therefore no animal is a stone?' That is not valid. A valid negative syllogism might be 'No stone is an animal; some stones are hard; therefore no animal is hard?' Not sure. But the point is that the reduction would need an immediate negative like 'No black is white' which seems intuitively true.
The passage hints that some immediate negatives are indeed known directly, but the objector questions their status.
4
Recognize the open resolution
The passage ends with the answer beginning: 'Just as the affirmative may be without...' implying that just as there are immediate affirmatives, there are also immediate negatives. However, the text is cut off. The student should understand that later logicians resolve this by accepting immediate negatives of the form 'No A is non-A' or by appealing to intuition.
This is an advanced nuance—not all logicians agree on the self-evidence of negatives.

Why the First Figure is Superior
Author enumerates reasons for the first figure's superiority over others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain at least three reasons why the first figure of the categorical syllogism is considered the most excellent among the four figures.

Core Idea
The first figure alone yields actual demonstrative knowledge and reduces to self-evident premises.

In this passage, the author explains why the first figure is superior to the other three figures of the categorical syllogism. He gives several reasons, from its unique ability to produce certainty to its role in demonstrative proof. Understanding these reasons will help you appreciate why the first figure is preferred in rigorous Islamic scholarly arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of superiority
Find the opening statement that the first figure is the best and most complete in producing certainty. This sets up the entire discussion.
2
Locate the first reason: demonstrative proof
Look for the statement that universal affirmative demonstration (burhan) is only possible in the first figure, because the effect is attributed to the cause directly. This is tied to the concept of burhan lim (demonstration of the cause).
3
Locate the second reason: analysis to primary premises
Find the claim that only the first figure allows the resolution of syllogisms into primary (self-evident) premises, because the universal affirmative can only be resolved in the first figure.
4
Locate the third reason: actual vs potential syllogism
Identify the statement that the first figure's form is that of an actual syllogism, whereas other figures are potential. This makes the first figure essentially superior.

فصل: في فضيلة بعض الأشكال على بعض وفي أن قياس الغلط كيف يقع في الأشكال
قد مرَّ من قبل أن الشكل الأول أفضل الأشكال وأكملها في إفادة اليقين بوجوه منها أن البيان البرهاني بالإيجاب الكلي لا يمكن إلا بالشكل الأول لما أن المعلول تارة للعلة بالقصد الأول فى هذا الشكل على خلاف غيره من الأشكال. وبالحقيقة هو بالفعل برهان لم؛ وغيره لا يكون برهان لم إلا بالقوة. ومن جملة ما يدل على فضيلته هو أن تحليل القياسات إلى المقدمات الأولية لا يمكن إلا بهذا الشكل لما أن الموجبة الكلية من اللوازم في كل قياس. والموجبة لا تنحل إلى مقدماتها بالشكل الثاني، والكلية لا تنحل إليها بالشكل الثالث لما مر . وقد كفى الأول من الفضائل أن هيئته هيئة قياس بالفعل؛ وهيئة غيره هيئة قياس بالقوة فيكون أفضل والحمل من الثاني والثالث والرابع. وقد مرَّ من قبل أيضًا أن الثاني أفضل من الثالث، والثالث أفضل من الرابع، فلا نعيده مرة أخرى.
Section: On the virtue of some figures over others and on how the erroneous syllogism occurs in the figures. It has been mentioned before that the first figure is the best of the figures and the most complete in producing certainty for several reasons: among them is that demonstrative proof by universal affirmative cannot be attained except in the first figure, because the effect is attributed to the cause by primary intention in this figure contrary to other figures. And in reality, it is actually a demonstration 'lim'; and other than it is not a demonstration 'lim' except potentially. Among the things that indicate its virtue is that the analysis of syllogisms into primary premises cannot be done except by this figure, because the universal affirmative is necessary in every syllogism. The affirmative cannot be resolved into its premises in the second figure, and the universal cannot be resolved into them in the third figure for reasons mentioned. And the first figure suffices in virtues that its form is the form of an actual syllogism; while the form of others is the form of a potential syllogism, so it is better, and the carrying over from the second, third, and fourth. And it has also been mentioned before that the second is better than the third, and the third better than the fourth, so we will not repeat it again.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the superiority of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author states that the first figure is the best and most perfect for producing certainty (yaqin) for several reasons. First, demonstrative proof (burhan) via universal affirmative propositions can only be achieved in the first figure, because in this figure the effect is attributed to the cause directly (bil-qasd al-awwal), unlike other figures. In fact, the first figure is actually a demonstration 'lim' (burhan lim, giving the cause), while other figures are only potentially so. Second, the analysis of syllogisms into primary premises (muqaddimat awwaliyya) can only be done in the first figure, because a universal affirmative is a necessary component in every syllogism. The affirmative cannot be resolved in the second figure, and the universal cannot be resolved in the third figure. The author then notes that the first figure's form is that of an actual syllogism, whereas others are merely potential, making it superior. He also recalls that the second figure is better than the third, and the third better than the fourth, but does not repeat the reasons.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
برهان لم
المقدمات الأولية
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) It cannot be analyzed into primary premises.

Key Takeaway
The first figure is the most excellent because it alone provides actual demonstrative proof of the cause, allows reduction to primary premises, and has the form of an actual syllogism.

Negative Propositions as Immediate: Objection and Answer
Author answers objection about negative immediate premises in syllogistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how negative propositions can be immediate (لا وسط لها) and apply this to reducing syllogisms from other figures to the first figure.

Core Idea
Negative propositions can be immediate just like affirmatives—making first-figure analysis complete.

In this passage, the author tackles a critical objection: if negative propositions always require a negative premise, how can they be reduced to immediate premises (لا وسط لها) in the first figure? The answer reveals that negativity does not preclude immediacy, just as affirmatives can be immediate. Understanding this is essential for mastering syllogistic reduction and defending the superiority of the first figure.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objector claims that negative propositions cannot be immediate because any resolution into premises must include a negative, so how can the chain end with a negative that has no middle term?
2
Understand the author's resolution
The author affirms that negatives can be immediate, analogous to affirmatives being 'without interruption' – the predicate is simply denied of the subject without requiring an intermediate term.
The analogy hinges on the predicate being either affirmed or denied directly.
3
Note the role of conversion
Universal negatives are convertible; this conversion is an immediate inference, showing that negativity does not prevent immediacy, especially in essential necessities.
Conversion preserves truth for universal negatives (No S is P → No P is S).
4
Apply to syllogistic reduction
In the first figure, a negative premise can be immediate and used to analyze syllogisms from other figures. The examples with classes show how to construct syllogisms using unique predicates.

ثم لقائل أن يقول على المعلم الأول لما بين أن الشكل الأول أصح الأشكال وأكثرها إفادة لليقين بوجوه، وذكر في أثناء ذلك البيان أن تحليل القياسات من الشكلين الآخرين إلى مقدمات غير ذات وسط في الشكل الأول أن السالبة كيف يكون بها تحليل إلى مقدمات غير ذات وسط، فإن المقدمات تنحل إليها السالبة فلا بد فيها من سالبة، فكيف تنتهي إلى سالبة غير ذات وسط، وكيف تكون سالبة غير ذات وسط أما الموجبة التي لا وسط لها فهي التي لا يمكن أن يكون المحمول فيها أو لا لشيء هو علة لوجوده للموضوع. فالسالبة كيف تكون فقدانها للوسط ليتَ شُعري؛ فقال إنه كما أن الموجبة قد تكون بغير انقطاع أي بحيث لا يقتضي حمل محموله على موضوعه شيئًا متوسطا بقطع مجاورتهما فيكون هو أو لا للموضوع والمحمول له أو لا ثم للموضوع، فكذلك السالبة قد تكون بغير انقطاع أي بحيث لا يكون الحكم يسلب محمولها عن موضوعها مقتضيا شيئًا آخر عنه يسلب محموله أولا وهو موجود للموضوع. ولأن السلب الكلي منعكس خصوصًا في الضروريات الذاتية فمتى وجد لأحد الحدين شيء محمول عليه لا على الآخر أو يوجد لكل واحد منهما شيء يخصه. فيكون في كل رتبة شيء يخصه ويساوي ذلك الحد، وكانت الرتبتان متنافيتين ليس في إحديهما شيء يدخل في الجملة الأخرى؛ إذ المحمول على إحديهما يمكن أن يجعل حدا أوسط، فيكون أيهما شئتَ عن الآخر بقياس : فإن كان المحمول الموجب إنما هو في جانب أحد الحدين فقط كان ذلك بقياس واحد مثل إن كان كل «أج»، ولا شيء من «بج» أو كل «بج»، ولا شيء من «أج». وإن كان المحمول الموجب في جانب كل واحد منهما كان ذلك بقياسين مثلا إن كان «أ» و «د» و «ج» طبقة متساوية، و «ط» يحمل عليها ويساويها، و «ب» و «هـ» و «ز» أيضًا طبقة متساوية، و «ج» يحمل عليها ويساويها: ومعلوم أن شيئًا من هذه الطبقة لا يحمل على شيء من تلك الطبقة. فإن قيل إن كان كل «أط» ، ولا شيء من ب ط كان قياسا، وإن قيل كل ب ج ، ولا شيء من «أج» كان قياسا، وهما قياسان. وكذلك الحال إن لم يكن لا أن يحمل عليه (ط) فقط ، أو «ب» يحمل عليه «ج» فقط لكن العادة في التمثيل ج ب لذلك، فإذا كان لأحد الحدين محمول خاص
Then an objector may say against the First Teacher: When he showed that the first figure is the soundest of figures and most productive of certainty in several ways, and he mentioned in that explanation that the analysis of syllogisms from the other two figures into premises having no middle term is in the first figure, how can the negative be analyzed into premises having no middle term? For the premises into which the negative resolves must include a negative, so how does it end up with a negative having no middle term, and how can a negative have no middle term? As for the affirmative which has no middle term, it is that in which the predicate cannot be either something that is a cause for its existence for the subject. So how does the negative lack a middle term? I wish I knew. So he said: Just as the affirmative may be without interruption, i.e., such that the attribution of its predicate to its subject does not require something intermediate by cutting their adjacency so that it is or is not for the subject and the predicate is or is not then for the subject, so likewise the negative may be without interruption, i.e., such that the judgment negating its predicate from its subject does not require something else beyond it that negates its predicate firstly while it exists for the subject. And because universal negation is converses, especially in essential necessities, so when there is for one of the two limits something predicated on it and not on the other, or there is for each of them something specific to it. So in each rank there is something specific to it and equal to that limit, and the two ranks are mutually exclusive, none of one containing anything entering into the other; because what is predicated on one of them can be made a middle term, so whichever you wish from the other by a syllogism: If the affirmative predicate is only on one side of the two limits, then that is by one syllogism, such as 'Every A is J' and 'No B is J' or 'Every B is J' and 'No A is J'. And if the affirmative predicate is on each side, then that is by two syllogisms, for example if A, D, and J are an equal class, and T is predicated on them and equals them, and B, H, and Z are also an equal class, and J is predicated on them and equals them; and it is known that none of this class is predicated on anything from that class. So if it is said 'Every A is T' and 'No B is T', that is one syllogism, and if it is said 'Every B is J' and 'No A is J', that is a syllogism, and they are two syllogisms. And likewise the case if it is not that only T is predicated on it, or only J is predicated on B, but the custom in examples is J B for that. So when for one of the two limits there is a specific predicate...
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection raised against the First Teacher (Aristotle) concerning the analysis of syllogisms from the second and third figures into immediate premises (no middle term) in the first figure. The objector focuses on negative propositions: how can a negative proposition be reduced to premises with no middle term? After all, any resolution of a negative must include a negative premise, so how can it end with a negative that itself has no middle term? The answer explains that just as an affirmative can be immediate (without interruption), meaning its predication requires no intermediate term, so too a negative can be immediate. The author then discusses the conversion of universal negatives, especially in essential necessities. He illustrates with examples using terms (A, B, J, T, etc.) representing classes. When one limit has a predicate unique to it and not the other, a single syllogism can be formed. When each limit has its own specific predicate, two syllogisms are needed. The passage includes a detailed example with classes A, D, J, T and B, H, Z, J, showing how to derive syllogisms. The explanation emphasizes that negative immediate propositions are possible and that the analysis into the first figure works for both affirmative and negative propositions.

The Syllogism

Direct vs. Indirect Negation in Categorical Syllogisms
Author explains conditions for direct vs. indirect negative syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct and indirect negative propositions and identify the conditions under which a negative syllogism requires a middle term.

Core Idea
A negative proposition can be proven directly without a middle term when the two terms have no specific predicate in common.

This passage from a classical logic text introduces a crucial distinction: negative propositions can be either direct (inqita') or indirect, depending on whether a middle term is needed to prove the negation. The author defines 'cutting off' and the conditions for each type. This distinction is essential for correctly evaluating negative judgments in Islamic sciences, such as 'No intoxicant is permissible' or 'The world is not eternal'. Let's break down the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Define direct and indirect negation
Find where the author defines 'cutting off' (inqita'). Understand that a direct negation does not require an intermediate term, while an indirect negation does. Note the phrase 'the judgment does not negate its predicate from its subject while necessitating something else.'
2
Identify the condition for direct negation
Locate the sentence: 'if one of the two terms does not have a specific predicate, and one of them is negated from the other, that negation must be without cutting off, without a middle term.' This tells you when a negative proposition can be direct.
A specific predicate is one that applies to one term but not the other.
3
Examine the role of specific predicates
The author says that if there is a predicate specific to one term (e.g., J predicated only on A and not on B), that predicate can serve as a middle term to prove the negation between A and B. This makes the negation indirect.
The middle term must be known to be predicated on one and not on the other.
4
Map the example with Arabic variables
Work through the author's example: 'if every A is J and no B is J, that is a syllogism.' Here J is the specific predicate of A. Identify the premises and conclusion. This is an indirect negation because a middle term (J) is used.
Apply to a real case: Let A = 'intoxicant', B = 'permissible', J = 'prohibited'. Then 'Every intoxicant is prohibited' and 'No permissible is prohibited' yields 'No intoxicant is permissible'.
5
Understand the objection and response
Someone objects that all things have specific predicates, so direct negation never occurs. The author replies that the middle term must be a cause and its judgment more known. This refines the condition: not just any predicate qualifies.
This highlights the epistemological requirement for a sound syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقطاع أي بحيث لا يقتضي حمل محموله على موضوعه شيئًا متوسطا بقطع مجاورتهما فيكون هو أو لا للموضوع والمحمول له أو لا ثم للموضوع، فكذلك السالبة قد تكون بغير انقطاع أي بحيث لا يكون الحكم يسلب محمولها عن موضوعها مقتضيا شيئًا آخر عنه يسلب محموله أولا وهو موجود للموضوع. ولأن السلب الكلي منعكس خصوصًا في الضروريات الذاتية فمتى وجد لأحد الحدين شيء محمول عليه لا على الآخر أو يوجد لكل واحد منهما شيء يخصه. فيكون في كل رتبة شيء يخصه ويساوي ذلك الحد، وكانت الرتبتان متنافيتين ليس في إحديهما شيء يدخل في الجملة الأخرى؛ إذ المحمول على إحديهما يمكن أن يجعل حدا أوسط، فيكون أيهما شئت عن الآخر بقياس: فإن كان المحمول الموجب إنما هو في جانب أحد الحدين فقط كان ذلك بقياس واحد مثل إن كان كل أج، ولا شيء من بج أو كل بج، ولا شيء من أج. وإن كان المحمول الموجب في جانب كل واحد منهما كان ذلك بقياسين مثلا إن كان أ و د و ج طبقة متساوية، و ط يحمل عليها ويساويها، و ب و هـ و ز أيضًا طبقة متساوية، و ج يحمل عليها ويساويها: ومعلوم أن شيئًا من هذه الطبقة لا يحمل على شيء من تلك الطبقة. فإن قيل إن كان كل أط، ولا شيء من ب ط كان قياس، وإن قيل كل ب ج، ولا شيء من أج كان قياس، وهما قياسان. وكذلك الحال إن لم يكن لا أن يحمل عليه ط فقط، أو ب يحمل عليه ج فقط لكن العادة في التمثيل ج ب لذلك، فإذا كان لأحد الحدين محمول خاص كان السلب بانقطاع فيجب أنه إذا لم يكن لأحد الحدين محمول خاص، وأحدهما مسلوب عن الآخر أن يكون ذلك سالبا بلا انقطاع له بلا واسطة. ولقائل أن يقول إن السالبة التي لا وسط بها إن طلبت بهذه الشريطة لم توجد فإنه لا يخلو أ و ب من حد أو رسم ومن أجزائهما. وبالجملة الأشياء لا تخلو عن الخواص واللوازم حتى الأجناس العالية؛ فكيف يوجد أ و ب غير محمول على أحدهما شيء لا يحمل على الآخر. لكنا نقول: عسى أن لا يكون لهذا الوسط أي محمول اتفق، وأن لا يكون القياس من كل ما له وسط أي وسط اتفق بل يجب أن يكون الوسط سببًا وجوده للأصغر. والحكم بالأكبر عليه كل واحدة منهما أعرف من الحكم بالأكبر على الأصغر. وفي المطلوب السالب يجب أن يكون وجود الأوسط للأصغر؛ وسلب الأكبر عن الأوسط أعرف من سلب الأكبر عن الأصغر فحينئذ يكون وسطا وقياسًا، ويشبه أن يكون المحمول عند المعلم الأول محمولا نسبته إلى أ وإلى ب أعرف من نسبة ما بينهما هذا.
Cutting off, in that the carrying of its predicate on its subject does not necessitate something intermediate by cutting off their contiguity, so it is either yes or no for the subject and the predicate has it or not, then for the subject. Similarly, the negative may be without cutting off, in that the judgment does not negate its predicate from its subject while necessitating something else from which its predicate is negated first and which exists for the subject. And because universal negation is converted, especially in essential necessities, so whenever there exists for one of the two terms something predicated on it and not on the other, or there exists for each of them something specific to it. So in each rank there is something specific to it and equal to that term, and the two ranks are mutually exclusive, not having in one of them anything entering into the other class; because the predicate on one of them can be made a middle term, so whichever you want [to prove] of the other, it is by a syllogism. If the affirmative predicate is only on the side of one of the two terms, that is by one syllogism, like if every A is J and no B is J, or every B is J and no A is J. And if the affirmative predicate is on the side of each one, that is by two syllogisms, for example if A, D, and J are an equal class, and T is predicated on them and equals them, and B, H, and Z are also an equal class, and J is predicated on them and equals them; and it is known that nothing of this class is predicated on anything of that class. For if it is said that every A is T and no B is T, it is a syllogism; and if it is said that every B is J and no A is J, it is a syllogism, and they are two syllogisms. And likewise if it is not that only T is predicated on A, or only J is predicated on B, but custom in exemplification is J and B for that. So if one of the two terms has a specific predicate, the negation is with cutting off. Hence, if one of the two terms does not have a specific predicate, and one of them is negated from the other, that negation must be without cutting off, without a middle term. And someone might say that the negative which has no middle term, if sought under this condition, does not exist, because A and B are not free from a definition or a description and from their parts. In short, things are not free from properties and necessary concomitants, even the highest genera. So how can A and B exist without something being predicated on one that is not predicated on the other? But we say: perhaps this middle term is not any accidental predicate, and the syllogism is not from everything that has a middle term, any middle term whatever, but the middle term must be a cause of the existence of the minor term. And the judgment of the major on each of them is more known than the judgment of the major on the minor. And in the desired negative, the existence of the middle term for the minor must be [more known], and the negation of the major from the middle term is more known than the negation of the major from the minor. Then it is a middle term and a syllogism. And it seems that the predicate, according to the First Teacher, is a predicate whose relation to A and to B is more known than the relation between them.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions under which a negative proposition can be proven directly (without a middle term) or indirectly (via a middle term). The author begins by defining 'cutting off' (inqita') in the context of immediate inference: a predicate is carried on a subject without requiring an intermediate term that cuts off contiguity. Similarly, a negative proposition can be either direct (without cutting off) or indirect (with cutting off). The author then states that universal negation is convertible, especially in essential necessities. The core idea is that when two terms are in different ranks (classes) with no overlap, there will be a predicate specific to each rank. Such a predicate can serve as a middle term to prove the separation between the ranks. The passage then distinguishes cases where the affirmative predicate is on only one side or on both sides, giving examples using variable letters: e.g., if every A is J and no B is J, that is one syllogism; if both sides have an affirmative predicate, two syllogisms are needed. The author also addresses an objection: it might seem that there is always some predicate specific to any two terms, making direct negation impossible. The reply is that the middle term must be a cause for the minor's existence, and the judgment of the major on the middle must be more known than on the minor. This ensures that the syllogism is sound and the negation is not arbitrary.

سلب كلي
منعكس
حد أوسط
ضروريات ذاتية

Key Takeaway
A negative proposition can be direct (without a middle term) if the two terms have no specific predicate in common; otherwise, a middle term is needed if there is a predicate specific to one term.

Negative Propositions and the Middle Term
Author discusses negative propositions without a middle term and responds to objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a middle term is valid in a negative syllogism, and respond to the objection that every pair of terms shares a property.

Core Idea
Not every shared property makes a valid middle term; it must be a causal link and more known.

In this passage, the author tackles a critical question in syllogistic logic: can a negative proposition be proven without a middle term? An objector argues that every two terms share some property, so a middle term always exists, making immediate negatives impossible. The author responds by clarifying that not just any shared property suffices; the middle term must be a cause of the attribution and its relation to each extreme must be more evident than the direct relation. This lesson unpacks that condition and its implications for constructing valid syllogisms in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about immediate negatives
The author states that if a universal negative ('no S is P') holds and there is no specific predicate applying to one term that does not apply to the other, then the negation is immediate (no middle term). Locate this claim in the Arabic.
The phrase 'السلب بانقطاع' refers to universal negation.
2
Understand the objection
The objector argues that such an immediate negative cannot exist because every two terms ('A' and 'B') share some definition, description, or part. Thus, there is always a middle term. Follow the logical force of this objection.
3
Extract the author's response
The author counters that the middle term must be a cause of the predicate's existence for the subject, and the judgments through it must be more known than the direct relation. Not any shared property qualifies. Identify the conditions: causal connection and epistemic priority.
The author references 'the First Teacher' (Aristotle) to support this.
4
Apply the condition to a negative syllogism
Test a negative syllogism from Islamic sciences: e.g., 'No intoxicant is permissible; wine is intoxicant; therefore wine is not permissible.' Check if the middle term ('intoxicant') satisfies the causal and epistemic conditions.
In usul al-fiqh, 'intoxicant' is the effective cause ('illah) for prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان السلب بانقطاع فيجب أنه إذا لم يكن لأحد الحديــن محمول خاص، وأحدهما مسلوب عن الآخر أن يكون ذلك سالبا بلا انقطاع له بلا واسطة. ولقائل أن يقول إن السالبة التي لا وسط بها إن طلبت بهذه الشريطة لم توجد فإنه لا يخلو «أ» و «ب) من حد أو رسم ومن أجزائهما. وبالجملة الأشياء لا تخلو عن الخواص واللوازم حتى الأجناس العالية؛ فكيف يوجد «أ» و «ب» غير محمول على أحدهما شيء لا يحمل على الآخر. لكنا نقول: عسى أن لا يكون لهذا الوسط أي محمول اتفق، وأن لا يكون القياس من كل ما له وسط أي وسط اتفق بل يجب أن يكون الوسط سببًا وجوده للأصغر. والحكم بالأكبر عليه كل واحدة منهما أعرف من الحكم بالأكبر على الأصغر. وفي المطلوب السالب يجب أن يكون وجود الأوسط للأصغر؛ وسلب الأكبر عن الأوسط أعرف من سلب الأكبر عن الأصغر فحينئذ يكون وسطا وقياسًا، ويشبه أن يكون المحمول عند المعلم الأوّل محمولا نسبته إلى «أ» وإلى «ب» أعرف من نسبة / ما بينهما هذا.
If the negation is with separation, then it is necessary that if one of two boundary terms has no specific predicate, and one of them is negated of the other, that negation be without separation, without a middle term. Someone may say that the negative proposition which has no middle term, if sought with this condition, does not exist, because 'A' and 'B' are not free from a definition or description and from their parts. In general, things are not free from properties and concomitants, even the highest genera; so how can 'A' and 'B' exist such that nothing is predicated of one that is not predicated of the other? But we say: perhaps this middle term is not any predicate that happens, and the syllogism is not from every thing that has a middle term, i.e., any middle that happens, but rather the middle term must be a cause of the existence [of the predicate] for the minor term. And the judgment by the major [term] upon each of them is more known than the judgment by the major upon the minor. In the desired negative [proposition], the existence of the middle for the minor must be more known, and the negation of the major from the middle more known than the negation of the major from the minor. Then it becomes a middle term and a syllogism. It seems that the predicate, according to the First Teacher, is a predicate whose relation to 'A' and to 'B' is more known than the relation between them.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for a negative syllogism without a middle term. The author begins by stating that if a negation is 'with separation' (i.e., a universal negative), then if there is no specific predicate for one of the terms, and one is negated of the other, the negation should be immediate (without a middle term). An objector then argues that such an immediate negative proposition is impossible because every two terms 'A' and 'B' share some property or description or part, so there will always be a middle term. The author counters that not just any predicate can serve as a middle term; it must be a cause of the attribution of the major to the minor, and the relation of the middle to each extreme must be more known than the direct relation. In a sought-after negative conclusion, the existence of the middle for the minor must be more known, and the negation of the major from the middle must be more known than the direct negation. Only then does the middle term properly serve in a syllogism. The author references the 'First Teacher' (Aristotle) to emphasize that the predicate's relation to the terms should be better known.

Key Takeaway
A valid middle term in a negative syllogism must be a causal link and its relation to the extremes must be more evident than the direct relation between the extremes.

Consider the negative syllogism: 'No rationally obligatory act is futile; prayer is rationally obligatory; therefore prayer is not futile.' Identify the middle term and explain whether it satisfies the author's conditions (causal link and more known).
?
The objector claims every pair of terms shares a property. Construct a response using the author's argument that not every property qualifies as a middle term. Give an example from Islamic theology (kalam) where two terms share a property but it does not yield a valid syllogism.

The Syllogism
advanced
8 min
Syllogisms That Cause Compound Ignorance in the First Figure
Author analyzes first figure syllogisms causing compound ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct categorical syllogisms in the first figure that produce compound ignorance through false premises, distinguishing cases where both premises are false from those where only the minor is false.

Core Idea
False premises, not invalid form, cause compound ignorance in valid syllogisms.

In Islamic logic, compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) occurs when one holds a false belief opposite to the truth. This lesson explores how categorical syllogisms in the first figure, with false premises, can produce such deceptive conclusions. The author distinguishes cases where both premises are false and where only the minor is false, illustrating the 'necessary deception' (al-indikha' al-wajib). Understanding this helps prevent being deceived by superficially valid arguments built on false foundations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the truth and the deception
The truth is a universal negative: no A is B (without qualification). The deception is the opposite universal affirmative: every B is A. Your task is to recognize that the deceived person holds a belief that is the utmost contradiction of the truth.
This is the necessary deception: the false belief directly opposes the truth.
2
Locate the middle term and premises
The syllogism uses a middle term C. The minor premise is 'every B is C' and the major is 'every C is A'. Check whether these premises are true or false given the actual state of affairs.
The conclusion of this first-figure syllogism would be 'every B is A', which is false if the truth is 'no A is B'.
3
Analyze the scenario where only the major is true
Consider the example: A is predicated affirmatively of C and negatively of B, with B and C unrelated. Then 'every B is C' is false, but 'every C is A' is true. The syllogism still produces the false conclusion 'every B is A'. This only occurs in the first figure.
This shows that compound ignorance can arise even when one premise (the major) is true, as long as the minor is false.
4
Recognize the compound ignorance syllogism without a middle term
The author describes a special case: a universal negative with no middle term (immediate). If the truth is 'every C is B' and 'every B is A', but one asserts 'every B is C' and 'no C is A', the false conclusion 'no B is A' results. Compound ignorance does not occur from a premise without a middle term.
This highlights that compound ignorance requires a middle term, even if the premises are false.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Replace A, B, C with terms from fiqh: Let A = 'permissible', B = 'wine', C = 'alcohol'. Truth: 'No alcohol is permissible' (universal negative). But someone falsely believes 'Every wine is permissible'. The deceptive syllogism: 'Every wine is alcohol' (true), 'Every alcohol is permissible' (false) leads to 'Every wine is permissible'. Identify which premise is false and why compound ignorance occurs.
This illustrates how false premises in a valid syllogism can lead to a false belief that contradicts the known truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنبتدأ بالانخداع الواجب فنقول: إذا كان الحق هو أنه لا شيء من «أب» وكان بغير انقطاع واختدع فظن أن كل ب أ حتى يكون في غاية المضادة للحق. وكان ذلك بقياس حده الأوسط «ج» ؛ فقد يمكن أن تكون الكبرى والصغرى كاذبة، وقد يمكن أن لا تكون بل الكاذبة إحداهما فقط، أما الأول فإذا كان شيئًا لا يحمل عليه «ب» ولا يحمل عليه «أ» واحد إن كل «بج»، وكل «ج أ» ينتج الباطل؛ وهذا ممكن، ولا يلزم أن يكون بوسط. وكذلك إذا كان «ج» يحمل على بعض ما هو من «ب» لا على كله، و «ب» غير ممكن أن يكون في كل شيء البتة، أو بأن يكون في كله شيء البتة فيكون قولنا كل بج كاذبا بالجزء، وكل «ج أ» كاذبًا بالكل والجزء معًا أو بالجزء وحده. وأما إذا كانت إحديهما صادقة فقط فلا يمكن إلا أن تكون الكبرى. ومثال هذا أن يعرض «أ» محمولا وله موضوعان «ج» و «ب» لكنه يكون موجبًا على «ج» ومسلوبًا عن «ب» بلا انقطاع؛ و «ب» و «ج» لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر. فإن قيل كل بج وهو باطل، وكل ج أ وهو حق ينتج باطلا وهو كل ب أ. وسواء كان هذا السلب والإيجاب بانقطاع أو بغير انقطاع فإن هذه المادة لا ينتج إلا باطلا، فهذا هو الذي لا يكون إلا في الشكل الأول. وأما القياس الموقع للجهل المركب فكل سالب غير ذي وسط فيكون في الشكل الأول عن كاذبتين فإنه إذا كان كل ج ب، وكل ب أ بلا وسط؛ وكان لا شيء من ب ج فقيل كل ب ج، ولا شيء من ج أ ينتج لا شيء من ب أ. ويمكن أن تكون إحداهما صادقة وهي الكبرى مثلا؛ وليكن «أ» من المسلوبات عن «ج» والموجبات لـ «ب» وهما كما قلنا وهذا ممكن. فيجب أن يكون قولنا كل ب ج كاذبًا وهو الصغرى. فإن قيل كل بج وهو كذب، ولا شيء من ج أ وهو صدق ينتج كذبًا. وإن كانت الصادقة هي الصغرى فإنه إذا كان الحق أن كل ب ج، وكل ج أ فقيل كل ب ج، ولا شيء من ج أ ينتج لا محالة سالبا يقابل الحق وهذه المادة هي أن يكون «أ» موجبا لـ «ب» و «ج» معا؛ و «ب» تحت «ج» أو مساو لـ «ج» لكن الجهل المركب لا يكون بمقدمة غير ذات وسط. وأما في الشكل الثاني والمقدمتان كاذبتان بالكل
Let us begin with the necessary deception. We say: If the truth is that no A is B, and it is without qualification, and one is deceived so that he thinks that every B is A, such that it is utmost contradictory to the truth. And that is by a syllogism whose middle term is C; then it is possible that both the major and minor premise are false, and it is possible that not both, but only one of them is false. As for the first case: if there is something such that B is not predicated of it, nor is A predicated of it—provided that every B is C and every C is A—it produces falsehood; this is possible, and it does not require that there be a middle term. Likewise if C is predicated of some of what belongs to B, not all of it, and B is impossible to be in every thing absolutely, or for everything to be in it absolutely, then our statement 'every B is C' is false in part, and 'every C is A' is false wholly and partially together, or partially alone. As for when only one of them is true, it is impossible except that it be the major. An example: let A be a predicate with two subjects C and B, such that it is affirmative of C and negative of B without qualification; and B and C are not predicated of each other. If it is said 'every B is C', which is false, and 'every C is A', which is true, it produces falsehood, namely 'every B is A'. Whether this negation and affirmation are qualified or unqualified, this matter only produces falsehood; thus this is what occurs only in the first figure. As for the syllogism that causes compound ignorance, every negative that has no middle term occurs in the first figure from two false premises: when it is given that every C is B, and every B is A without a middle, and it is false that no B is C, so it is said 'every B is C' and 'no C is A', producing 'no B is A'. It is possible that one of them is true, such as the major; let A be among those negated of C and affirmed of B, as we said, and this is possible. Then our statement 'every B is C' must be false, which is the minor. If it is said 'every B is C', which is false, and 'no C is A', which is true, it produces falsehood. And if the true one is the minor, then if the truth is that every B is C and every C is A, it is said 'every B is C' and 'no C is A', necessarily producing a negative opposite to the truth. This matter is that A is affirmative of both B and C, and B is under C or equal to C. But compound ignorance does not occur from a premise without a middle term. As for the second figure, both premises are completely false.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed analysis of the syllogisms that lead to compound ignorance (jahl murakkab). The author begins by describing a 'necessary deception' (al-indikha' al-wajib), where the truth is a universal negative (no A is B) but the deceived person believes the opposite universal affirmative (every B is A). This false belief is produced by a syllogism with middle term C. The author then examines two scenarios: when both premises are false, and when only one premise is false. He explains that in the first scenario, both premises can be false in various ways: either the middle term C is not actually predicated of anything relevant, or the premises are false in part or in whole. For the second scenario, only the major premise can be true; the minor must be false. An example is given: if A is predicated affirmatively of C and negatively of B, and B and C are unrelated, then asserting 'every B is C' (false) and 'every C is A' (true) yields the false conclusion 'every B is A'. This only occurs in the first figure. Next, the author discusses a special type of syllogism that causes compound ignorance: a universal negative without a middle term (i.e., where the middle is immediate?). In the first figure, with two false premises, if the truth is that every C is B and every B is A, and it is falsely said that every B is C and no C is A, the conclusion 'no B is A' is false. He then considers cases where one premise is true. If the major is true (A is negated of C and affirmed of B), then the minor must be false. If the minor is true (every B is C and every C is A), then saying 'every B is C' (true) and 'no C is A' (false) yields a false negative. Importantly, compound ignorance does not arise from a premise without a middle term. Finally, he mentions the second figure, where both premises are completely false. This analysis underscores the logical structure underlying the acquisition of false beliefs that constitute compound ignorance.

Key Terms
الانخداع الواجب (al-indikha' al-wajib): The necessary deception: a situation where one holds the opposite of a universal truth, specifically believing a universal affirmative when the truth is a universal negative.
القياس الموقع للجهل المركب (al-qiyas al-muqi' li al-jahl al-murakkab): A syllogism that produces compound ignorance, characterized by false premises leading to a false conclusion opposite to the truth. [Note: In later texts, such syllogisms are often categorized under sophistical refutations (mughalata).]
الشكل الأول (al-shakl al-awwal): The first figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise. [Note: This figure is considered the most natural and yields valid conclusions without conversion in the classical tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Second figure
B) First figure
C) Third figure
D) Fourth figure

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance arises from false premises in a valid syllogistic form, especially in the first figure, where the minor premise is false even if the major is true.

The Syllogism

First Figure Syllogisms with One True Premise
Author explains conditions for false conclusions in first figure when one premise is true.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition under which a first-figure categorical syllogism with one true premise produces a false conclusion and construct a fiqh example demonstrating it.

Core Idea
A true major premise does not save the syllogism from falsehood if the minor is false.

In the first figure, when only one premise is true, the author asserts that the true premise must be the major. Otherwise, a false conclusion cannot arise from this configuration. We will dissect the passage to understand why this is the case and apply it to real Islamic arguments, such as those found in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogistic structure
Recognize that the passage discusses first-figure categorical syllogisms. In the first figure, the middle term is subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor premise.
e.g., All B are C (minor), All C are A (major), so All B are A (conclusion).
2
Locate the two types of false premises
The author distinguishes between premises false in part (i.e., some instances) and false in whole. Note how partial or total falsity affects the conclusion.
Example: 'Every B is C' can be false in part (some B are not C) or false in whole (no B is C).
3
Focus on the one-true-premise condition
The author asserts that when exactly one premise is true, it must be the major premise. Understand why: a true minor and false major cannot produce a false conclusion (or the syllogism would be invalid in another figure).
This is a specific property of the first figure.
4
Trace the author's example
Work through the author's variables: A is affirmed of C and denied of B, B and C are unrelated. The false minor 'Every B is C' and true major 'Every C is A' yield false conclusion 'Every B is A'. Map this to a concrete Islamic example.
e.g., A=forbidden, C=intoxicant, B=nabidh. But if nabidh is not intoxicating, the minor is false.
5
Generalize the principle
Conclude that in the first figure, a false minor combined with a true major inevitably leads to a false conclusion. A true minor with a false major does not produce falsehood (or the syllogism is not first-figure).
This helps in evaluating arguments: a true major does not guarantee truth if the minor is unsound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا كان شيئًا لا يحمل عليه «ب» ولا يحمل عليه «أ» واحد إن كل «بج»، وكل ج أ» ينتج الباطل؛ وهذا ممكن، ولا يلزم أن يكون بوسط . وكذلك إذا كان «ج» يحمل على بعض ما هو من «ب» لا على كله، و «ب» غير ممكن أن يكون في كل شيء البتة، أو بأن يكون في كله شيء البتة فيكون قولنا كل بج» كاذبا بالجزء، وكل «ج أ» كاذبًا بالكل والجزء معًا أو بالجزء وحده. وأما إذا كانت إحديهما صادقة فقط فلا يمكن إلا أن تكون الكبرى. ومثال هذا أن يعرض «أ» محمولا وله موضوعان «ج» و «ب» لكنه يكون موجبًا على «ج» ومسلوبًا عن (ب) بلا انقطاع ; و (ب) و (ج) لا يحمل أحدهما على الآخر. فإن قيل كل بج) وهو باطل، وكل (ج) أ) وهو حق ينتج باطلا وهو كل ب أ». وسواء كان هذا السلب والإيجاب بانقطاع أو بغير انقطاع فإن هذه المادة لا ينتج إلا باطلا، فهذا هو الذي لا يكون إلا في الشكل الأول
If there is a thing upon which 'B' is not predicated and upon which 'A' is not predicated, and 'Every BA' and 'Every C A' produce falsehood; this is possible, and it is not necessary that there be a middle term. Likewise, if 'C' is predicated of some of what is from 'B', not all of it, and 'B' is impossible to be in everything at all, or for something to be in all of it at all, then our statement 'Every B C' is false in part, and 'Every C A' is false in whole and in part together or in part alone. As for when only one of them is true, it is inevitable that it be the major. An example of this is that 'A' occurs as a predicate having two subjects 'C' and 'B', but it is affirmative of 'C' and negative of (B) without interruption; and (B) and (C) are not predicated of each other. For if one says 'Every B C' which is false, and 'Every C A' which is true, it produces a false conclusion, namely 'Every B A'. Whether this negation and affirmation are with interruption or without interruption, this material produces only falsehood. So this is that which occurs only in the first figure.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage analyzes the conditions under which a first-figure syllogism produces a false conclusion. The author begins by considering cases where both premises are false in various ways (partial or total falsity), then focuses on the case where only one premise is false. He asserts that when only one premise is true, it must be the major premise; otherwise the combination cannot yield a false conclusion. He illustrates with an example using variables: suppose 'A' is affirmed of 'C' but denied of 'B', and 'B' and 'C' are not related. If we take the false premise 'Every B is C' (minor) and the true premise 'Every C is A' (major), the conclusion 'Every B is A' is false. The author emphasizes that regardless of whether the negation or affirmation is immediate or mediate, the material composition only yields false conclusions, and this occurs exclusively in the first figure.

The Syllogism

5 min
Reduction to Truth and Compound Ignorance in Second Figure Syllogisms
Analysis of second-figure syllogisms causing compound ignorance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply reduction to truth (radd ila al-sidq) to determine the actual truth value of a conclusion in a second-figure categorical syllogism with false premises.

Core Idea
False premises in second figure guarantee a true conclusion after reduction.

Compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) arises when one holds a false belief with conviction. In syllogistic logic, some arguments with false premises still yield true conclusions. The author explores how reduction to truth—converting false premises to their true counterparts—reveals this in the second figure. Understanding this prevents mistaken assumptions about syllogistic validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and premises
Look for the second figure: middle term is predicate of both premises. In the example from the passage, the premises are 'Every B is C' (false) and 'No A is C' (false). Note that both are entirely false.
2
Apply reduction to truth
Replace each false premise with its true contradictory. For 'Every B is C' (false), the true form is 'No B is C'. For 'No A is C' (false), the true form is 'Every A is C'. This is the process of radd ila al-sidq.
The reduction yields premises that are true.
3
Derive the conclusion from reduced premises
From the reduced premises 'No B is C' and 'Every A is C', the conclusion in the second figure is 'No B is A'. This is a valid syllogism (Camestres mood).
4
Compare with original conclusion
The original conclusion was also 'No B is A'. Therefore, the original conclusion is true, despite both original premises being false. Compound ignorance does not occur here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القياس الموقع للجهل المركب فكل سالب غير ذي وسط فيكون في الشكل الأول عن كاذبتين فإنه إذا كان كل ج ب»، وكل «ب» «أ» بلا وسط; وكان لا شيء من (ب) ج» فقيل كل ب ج ، ولا شيء من «ج أ» ينتج لا شيء من (ب) .أ». ويمكن أن تكون إحداهما صادقة وهي الكبرى مثلا; وليكن «أ» من المسلوبات عن «ج» والموجبات لـ «ب» وهما كما قلنا وهذا ممكن. فيجب أن يكون قولنا كل ب ج كاذبًا وهو الصغرى. فإن قيل كل «بج» وهو كذب، ولا شيء من (ج) أ) وهو صدق ينتج كذبًا. وإن كانت الصادقة هي الصغرى فإنه إذا كان الحق أن كل ب ج ، وكل (ج) أ) فقيل كل ب ج»، ولا شيء من (ج) أ» ينتج لا محالة سالبا يقابل الحق وهذه المادة هي أن يكون «أ» موجبا لـ «ب» و «ج» معا; و (ب) تحت (ج) أو مساو لـ «ج» لكن الجهل المركب لا يكون بمقدمة غير ذات وسط. وأما في الشكل الثاني والمقدمتان كاذبتان بالكل فلا يمكن ذلك; لأنهما إذا رُدَّا إلى الصدق ينتجان ذلك بعينه. فلو قلت أولا «ب كل ب ج ، ولا شيء من أج» وهما كاذبتان بالكلية. فإن رُدَّا إلى الصدق فقيل لا شيء من ب ج ، وكل أج» ينتج ذلك بعينه وهو لا شيء من ب أ). وكذلك إذا كان القياس من الضرب الثاني منه كما يقال لا شيء من بج»، وكل أج» وهما كاذبتان بالكلية. والنتيجة بعد الرد هي التي قبله، فإذن نتيجة هذا القسم صادقة دائما.
As for the syllogism that causes compound ignorance, every negative non-mediated syllogism is in the first figure from two false premises. For if 'Every C is B' and 'Every B is A' without a middle, and 'No B is C' was the case, so one says 'Every B is C' and 'No C is A', it produces 'No B is A'. And it is possible that one of them is true, namely the major; let 'A' be among things denied of 'C' and affirmed of 'B', as we said, and this is possible. So it is necessary that our statement 'Every B is C' is false, and it is the minor. For if one says 'Every B is C' which is false, and 'No C is A' which is true, it produces a false conclusion. And if the true one is the minor, then if the truth is that 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A', so one says 'Every B is C' and 'No C is A', it inevitably produces a negative opposite to the truth. This material is that 'A' is affirmative of 'B' and 'C' together, and 'B' is under 'C' or equal to 'C'. But compound ignorance does not occur from a non-mediated premise. As for the second figure, if both premises are false in totality, that is not possible; because when they are reduced to truth, they produce the very same thing. So if you first said 'Every B is C' and 'No A is C' and both are false in entirety, then when reduced to truth one says 'No B is C' and 'Every A is C', it produces the same, namely 'No B is A'. Likewise if the syllogism is from the second mood of it, as one says 'No B is C' and 'Every A is C' and both are false in entirety. The conclusion after reduction is the same as before, so the conclusion of this section is always true.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage discusses compound ignorance (jahl murakkab) in syllogisms. The author first considers the first figure with a non-mediated middle term (immediate predicate relations). He states that in such cases, both premises must be false to produce a false conclusion, though it is possible for the major to be true, with the minor false, still yielding falsehood. However, he clarifies that compound ignorance does not arise from a non-mediated premise. Then he turns to the second figure: when both premises are entirely false, their reduction to truth yields the same conclusion, meaning the original conclusion is always true. This is a key insight: in the second figure, false premises lead to a true conclusion after reduction, so the original syllogism's conclusion is actually true, not ignorance.

Key Terms
الجهل المركب (al-jahl al-murakkab): compound ignorance
غير ذي وسط (ghayr dhi wasat): non-mediated
رد إلى الصدق (radd ila al-sidq): reduction to truth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The conclusion is uncertain
D) The syllogism becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
In second-figure syllogisms with both premises false, reduction to truth confirms that the original conclusion is true, preventing compound ignorance.

8 min
Compound Ignorance with Mediated Middle Term
Author analyzes compound ignorance with a mediated middle, distinguishing appropriate and inappropriate middle terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a middle term in a mediated categorical syllogism is appropriate and determine which premise must be false for compound ignorance to occur in the first figure.

Core Idea
The middle term must truly connect the terms; otherwise both premises can be false.

We now extend the analysis of compound ignorance to syllogisms with a mediated middle term. The author distinguishes between two cases: when the middle term is appropriate (munasib) and when it is disparate (mubayin). Understanding this distinction is crucial for diagnosing why a syllogism yields falsehood and for evaluating theological proofs that rely on middle terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term
In the given syllogistic argument, locate the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. This is the middle term.
In the first-figure example, the middle is 'C'.
2
Check if the middle is appropriate
A middle is appropriate if it truly connects the two terms, such that the premises yield a valid syllogism (e.g., Barbara). If the terms are under a common genus but disjoint (like man and horse under animal), the middle is not appropriate.
The author's example: 'man' and 'horse' are disparate under 'animal'.
3
For appropriate middle in first figure, locate the false premise
If the syllogism produces the opposite of truth (compound ignorance), only the major premise can be false. The minor cannot be false, and both cannot be false together, else the combination would not be productive.
The major is the premise containing the major term (predicate of conclusion).
4
For inappropriate middle, consider possibilities
When the middle is not appropriate, both premises can be false and yield a false conclusion. Or one premise false and the other true can yield a false conclusion. The syllogism does not produce a valid conclusion.
The author gives an example where both premises are false producing falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القياس الموقع للجهل المركب بقضية ذات وسط فنقول إذا كان الأوسط مناسبًا كان القياس من الضرب الأول في الشكل الأول مثلا كل ب ج»، وكل «ج أ ينتج الحق. ولما غلط فيه حتى ينتج المقابل للحق فلا يمكن أن يكذب في المقدمتين جميعًا; ولا في الصغرى كذلك وإلا لا يكون التأليف منتجا بل إنما يمكن أن يرد إلى الكذب ما يمكن أن يكون سالبا في الشكل وهو الكبرى لا محالة في هذا الشكل. وأما إذا لم يكن الأوسط مناسبًا فيمكن أن يكون (( أ ) محمولاً على كل «ب»; و «ج» موضوع لـ «أ» مثل «ب» إلا أنه مباين مثل الإنسان والفرس تحت الحيوان: فإن قيل كل بج»، ولا شيء من «ج أ) وهما كاذبتان ينتج الكذب والحق لا شيء من «بج»، وكل (ج) أ»; وإنه لا ينتج أصلا، فليس الأوسط مناسبًا. ويمكن أن تكون إحديهما صادقة فقط كما إذا قلت كل ج ب وهو كاذب، ولا شيء من (ج أ) وهو صادق ينتج كاذبًا. وأما في الشكل الثاني مناسبًا كان الأوسط أو لا مناسبًا فالمركب من الكاذبتين في الكل ينتج صادقة، ولا يمتنع أن تكون إحديهما كاذبة فقط. وكذلك إذا كان الكذب بالجزء، فاعتبر بما عرفت في الضروب الباقية من الأشكال الباقية، وبالله التوفيق.
As for the syllogism that causes compound ignorance from a mediated premise, we say: if the middle term is appropriate, the syllogism is from the first mood of the first figure, e.g., 'Every B is C' and 'Every C is A' produce truth. But when one errs in it so that it produces the opposite of truth, it is not possible for both premises to be false; nor for the minor specifically, otherwise the combination would not be productive. Rather, it is possible for the falsehood to be in what can be negative in the figure, which is inevitably the major in this figure. However, if the middle term is not appropriate, it is possible that 'A' is predicated of all 'B', and 'C' is a subject for 'A' like 'B', but it is disparate, like man and horse under animal. For if one says 'Every B is C' and 'No C is A', both false, it produces falsehood; and the truth is 'No B is C' and 'Every C is A'; but it does not produce anything at all, so the middle is not appropriate. And it is possible that only one of them is true, as when you say 'Every C is B' which is false, and 'No C is A' which is true, it produces a false conclusion. As for the second figure, whether the middle is appropriate or not, the composite from two false premises in totality produces a true conclusion, and it is not impossible that only one of them is false. Likewise if the falsehood is in part, then consider based on what you know of the remaining moods of the remaining figures, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion on compound ignorance by considering a mediated middle term (i.e., where the middle term is not immediate). The author distinguishes two cases: (1) the middle term is appropriate (munasib) — here the syllogism normally produces truth; if it produces falsehood due to error, the false premise must be the major, not the minor or both. (2) The middle term is not appropriate (ghayr munasib) — e.g., when the terms are disparate, like 'man' and 'horse' both under 'animal' but not overlapping. In this case, both premises can be false, yielding a false conclusion; or one can be false. Then the author notes that in the second figure, regardless of the appropriateness of the middle, two false premises always yield a true conclusion, and it is also possible for only one to be false. He concludes by directing the reader to extend this analysis to other moods and figures.

Key Terms
الأوسط مناسب
مباين
الضرب الأول

Checkpoint
In the first figure with an appropriate mediated middle, which premise must be false for compound ignorance to occur?
A) The minor premise
B) Both premises
C) The major premise
D) Either premise

Key Takeaway
Compound ignorance with a mediated middle term arises from a false major premise when the middle is appropriate, or from false premises when the middle is inappropriate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following argument: 'Every contingent being has a cause; the world is contingent; therefore the world has a cause.' Identify the middle term and determine if it is appropriate. Could this syllogism produce compound ignorance? If so, which premise would be false?
?
Construct a first-figure categorical syllogism with an inappropriate middle (e.g., using disparate terms like 'human' and 'horse' under 'animal') and show that both premises can be false while the conclusion is false. Explain why this does not yield a valid argument.

The Syllogism
advanced

False Premises in Categorical Syllogisms
Author examines effects of false premises on syllogistic conclusions across figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given categorical syllogism with false premises yields a true or false conclusion based on its figure, mood, and appropriateness of the middle term.

Core Idea
False premises can produce true conclusions — logical form dictates truth transfer, not premise truth.

This passage explores a counterintuitive aspect of categorical syllogisms: even when both premises are false, the conclusion can be true. The author systematically examines different figures and moods, using the technique of reduction to truth (al-radd). This lesson will guide you through his analysis so you can predict the outcome of false-premise syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Understand reduction to truth
The author uses 'reduction' (al-radd): replace false premises with their true counterparts and see what conclusion follows. This reveals that in some cases the conclusion remains unchanged despite premise falsehood.
This is a thought experiment, not a proof procedure.
2
Examine second figure with completely false premises
Consider the example: original premises 'Every B is J' and 'No A is J' are both false. Reducing to truth gives 'No B is J' and 'Every A is J', which produce the same conclusion 'No B is A'. Therefore the conclusion is always true when both premises are completely false in the second figure.
This holds regardless of middle term appropriateness.
3
Analyze first figure with appropriate middle term
When the middle term is appropriate (e.g., 'Every B is J, every J is A'), if the conclusion is false, it is impossible that both premises are false. Only the major premise can be false; the minor must be true.
The minor premise is affirmative and cannot be false if the middle term is appropriate.
4
Analyze first figure with inappropriate middle term
When the middle term is inappropriate (e.g., B and J are different species under the same genus like man and horse under animal), both premises can be false and yield a false conclusion. However, if one premise is false and the other true, the conclusion can still be false.
The inappropriateness allows both premises to be false without forcing a true conclusion.
5
Generalize to other cases
In the second figure, two completely false premises always give a true conclusion, regardless of middle term. Partial falsehood and other moods follow similar patterns; the author says to consider the remaining moods of the remaining figures analogously.
This is a systematic analysis: figure, mood, and middle term appropriateness are the key factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلا يمكن ذلك؛ لأنهما إذا رُدَّا إلى الصدق ينتجان ذلك بعينه. فلو قلت أولا «ب كل ب ج ، ولا شيء من أج» وهما كاذبتان بالكلية. فإن رُدَّا إلى الصدق فقيل لا شيء من ب ج ، وكل أج» ينتج ذلك بعينه وهو لا شيء من ب أ). «ب ج»، وكذلك إذا كان القياس من الضرب الثاني منه كما يقال لا شيء من بج»، وكل أج» وهما كاذبتان بالكلية. والنتيجة بعد الرد هي التي قبله، فإذن نتيجة هذا القسم صادقة دائما. ولا يمتنع أن يكون الكذب بالجزء؛ والقياس على موجبه غير منقطعة، وأن يكون الكذب في إحديهما لا غير أيضًا. وأما القياس الموقع للجهل المركب بقضية ذات وسط فنقول إذا كان الأوسط مناسبًا كان القياس من الضرب الأول في الشكل الأول مثلا كل ب ج»، وكل «ج أ ينتج الحق. ولما غلط فيه حتى ينتج المقابل للحق فلا يمكن أن يكذب في المقدمتين جميعًا؛ ولا في الصغرى كذلك وإلا لا يكون التأليف منتجا بل إنما يمكن أن يرد إلى الكذب ما يمكن أن يكون سالبا في الشكل وهو الكبرى لا محالة في هذا الشكل. وأما إذا لم يكن الأوسط مناسبًا فيمكن أن يكون (( أ ) محمولاً على كل «ب»؛ و «ج» موضوع لـ «أ» مثل «ب» إلا أنه مباين مثل الإنسان والفرس تحت الحيوان: فإن قيل كل بج»، ولا شيء من «ج أ) وهما كاذبتان ينتج الكذب والحق لا شيء من «بج»، وكل (ج) أ» ؛ وإنه لا ينتج أصلا، فليس الأوسط مناسبًا. ويمكن أن تكون إحديهما صادقة فقط كما إذا قلت كل ج ب وهو كاذب، ولا شيء من (ج أ) وهو صادق ينتج كاذبًا. وأما في الشكل الثاني مناسبًا كان الأوسط أو لا مناسبًا فالمركب من الكاذبتين في الكل ينتج صادقة، ولا يمتنع أن تكون إحديهما كاذبة فقط. وكذلك إذا كان الكذب بالجزء، فاعتبر بما عرفت في الضروب الباقية من الأشكال الباقية، وبالله التوفيق.
Then that is not possible; because if they are reduced to truth, they produce the same (conclusion). So if you first say, 'Every B is J, and no A is J' and both are completely false. Then if they are reduced to truth, it is said, 'No B is J, and every A is J' produces the same, namely 'No B is A'. And likewise if the syllogism is from the second mood, as it is said 'No B is J, and every A is J' and both are completely false. The conclusion after reduction is what it was before; therefore the conclusion of this class is always true. And it is not impossible that the falsehood is in part, and the syllogism upon its affirmative is uninterrupted, and that the falsehood is in one of them only as well. As for the syllogism that produces compound ignorance concerning a proposition with a middle term, we say: if the middle term is appropriate, the syllogism is from the first mood of the first figure, e.g., 'Every B is J, and every J is A' produces truth. And when one errs in it such that it produces the opposite of truth, it is not possible that both premises are false, nor in the minor likewise, otherwise the composition would not be productive; rather it is possible to refer to falsehood only what can be negative in the figure, which is the major necessarily in this figure. And if the middle term is not appropriate, it is possible that (A) is predicated of every B, and J is subject for A like B but it is different, like man and horse under animal: for if one says 'Every B is J, and no J is A' and both are false, it produces falsehood, and truth is 'No B is J, and every J is A' and it produces nothing at all; so the middle term is not appropriate. And it is possible that one of them is true only, as when you say 'Every J is B' which is false, and 'No J is A' which is true, it produces falsehood. As for the second figure, whether the middle term is appropriate or not, the composite from both false in total produces truth, and it is not impossible that one of them is false only. And likewise when the falsehood is partial, so consider by what you know of the remaining moods of the remaining figures. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of what happens when premises in categorical syllogisms are false. The author is explaining that false premises do not necessarily lead to false conclusions; the result depends on the figure, mood, and appropriateness of the middle term. He begins with a case from the second figure where both premises are completely false, yet reduction to truth yields a true conclusion. He then moves to the first figure, distinguishing between appropriate and inappropriate middle terms. When the middle term is appropriate (e.g., 'Every B is J, every J is A'), if the conclusion is false, only the major premise can be false; both cannot be false. When the middle term is inappropriate (e.g., man and horse under animal), both premises can be false and the conclusion false, but a different combination yields no conclusion. He also notes that one premise can be false and the other true. Finally, he states that in the second figure, regardless of middle term appropriateness, two false premises always yield a true conclusion. The overall lesson is that the truth value of premises does not guarantee the truth value of the conclusion; the logical form matters. The author uses the technique of reduction (الرد) to truth to analyze the syllogisms.

الأوسط
الضرب
الشكل

Key Takeaway
The truth of premises does not guarantee the truth of the conclusion; logical form and figure determine what follows from false premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every khamr (wine) is haram. This drink is not khamr. Therefore this drink is not haram.' The premises are: minor 'This drink is not khamr' may be true, but major is true. Is this syllogism valid? Does it follow the patterns discussed? Actually, it's invalid because it's denying the antecedent. But using false premises: suppose both premises are false (e.g., 'Every khamr is halal' and 'This drink is khamr' but it's not), what conclusion follows? Apply the first-figure rules: if middle term appropriate? (Here middle term is 'khamr'?) Formulate a first-figure syllogism with false premises and determine if the conclusion must be true or false based on the passage.
?
Construct a second-figure syllogism with both premises completely false concerning an Islamic ruling (e.g., about riba or halal meat). Use the reduction technique to show that the conclusion is always true regardless of premise falsehood. Explain which figure and mood you are using.

The Syllogism

The Regress to First Principles in Syllogisms
Author explains that every syllogism ends in middle-less principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why every syllogism must ultimately rest on indemonstrable first principles (mabadi').

Core Idea
Every syllogism traces its premises back to foundational truths needing no middle term.

In logic, every argument must start somewhere. The author explains that any syllogism composed of multiple premises cannot go on infinitely; it must end in principles that are accepted without proof. This lesson unpacks that crucial foundational idea and connects it to how Islamic sciences anchor their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about syllogisms
Read the first sentence: the author asserts that every syllogism must end in principles (mabadi') that have no middle term, either absolutely or within that science. This is a foundational claim about the structure of all reasoning.
The phrase 'لا وسط لها' means 'having no middle term'.
2
Understand the concept of 'middle term' (wasat)
Recall that a syllogism has three terms: two premises share a common term (the middle) that disappears in the conclusion. The author says that at some point, we reach premises that do not require a middle term to connect them—they are self-evident or given.
Middle term is the one that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion.
3
Trace the regress to first principles
Imagine you have a syllogism proving a conclusion. The premises might themselves need proof via another syllogism. The author warns this cannot go on forever; there must be premises that are accepted without further proof. Those are the mabadi'.
This is the logical foundation to avoid infinite regress.
4
Distinguish absolute vs. discipline-specific middle-less premises
The author says premises can be middle-less 'absolutely' (i.e., always true) or 'in that science' (accepted only within a particular field). For example, in geometry, the axioms are middle-less within geometry, even if they could be questioned in philosophy.
Demonstrative syllogisms require absolute middle-less premises.
5
Connect to Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, jurists rely on first principles like 'a command implies obligation' (al-amr li al-wujub). These are not proven within fiqh itself but are foundational for deriving rulings. Recognizing this structure helps evaluate arguments.
Without first principles, legal reasoning would be circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان كل قياس مؤلفًا من حدود ثلاثة كما مر أن هذا ثابت لذلك، وذلك لغيره ؛ ولا بد من أن ينتهي إلى مباد وأصول موضوعة موجبة أو سالبة لا وسط لها على الإطلاق أو في ذلك العلم. والمبرهن مأخذ المقدّمات الأولى على أن لا وسط لها بأحد هذين الوجهين وتنحل أجزاؤه إلى ما لا وسط له مطلقا؛ وإنه له القياس إما على الظن وهو الخطابي، وإما على الرأي المشهور وهو الجدلي فليس يجب أن ينتهي تحليل قياسهما إلى مقدمات غير ذات وسط في الحقيقة بل إذا انتهت إلى المشهورات أو المقبولات مثل أن العدل جميل، والظلم قبيح ؛ وإنه مأخوذ في الجدل عل أن لا وسط له وفي العلوم يطلب (۱) لذلك وسط . ثمَّ هذه وما هي لها هذه الأوساط متناهية؛ وإنها إما بالذات وإما بالعرض على ما عرف .
Since every syllogism is composed of three terms, as it has passed that this is established for that, and that for something else, it must finally end in principles and posited premises, affirmative or negative, which have no middle term absolutely or in that science. The demonstrator takes the first premises as having no middle in one of these two ways, and his syllogism's parts resolve into what has no middle absolutely. He has the syllogism either based on opinion, which is the rhetorical, or based on common opinion, which is the dialectical; it is not necessary that the analysis of their syllogisms ends in premises that are truly middle-less, but when they end in well-known or accepted premises, such as 'justice is beautiful' and 'injustice is ugly', and it is taken in dialectic as having no middle, while in sciences a middle is sought for it. Then these and what have these middle terms are finite, and they are either essential or accidental, as has been known.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage discusses the foundational structure of syllogisms (qiyas). The author states that every syllogism composed of three terms must ultimately be reduced to first principles (mabadi') that are without a middle term—either absolutely or within that particular science. The demonstrative syllogism (burhani) uses premises that have no middle in either sense, and its analysis resolves into what has no middle absolutely. In contrast, the rhetorical (khatabi) and dialectical (jadali) syllogisms are based on opinion and common opinion respectively; they do not require their premises to be truly middle-less. Their analysis ends at well-known or accepted premises (e.g., 'justice is beautiful') which are taken in dialectic as having no middle, although in the sciences a middle term is sought for them. The author concludes that these middle terms are finite and are either essential (bi al-dhat) or accidental (bi al-'arad), as previously defined.

Key Terms
قياس
مباد
وسط
برهان
خطابي
جدلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the characteristic of a demonstrative (burhani) syllogism's premises?
A) They have a middle term.
B) They have no middle absolutely.
C) They are based on opinion (zann).
D) They are finite.

Key Takeaway
Every syllogism must terminate in axioms that require no further demonstration, either absolutely or within a particular science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take any syllogism from a kalām text, such as 'The world is originated, everything originated has a cause, therefore the world has a cause.' Identify which premise is treated as a first principle in that science and explain why it is taken as middle-less.
