The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Three Types of Qiyas: Burhani, Khatabi, and Jadali
Author distinguishes demonstrative, rhetorical, and dialectical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the demonstrative, rhetorical, and dialectical syllogisms based on the epistemic status of their premises.

Core Idea
Certainty comes from true premises; persuasion from accepted opinion.

Not all syllogisms aim for absolute certainty. The author categorizes syllogisms into three types depending on the nature of their premises. This lesson will help you classify any syllogism you encounter in Islamic discourse—whether it is a rigorous demonstration, a rhetorical appeal, or a dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types mentioned
Scan the Arabic for the terms: برهان (burhān), خطابي (khaṭābī), and جدلي (jadalī). These are the three types of qiyas discussed.
The author explicitly names them in the passage.
2
Understand the premise base of each type
Burhān uses premises that are certain (yaqīniyyāt), obtained from first principles. Khaṭābī uses premises based on opinion (ẓann). Jadalī uses premises based on common opinion (ra'y mashhūr), such as 'justice is beautiful'.
Only burhān requires truly middle-less premises.
3
Note the epistemic difference for middle-less premises
For burhān, the final premises are absolutely middle-less. For khaṭābī and jadalī, the premises are treated as if they have no middle, but in reality they could be questioned (e.g., in sciences, a middle is sought for them).
The dialectician takes 'justice is beautiful' as a starting point, but a scientist might ask for a proof.
4
Consider examples from Islamic sciences
In kalām, a dialectical syllogism might start from 'the world is contingent' which is widely accepted but not necessarily certain. A demonstrative syllogism would start from necessary premises like 'every contingent has a cause'.
Examples help solidify the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان كل قياس مؤلفًا من حدود ثلاثة كما مر أن هذا ثابت لذلك، وذلك لغيره ؛ ولا بد من أن ينتهي إلى مباد وأصول موضوعة موجبة أو سالبة لا وسط لها على الإطلاق أو في ذلك العلم. والمبرهن مأخذ المقدّمات الأولى على أن لا وسط لها بأحد هذين الوجهين وتنحل أجزاؤه إلى ما لا وسط له مطلقا؛ وإنه له القياس إما على الظن وهو الخطابي، وإما على الرأي المشهور وهو الجدلي فليس يجب أن ينتهي تحليل قياسهما إلى مقدمات غير ذات وسط في الحقيقة بل إذا انتهت إلى المشهورات أو المقبولات مثل أن العدل جميل، والظلم قبيح ؛ وإنه مأخوذ في الجدل عل أن لا وسط له وفي العلوم يطلب (۱) لذلك وسط . ثمَّ هذه وما هي لها هذه الأوساط متناهية؛ وإنها إما بالذات وإما بالعرض على ما عرف .
Since every syllogism is composed of three terms, as it has passed that this is established for that, and that for something else, it must finally end in principles and posited premises, affirmative or negative, which have no middle term absolutely or in that science. The demonstrator takes the first premises as having no middle in one of these two ways, and his syllogism's parts resolve into what has no middle absolutely. He has the syllogism either based on opinion, which is the rhetorical, or based on common opinion, which is the dialectical; it is not necessary that the analysis of their syllogisms ends in premises that are truly middle-less, but when they end in well-known or accepted premises, such as 'justice is beautiful' and 'injustice is ugly', and it is taken in dialectic as having no middle, while in sciences a middle is sought for it. Then these and what have these middle terms are finite, and they are either essential or accidental, as has been known.

Commentary
This passage discusses the foundational structure of syllogisms (qiyas). The author states that every syllogism composed of three terms must ultimately be reduced to first principles (mabadi') that are without a middle term—either absolutely or within that particular science. The demonstrative syllogism (burhani) uses premises that have no middle in either sense, and its analysis resolves into what has no middle absolutely. In contrast, the rhetorical (khatabi) and dialectical (jadali) syllogisms are based on opinion and common opinion respectively; they do not require their premises to be truly middle-less. Their analysis ends at well-known or accepted premises (e.g., 'justice is beautiful') which are taken in dialectic as having no middle, although in the sciences a middle term is sought for them. The author concludes that these middle terms are finite and are either essential (bi al-dhat) or accidental (bi al-'arad), as previously defined.

Key Terms
قياس
مباد
وسط
برهان
خطابي
جدلي

Key Takeaway
Only demonstrative syllogisms require premises that are absolutely indemonstrable; rhetorical and dialectical syllogisms rely on opinion or common acceptance.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Finitude of Intermediate Terms in Predication
Author proves finitude of intermediate terms in predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the chain of intermediate terms between subject and predicate must be finite in categorical propositions, and identify the key distinction between actual and potential infinity.

Core Idea
The chain of middle terms in predication cannot be infinite; mathematical potential infinity does not apply to actual logical relations.

In this passage, the author establishes a crucial principle for syllogistic reasoning: the chain of middle terms connecting subject and predicate in any categorical proposition must be finite. This is not a mathematical claim but a logical one, as the author distinguishes between actual and potential infinity. Understanding this principle is essential for ensuring the soundness of demonstrations and arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extremes
In an affirmative proposition like 'Every B is A', the extremes are the subject (B) and the predicate (A). The author argues that any chain of intermediates (wasait) linking B to A must be finite.
The extremes are 'haddi al-ijab'.
2
Understand the chain of intermediates
Intermediates are terms that can be inserted between B and A to form a series of predications, e.g., 'Every B is C, and every C is A.' The chain can be ascending (from B upward) or descending (from A downward).
3
Examine the reductio argument
Assume the chain is infinite. Then the second extreme would never be reached through actual predications, which is impossible. Therefore the chain must be finite.
The author uses an implicit proof by contradiction.
4
Address the objection from magnitudes
One might object that magnitudes have infinitely many divisions between two points. The author responds that these divisions are only potential, not actual. Logical predication deals with actual terms, so the objection fails.
Key distinction: potential (bil-quwwa) vs. actual (bil-fi'l).
5
Extend to negative propositions
For a negative proposition like 'No A is B', if there were an intermediate, then the minor premise would still be affirmative and must be finite, leading to the same conclusion. Thus all syllogisms require a finite chain and at least one affirmative premise.
Applies to all four figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما تقرر هذا فنقول إن الوسائط بين حدي الإيجاب متناهية وإلا لم يبلغ الطرف الثاني البتة سواء كان من جهة (ب) في قولنا كل ب أ) صاعدين على الولاء، أو من جهة «أ» نازلين على الولاء. وسواء كان بعضها على الولاء بلا وسط أو الكل متنازلة ولا واسطة بينهما؛ وكانت غير متناهية وكيف كان فالكلام في الكل واحد ثمَّ من المحال أن يكون حد ولا يبلغ البتة ونهاية، ولا يتناهى إليها كذلك ولا ينتقض هذا بالمقادير : فإن بين طرفي كل مقدار حدود بالقوة بلا نهاية. وذلك لأن المقادير المتصلة لا قسم لها ما لم يقسم البتة واللانهاية التي هي بين الحدين قسما آخر بالقوة لا ما هو موجود بالفعل : فتبين إذن أنه لا يمكن أن يكون بين هذين الطرفين وسائط بلا نهاية. وكذلك الأمر في السلب إذا قلنا لا شيء من ( أ ) ؛ وكان بينهما واسطة؛ إذ ليس يمكن أن يكون دائما واسطة بعد واسطة في المقدمتين جميعًا الكبرى السالبة والصغرى الموجبة: إما الموجبة فلما مر ، وإما السالبة فلأن بيانها من الأشكال الأربعة فيجب على كل حال إن كان الوسائط التي للكبريات السالبة غير متناهية أن تكون الموجبات بغير نهاية؛ وذلك محال لما مر . وعلى هذا في الشكل الثاني وغيره فإنه لا بد من أن يكون في كل قياس مقدمة موجبة.
And when this is established, we say that the intermediates between the two extremes of affirmation are finite, otherwise the second extreme would never be reached, whether from the side of 'B' in our saying 'Every B is A' ascending successively, or from the side of 'A' descending successively. And whether some of them are successively without a middle or all are descending with no intermediate between them; and they were infinite, and however it is, the discussion in all is one. Then it is impossible that there be a term and it never reaches a limit and end, and does not terminate to it likewise. And this is not refuted by magnitudes: for between the two ends of every magnitude there are potentially infinite divisions. That is because continuous magnitudes have no part unless it is divided at all, and the infinity that is between the two ends is another division potentially, not what exists actually. So it is clear then that it is not possible that there be infinite intermediates between these two extremes. And similarly in negation if we say 'No A is B' and there were an intermediate between them, for it is not possible that there always be intermediate after intermediate in both premises, the major negative and the minor affirmative: as for the affirmative, it is according to what passed, and as for the negative, because its demonstration is from the four figures. Therefore it is necessary in any case that if the intermediates for the major premises are infinite, then the affirmatives would be infinite; and that is impossible according to what passed. And upon this in the second figure and others, it is necessary that in every syllogism there be an affirmative premise.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage proves that the chain of middle terms between subject and predicate in a categorical proposition must be finite. The author argues that if there were infinite intermediates, the second extreme would never be reached, which is impossible. He addresses an objection from mathematics: magnitudes have infinitely many potential divisions, but these are only potential, not actual. So actual predication cannot involve infinite steps. The argument extends to negative propositions: if 'No A is B' and there is a middle term, the chain cannot be infinite because that would require infinite affirmative premises (since the minor premise must be affirmative), which is already proven impossible. Therefore, in any syllogism (especially in the second figure), an affirmative premise is always required. This establishes a foundational principle for the structure of demonstrations.

Key Terms
وسائط
حدي الإيجاب

Key Takeaway
The chain of intermediate terms in both affirmative and negative categorical propositions must be finite; otherwise, the extremes would never be related, and mathematical potential infinity does not threaten this logical principle.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Position of the Middle Term in the Four Figures
Author explains the position of the middle term in each syllogistic figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the position of the middle term in each syllogistic figure and determine which figures yield valid categorical conclusions.

Core Idea
The middle term shifts from between extremes to outside them to generate valid arguments.

The logical power of a syllogism depends on where the middle term sits. In this passage, the author maps the middle term's position across all four figures, showing how it moves from between the extremes to outside on different sides. Understanding this map is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of middle term
The middle term appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. Its placement relative to major and minor terms determines the figure.
2
Universal affirmative: middle term between extremes
In a universal affirmative premise, the middle term must lie between the subject and predicate (first figure).
3
Trace positions in all four figures
First: middle between extremes. Second: goes outside on minor side. Third: goes outside on major side. Fourth: outside both sides.
The author emphasizes that in the third figure the demonstration is potential, not actual.
4
Apply to negative conclusions
To correct or produce a negative syllogism, the middle term must not go outside on the side of the minor in the second figure, and not on the side of the negative in the third.
This shows how the figures constrain valid deductions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا انقسمت المقدّمة بالحد الأوسط وكانت موجبة كلية فلا يمكن أن يقع الحد الأوسط خارجا عن الطرفين بل يكون متوسطا بينهما لا محالة، وأما في السالب فقد يقع خارجًا، وقد يقع غير خارج. وأما البيان بالشكل الثالث فذلك لا بالفعل بل بالقوة. وكل حد يقع في الشكل الأول فإنه يقع بين الحدين لكنه في الشكل الثاني يخرج لا من جانب الأصغر، وبالجملة من جانب الكلي. وفي الشكل الثالث يخرج لا من جانب الأكبر، وفي الرابع يخرج من جانبي الأكبر والأصغر. وكذلك إذا أردت أن تصحح السالبة من قياس مقدمة من الأول، أو أردت أن تركب قياسا لإنتاج السالبة في الشكل الثاني فإنه لم يخرج من جانب الأصغر، وفي الثالث لم يخرج من جانب السالب. ويجب أن تعلم أن جملته تنحصر في أنه إذا كان حدان امتنع السلوك من أحدهما إلى الآخر وإن كانت وسائط بلا نهاية. وإن كانت محمولات بالحقيقة غير متناهية لم يكن حد ولا برهان. ولا يلزم شيء من هذا من يُنكر البرهان والحد إلا أن يبين عليه من وجه آخر أن هذا حدا وبرهانا؛ وليس ذلك على المنطقي بل هو موضوع له. وإن المعتمد ما ذكرناه من أمر التحليل لأن التحليل يوجب أن لا تكون المحمولات في الشيء متناهية. ومن جميع هذا لا يبين أن التزايد في البراهين لا يذهب إلى غير النهاية بل التحليل فقط. وأما أن التزايد كيف يذهب إلى غير النهاية فستعرف من بعد.
And when the premise is divided by the middle term and is universal affirmative, it is impossible for the middle term to fall outside the two extremes, but it must be intermediate between them without exception. As for the negative, it may fall outside and may not fall outside. As for the proof in the third figure, that is not actual but potential. Every term that falls in the first figure falls between the two terms, but in the second figure it goes out from the side of the minor, and in summary from the side of the universal. In the third figure it goes out from the side of the major, and in the fourth it goes out from both sides of the major and minor. Likewise, if you want to correct the negative from a syllogism having a premise from the first figure, or if you want to construct a syllogism to produce a negative in the second figure, then it does not go out from the side of the minor, and in the third it does not go out from the side of the negative. You must know that its entirety is confined to the fact that if there are two terms, passage from one to the other is impossible even if there are infinite middle terms. And if there were actually infinite predicates, there would be no term and no demonstration. None of this follows from one who denies demonstration and definition, unless it is shown from another aspect that this is a definition and demonstration; that is not the responsibility of the logician but is a postulate for him. The relied upon is what we have mentioned regarding the matter of analysis, because analysis necessitates that predicates in a thing are not infinite. From all of this it is not proven that the increase in demonstrations does not go to infinity, but only analysis. As for how the increase goes to infinity, you will know later.
Source pages p. 464

Commentary
This passage delves into the technicalities of syllogistic figures and the impossibility of infinite predication. The author first states that in a universal affirmative premise, the middle term must lie between the subject and predicate (as in the first figure). However, in a negative premise, the middle term may be outside or inside. The demonstration in the third figure is only potential, not actual. The author then systematically describes the position of the middle term in each of the four figures: in the first figure it lies between the extremes; in the second it goes outside on the side of the minor term (the subject of the conclusion), i.e., it is not between the extremes but has the major term under it; in the third figure it goes outside on the side of the major term (predicate of conclusion); in the fourth figure it goes outside both sides. This is crucial for understanding which figures can produce valid conclusions. Next, the author explains how to correct a negative syllogism: when you want to produce a negative conclusion, in the first figure the middle term does not go out from the side of the minor, in the third it does not go out from the side of the negative (i.e., the major? The text says 'from the side of the negative' which likely means the predicate of the negative premise). Then the author makes a profound epistemological point: if two extremes are given, one cannot pass from one to the other through an infinite number of middle terms—there must be a limit. If predicates were infinite, there would be no definite subject and no demonstration. This argument does not affect someone who denies demonstration and definition altogether; such a person would need separate proof. It is not the logician's job to prove the existence of demonstration and definition; they are taken as given. The method of analysis (tahlil) shows that predicates in a thing are finite. However, the author clarifies that this does not prove that demonstrations cannot increase infinitely; only analysis demonstrates finitude. The question of how the increase tends to infinity is deferred to later discussion.

Key Terms
الحد الأوسط
الشكل
السالبة
